*Introduction*

*His most sweet Utterance, blessed and exalted be He:*

He had made mention of the Twin Shining Lights. Blessed be He! From him We heard mention of those for whom My Most Exalted Pen hath lamented, and unto whose exaltation and sublimity the Tongue of Oneness hath testified in the Tabernacle of Grandeur. O Muhammad! Well is it with whosoever is occupied with their commemoration, and who leaveth a record of their sufferings, whether in poetry or in prose, upon the pages of time. Hasan attained unto a twofold martyrdom, for throughout the days of his life he was selfless in the Will of the One True God. Whosoever hath assimilated his will and purpose to those of his Lord and Master hath, verily, attained unto true martyrdom. He attained likewise unto an outward martyrdom. Blessed be he and his brother, and he who was called Kázim.

*Here end the Words of God, glorified and exalted be He*

Isfahán, which was accounted the centre of Persian 'Iráq, held ever from ancient times -- that is, from before this blessed Revelation and the appearance of the holy religion of Islám -- in relation to the other inhabited parts of the world the rank of the eye; while in view of the plenteousness of its cultivation and the abundance and excellence of its conditions, it was famed as "Nisf-i-Jihán" ("Half the World"). The Arabian and Persian poets have each in turn uttered verses and composed rhymed epigrams extolling its merit; while the learned of every age and epoch have left in works of history and geography a complete and detailed account of the city, its situation, and the marvellous happenings and weighty events that have taken place in it at different times. One poet has said:

In calling Isfahán but half the world * they left the half of Isfahán untold!

The late Muhammad-Hasan Khán, Saní'u'd-Dawliy-i-Marághi'í, son of the famed Hájibu'd-Dawliy-i-Marághi'í, has in his well-known work *Mir'átu'l-Buldán* ("Mirror of the Lands") -- in which, as he says, he reconciled and made peace between history and geography -- written a full and circumstantial description of Isfahán, its virtues, affairs, historical happenings, celebrated rulers, artists and craftsmen, to which work and the like references the reader must turn for further information. Till now, more than twenty substantial compositions have been written and published about Isfahán, and from all these the importance of the city becomes clearly evident. After the appearance of this Mighty Divine Cause, the city received its share of the light of guidance. In the first instance, his honour the Bábu'l-Báb (Gate of the Gate) arrived in Isfahán on His way to Khurásán at the behest of the blessed Báb, and, tarrying there a while, guided a number of receptive souls to recognize the Truth of the Cause. Important people embraced the Cause during these times. Thereafter, this great city won, in consequence of its being honoured by the presence of His Holiness the Primal Point -- glorified be His Most Exalted Name -- renewed glory and respect; a company from each class and station turned towards the Cause of God, and consecrated souls laid down their lives in the path of His Holiness the Word of God. Although the martyrs of this land are many, and have, each in a distinctive fashion, proven the measure of their faithfulness through sacrificing their lives in the Divine Court, yet are the martyrdom and sacrifice of the Twin Shining Lights, their honours Áqá Mírzá Hasan, the King of Martyrs, and Áqá Mírzá Husayn, the Beloved of Martyrs, a different tale. The Pen of His Holiness the Ancient Beauty -- glorified be His mention and praise -- has, in a Blessed Tablet revealed in honour of the late Áqá Mírzá Áqáy-i-Afnán, Núri'd-Dín -- upon whom be the Glory of God -- provided the following explanation concerning the martyrdom of these twin holy souls.

*His most exalted and sweetest Utterance:*

...Observe thou that the Prince of the World was martyred under the circumstances that have reached thy blessed ears, while these are accounted among His servants. Yet observably the effect of their martyrdom is greater, its influence more complete, and its pain more searing. Exalted be He Who raised them up. Exalted be He Who honoured them. Exalted be He Who caused them to win this station, in striving after which God's chosen ones have laid down their lives ... *Finis*.

These two brothers, who were the sons of his honour Áqá Mírzá Ibráhím, had the honour of entering the blessed presence of the Báb on the evening when their father invited Him to his home. His honour the Beloved of Martyrs was at that time eleven years old, while his honour the King of Martyrs was nine years old. In the course of that reception they were privileged to wait upon the Blessed Báb. Thereafter, having travelled to Baghdád in the company of their esteemed uncle, Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí, they had the further honour of entering the blessed presence of the Ancient Beauty, and attained unto the degrees of certitude, belief and assurance. The unseen favours of the True One encompassed these two holy souls, and God so willed it that in addition to faith, which is the real and genuine wealth, they should enjoy outward and material wealth and affluence as well. For a while they lived in Isfahán in glory and honour, and were known and famous under all conditions for their association with the Blessed Cause; nor did the blame of the blamers deter them from following in the way of God. They bore themselves towards all men, whether or not believers, with the utmost solicitude and kindness, becoming to each sick one a caring physician, and to each poor one a shelter and a refuge. In the famine of 1288, they saved a great number from certain death and starvation, and every tongue was eloquent in their praise and commendation. Throughout all the days of their life they never for a single moment concealed their faith, and accounted their association with the illustrious Divine Lote Tree a tremendous honour. So their days passed until, when the hour appointed for the Supreme Sacrifice arrived, they laid down their lives with perfect steadfastness and detachment. In the words of Bahá'u'lláh, wealth did not prevent them from turning towards the Holy Court of the True One. For long after the event, in every Tablet that was revealed, the Pen of God and the Tongue of Grandeur of the Ancient Beauty extolled them. In truth, it would be beyond the power of this feeble servant to record all the Tablets that were revealed by the Pen of God, both before and after their martyrdom, in honour of these two holy souls. Perchance their bereaved survivors and relatives may gradually amass those blessed Writings, and, compiling them in a special volume, undertake to print and publish it. Despite the acute eye affection which is his constant troublesome companion, this servant, placing his whole trust in Him Who is the Ever-Living, the All-Powerful, and beseeching confirmation from the heaven of Divine grace, has nevertheless to the extent possible assembled a collection of the Divine Tablets, and made use of the requisite available documents and sources so that at length, through the assistance and favour of God, this notebook has taken shape, and became available for the use of the friends of God. Of the Blessed Tablets revealed from the heaven of the Will of God, only such as were needful were set down in this notebook: for were I to include all the Blessed Tablets that have streamed forth from the pens of the Ancient Beauty, of His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá, and of his Holiness the Guardian of the Cause of God concerning the Twin Shining Lights and their survivors and relatives, this booklet, which is intended to be concise, would swell into a substantial volume. At the end of this treatise I have appended various historical materials, in both verse and prose, that have survived from some of the learned of the Cause of God, so that these precious writings, which are the fruit of the thoughts, and the effusions of the pens, of the learned in Bahá' and of those knowledgeable in the Blessed Cause may remain safe from the peril of obliteration and disappearance; and that they may form the musk-scented conclusion of this notebook. This notebook includes an account of the ancestors of the Twin Shining Lights, and a relation of the martyrdom of these two noble souls, in both of which all due brevity has been observed. In the compilation of this booklet, recourse has been had to the blessed Divine Tablets; to the work *Memorials of the Faithful* by His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá; to the short history composed by her Holiness Munírih Khánum, the respected Consort of the Centre of the Covenant and Testament of God; to the treatise by his honour Abu'l-Fadá'il-i-Gulpáygání; to the researches and brief history of the late Áqá Mírzá Asadu'lláh Fádil-i-Mázindarání, and to the second and third volumes of his work *Zuhúru'l-Haqq* ("The Appearance of the Truth"); to the Mathnaví of the late Áqá Mírzá 'Alí-Ashraf, 'Andalíb-i-Láhíjání; to the reminiscences of the early believers in the Cause, who had the honour of coming into their presence; and to other historical sources outside the Cause. In conclusion, I beseech the friends of God to remember, and to bring happiness and joy to, this lowly servant through their kind prayers on his behalf.

Isfahán -- the Day of Kamál, the Day of 'Alá' of the Month of Núr 115,

Monday the second of Tír-Máh 1337 in the Persian Muslim Solar calendar

'Abdu'l-Hamíd Ishráq-Khávarí

Section One

The Forbears and Paternal Uncles

Of the Twin Shining Lights

*Hájí Siyyid Muhammad-i-Hindí*

His honour Hájí Siyyid Muhammad was originally a native of Zavárih, in the region of Isfahán. Having passed his childhood in that town, he decided, after attaining the years of puberty and discretion, to emigrate to India, and to make that country his home. This intention was duly fulfilled, and he eventually rested from his travels in one of the cities of that vast land. By chance, one of its wealthy inhabitants, who was a follower of the Shí'ah school, and held the descendants of His Holiness, the Best of Mortals -- blessings and salutations be upon Him -- in the highest esteem, observing upon the brow of that young Siyyid the signs of both outward and inner perfection, chose to make him his son-in-law; and as a result of this happy event Siyyid Muhammad came into the possession of great riches and a considerable fortune. He made haste to undertake the pilgrimage to the House of God in Mecca the Blessed, and, having returned from visiting those holy precincts, dwelt in that same city of the Indian Sub-continent, living at the height of glory, honour, wealth and respect, until the angel of death arrived; he answered the call of the True One; and, agreeably with the noble saying, "Every soul shall taste of death," he winged his flight from this mortal dust-heap unto the rose garden of the spiritual worlds. As mentioned at the outset of these remarks, Siyyid Muhammad dwelt originally in the area around Isfahán; but in view of his long sojourn in the Indian sub-continent, he became known as Hájí Siyyid Muhammad-i-*Hindí*. He left behind him an illustrious son by the name of Hájí Siyyid Mihdí.

*Hájí Siyyid Mihdíy-i-Nahrí*

After the death of Hájí Siyyid Muhammad-i-Hindí, his distinguished son, his honour Hájí Siyyid Mihdí known as "Nahrí," took his father's place, and continued for a while in India, enjoying the fullest measure of respect and fame. Then, after a certain period, he gathered up his limitless wealth, and, setting out for the Exalted Thresholds, fixed his residence in the city of Najaf, where he expended his riches in founding caravanserais, houses, baths and trading quarters, amassing in the process numerous properties, estates and lands. Among the charitable works he successfully undertook was to construct for the benefit of the people of those parts a water channel ("*nahr*"), by which means he removed a problem they had been facing. Thereafter he became known and celebrated as Hájí Siyyid Mihdíy-i-*Nahrí*. In the course of his sojourn in 'Iráq news reached him of the teaching and activities of his honour Shaykh Ahmad-i-Ahsá'í -- may he be the object of God's mercy and good pleasure -- and he joined the ranks of the followers of that celebrated man of learning. After a period he conceived a longing for his native land, and so, having provided the means, and made all necessary preparations, for his journey he set out from Najaf for Isfahán, and fixed his abode in that fragrant city. The story of his marriage has a preamble, which we set forth in what follows:

At that time, one of the well-known divines of Isfahán, who was accounted a leader of the people and an authority to whom all, high and low alike, might turn, was the late Hájí Siyyid Muhammad-Báqir, Hujjatu'l-Islám. His father was Siyyid Muhammad-Taqíy-i-Músavíy-i-Shaftíy-i-Rashtí (Shaft is a village near Rasht in Gílán), whence the said Hujjatu'l-Islám was called "Siyyid-i-Shaftí." He wielded unbounded influence and enormous power, and had completed his various studies under such leading 'ulamá as Siyyid-i-Bahru'l-'Ulúm; the author of the *Ríyád* and the *Kashfu'l-Ghitá'*; Mullá Mihdíy-i-Naráqí; Mírzáy-i-Qumí, and others. The author of the *Rayhánatu'l-Adab* avers in his account of him, "To describe his magnificence is beyond the power of speech or writing to accomplish." He is the author of numerous compositions, which would fill near twenty volumes. He used himself to administer the penalties prescribed by Muslim law, and is said to have executed by his own hand a number of people pursuant to its provisions, and to have brought about the deaths of others by pronouncing them worthy of this punishment under the religious law. Some estimate the number of those whose deaths he caused to be as many as 120. Hujjatu'l-Islám Shaftí is the same who, as Nabíl-i-Zarandí records in his Narrative, took violent issue from the outset with the disciples of Shaykh Ahmad-i-Ahsá'í and Siyyid-i-Rashtí -- may God have mercy upon them -- nay, stubbornly opposed them, considering both the Shaykh and the Siyyid, and the followers of those two saintly figures, as unbelievers and seceders from the holy religion of Islám. The late Siyyid Kázim sent his honour Mullá Husayn-i-Bushrú'í (who was afterwards named the "Bábu'l-Báb") to Isfahán in order to engage with Siyyid-i-Shaftí in debate. After a number of meetings with the Hujjatu'l-Islám, Mullá Husayn succeeded in guiding him to a true perception of the matter, and proving to him that the teachings of the Shaykh and the Siyyid in no wise conflicted with Islám. Thereafter, Siyyid-i-Shaftí made up for his previous opposition, observing the utmost respect towards the Shaykh and the Siyyid. He even wrote in his own hand a letter to this effect, and sent it through the offices of Mullá Husayn-i-Bushrú'í to his honour Siyyid Kázim-i-Rashtí. Finally, in the year 1260 A.H., ere the Báb had declared His Mission, he died of dropsy aged eighty-five, and was buried in a mosque he himself had constructed, and which is known consequently as the "Mosque of the Siyyid," in the Bíd-Ábád district of Isfahán. At all events, the point of this introduction is that the wife of Hujjatu'l-Islám Shaftí was a disciple of the Shaykh and the Siyyid -- may God have mercy upon them -- and that when his honour Hájí Siyyid Mihdíy-i-Nahrí arrived in Isfahán, and made there his home, he took to wife a lady from the kinsfolk of the wife of the Hujjatu'l-Islám, from which union there sprang a number of scions of his illustrious stock; to whit: his honour Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí; his honour Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí; and his honour Áqá Mírzá Ibráhím Áqá. I now provide an account of each of these in turn.

*Photograph of Áqá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Isfahání (Nahrí), father of the blessed Consort of His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá*

*Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí*

This illustrious personage was born in Isfahán, where, after reaching maturity, he devoted himself to the pursuit of science and knowledge. He spent his days and nights at the Madrisih of Kásih-Garán, and exerted a mighty effort to acquire the recognized sciences of the age. So he passed his days for a while until, that he might perfect his attainments, he set off for Arabian 'Iráq, and took up residence in Karbilá the Exalted. Aided by a watchful fortune and by a favourable conjunction of circumstances and fates, he found his way into the circle of Hájí Siyyid Kázim-i-Rashtí -- may he be the object of God's mercy and good pleasure -- and became occupied in imbibing from the ocean of the learning of that heavenly doctor. In that same blessed city he chose for himself a bride; and during the period when, ere the declaration of His Mission, His Holiness the Primal Point -- glorified be His most exalted mention -- came to Karbilá, and tarried there a while, Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí was privileged on several occasions to meet Him in the sacred precincts of the shrine of His Holiness the Prince of Martyrs -- peace be upon Him -- and became enthralled and enraptured by that Pure Being. His distinguished brother Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí, as will be related hereafter, shared likewise in this bounty; and when the fame of the Blessed Cause was noised abroad from Shíráz, Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí, leaving his wife in Karbilá, set out with his brother Áqá Mírzá Hádí for that city. There he heard that the Báb had gone on pilgrimage to the Holy Kaaba. During these days, Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí and his brother chanced to meet Mullá Husayn-i-Bushrú'í, and, having attained through him to a perfect understanding of the Manifestation of His Holiness the Lord of Lords, and obtained a sufficient measure of belief and certitude, set out in his company for Isfahán, whence they made for Shíráz, that image of Paradise, and were honoured to meet the Promised One of God. The companions of the Blessed Báb were at that time converging upon Shíráz from all quarters in daily increasing numbers, and these, at His bidding, occupied themselves during the daytime in various corners of the city with some work or trade; while in the evening, depending upon the circumstances, they were honoured to come, with His permission, into His Most Luminous Presence. Of this number were Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí; his brother Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí; Mullá 'Abdu'l-Karím-i-Qazvíní; and others. After countless happenings, the companions were eventually dismissed by the Báb, each to a different quarter. Among them, Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí, as will be recounted hereafter, returned to Karbilá, while Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí went back to Isfahán and remained there. After a while, news arrived of the passing of his wife in Karbilá, and in consequence he forsook entirely his intention of journeying to that city, and resumed his residence in the Madrisih of Kásih-Garán in Isfahán. Some while later, a merchant of Isfahán by the name of Hájí Áqá Muhammad gave Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí his daughter to wife; yet she too, like her predecessor, bore him no offspring. So matters persisted until His Holiness the Exalted One -- glorified be His mention -- arrived from Shíráz in Isfahán, and on an evening when, at the invitation of Áqá Mírzá Ibráhím Áqá, the brother of Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí, the Blessed Báb was a guest in the former's home, His host besought Him to grant that God might bestow upon Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí a child. This favour the Báb granted, with the result that God bestowed upon him a distinguished daughter, whom he named Fátimih -- the same who was subsequently given the title of "Munírih Khánum," and had the honour of serving the Most Mighty Branch of God as helpmeet. God bestowed upon Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí other children besides, accounts of whom will in due course be provided. Not to prolong the tale, when, some considerable period after this event, His Holiness the Báb issued to the believers the command "Hasten to the land of Khá'!," Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí, upon hearing this blessed call, set out with his brother Áqá Mírzá Hádí from Isfahán for Mashhad in Khurásán, eventually participating in the Conference of Badasht, while both brothers bore fully too the consequences of the upheaval of Níyálá: Mírzá Hádí met his death under circumstances to be related in our account of him; while Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí proceeded alone to Tihrán, from whence he made his way to Isfahán, there ending his travels. Their honours Áqá Mírzá Hasan, the King of Martyrs, and Áqá Mírzá Husayn, the Beloved of Martyrs, the sons of Áqá Mírzá Ibráhím, attained through the instrumentality of their esteemed uncle, Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí, to the Most Great Guidance, and, under circumstances that -- God willing -- we shall set forth, seized from their peers the palm in the arena faithfulness and self-abnegation, rewriting the scroll of the sufferings of the martyrs in the path of God in bygone days. Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí was survived by one son named Siyyid Yahyá, and by three daughters named Fátimih Khánum, Rádíyih Begum and Gawhar Begum. The most famous of his children is the aforesaid Fátimih Khánum entitled "Munírih Khánum," an account of whom I shall now set forth in order to enhance the benefit of this narration.

*Fátimih Khánum Entitled "Munírih," The Blessed Consort of His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá, Exalted Be His Sovereignty*

As was mentioned above, the father of her Holiness Munírih Khánum was the illustrious Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahríy-i-Isfahání. This honoured lady had been adorned with the ornament of existence, and stepped forth out of the unseen into the visible realm, thanks solely to the will and favour of His Holiness the Exalted One. A little earlier it was recorded that His Holiness the Exalted One honoured Áqá Mírzá Ibráhím, the son of Hájí Siyyid Mihdíy-i-Nahrí, in response to his invitation, with a visit one evening to his home. The latter, out of respect for his revered Guest, had bidden to the occasion other company as well, among whom were Mír Siyyid Muhammad, the Imám-Jum'ih, the Sultánu'l-'Ulamá; his brother Mír Muhammad-Husayn; Áqá Siyyid Muhammad-Ridáy-i-Pá-Qal'i'í; Hájí Muhammad-i-Tájir, the father-in-law of Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí; Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí, and his brother Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí, who were brothers of the host; Mullá Muhammad-Taqíy-i-Harátí, and others. While dinner was being served, the Báb gave with His own hand a portion of the food to Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí, tacitly conveying to him the joyful tidings that a distinguished child would be born to him. Subsequently God in His mercy bestowed upon him a happy-fated daughter, whom he named Fátimih. (The manner of her becoming known by the title "Munírih Khánum" will be related hereafter). From the account she wrote of herself, it may be inferred that she was born in the year 1264 A.H., at a time when the Báb's companions were sustaining the depredations and assault of the enemy in Níyálá. Ere he set out from Isfahán for Khurásán, Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alí had addressed to his respected consort these words: "We are journeying in a path that is the Path of God, and the end of our enterprise is unknown. If, therefore, after the birth of thy child, it be a girl, thou shalt bestow upon her the name of Fátimih, and if it be a boy, do thou name him 'Alí." In fine, according to Munírih Khánum's account, the intention of her illustrious father had been to send his daughter to the Most Holy Court, so that she might occupy herself with rendering services to the dwellers of the Tabernacle of Inviolable and Spotless Sanctity; but before this aim could be achieved, Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí passed away, leaving Fátimih Khánum to grow and develop by degrees until she became a ripe and lovely sapling.

Not long after this, a Tablet arrived from the Most Holy Court in honour of the King of Martyrs and his brother the Beloved of Martyrs, which contained a statement to this effect: "Such is the Divine grace vouchsafed unto you that He hath appointed you members of the Holy Family and relations of the One True God." The meaning and fulfilment of this happy announcement became evident when Shaykh Salmán, the "Courier of the All-Merciful," arrived in Isfahán from the Holy Land, and informed the King of Martyrs and the Beloved of Martyrs that the Ancient Beauty had commanded him to arrange for their niece, the daughter of Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí, to join the next pilgrimage caravan, and, having done so, to escort her to the Holy Land; for God had willed it that she should be betrothed to the Most Mighty Branch of God. Fátimih Khánum and Shaykh Salmán accordingly set out on their journey, travelling by way of Shíráz and Búshihr. In Shíráz, Fátimih Khánum met the respected Consort of His Holiness the Exalted One, the Khayru'n-Nisá' (Most Excellent of Women), Khadíjih Khánum; and, after she had enjoyed the bounty of association with that sore-tried and saintly Leaf, they pressed on their journey. Travelling by way of the Hijáz, they arrived first in Alexandria, and then in 'Akká. Pursuant to Bahá'u'lláh's command, she was entertained in the home of Mírzá Músáy-i-Kalím, where she remained a guest for five months. It was during this period that Fátimih Khánum received from the Tongue of Bahá'u'lláh the title "Munírih Khánum." Hájí Mírzá Haydar-'Alíy-i-Isfahání has also mentioned this utterance of the Blessed Beauty concerning Fátimih Khánum, which was recorded earlier, and made clear that after it had circulated among the friends, Fátimih Khánum became known as Munírih Khánum. At this point I shall interrupt the story of Munírih Khánum, and, in order to complete the sequence of historical events, set down a few words regarding Shahr-Bánú, to whom His Holiness the Most Mighty Branch was first betrothed, and the reason why the betrothal agreement did not come into effect.

Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-Hasan, the brother of the Ancient Beauty -- exalted be His glory -- had a daughter named Shahr-Bánú. During the period ere His exile to Baghdád when the Ancient Beauty and the rest of His kindred were dwelling in Tihrán, it was decided that Shahr-Bánú, the daughter of Mírzá Muhammad-Hasan, should be betrothed to His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá, and a promise and undertaking were given to this effect. In those days they were both still small, for in Írán it was customary for noble and genteel families to betroth their sons and daughters to one another from infancy. In due course the matter of the exile and banishment of the Holy Family from Tihrán to Baghdád and elsewhere supervened, and Shahr-Bánú stayed behind in Tihrán with her father and other family members. The elder sister of the Ancient Beauty on his father's side, who entertained the utmost hatred and enmity for the Ancient Beauty and the Holy Family, took the step in Tihrán of giving away Shahr-Bánú in wedlock to Mírzá 'Alí-Khán, the son of the Atábak, having first deceitfully won her trust by bringing her a quantity of gold, jewels, finest diamonds and the like treasures. Shahr-Bánú, who inwardly had no inclination for this unsuitable match, and had been induced to accept it under coercion, was so filled with grief and sorrow that she became consumptive a short while afterwards, and bade farewell to life.

His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá -- exalted be His praise -- provides in His Tablet to Shaykh Qumí the following account of Shahr-Bánú, her taking ill of consumption, and her subsequent death.

*His most sweet utterance:*

... One of my female cousins on my father's side was, at the wish of Bahá'u'lláh, betrothed from a tender age to this servant. After we arrived in Baghdád, my honoured paternal uncle hastened unto the Holy Court, tearfully imploring that this connection be confirmed and ratified. This servant was not at first disposed to consent; but, in the end I bowed to the positive command of the Blessed Beauty, whilst ever wishing to remain single. So the matter stood until we arrived in Adrianople; and it was while we were here that Khánum Buzurg, my paternal aunt, who had a connection with Yahyá, merely that she might please him made it the object of her endeavour to give that poor maid away to Mírzá 'Alí Khán, the son of the Grand Vizir, which she successfully brought about. As was later related, she left her home in tears, fearful and trembling; nor did very many days pass ere she was afflicted with consumption, and died of that malady. May the mercy of God rest upon her ... *Finis*.

By the "honoured paternal uncle" mentioned in this Holy Tablet is intended Mírzá Muhammad-Hasan, the brother of the Blessed Beauty, to whom, in this connection, allusion is made by the Pen of the Most High towards the end of the Epistle to the Son of the Wolf. According to Hájí Mírzá Haydar-'Alíy-i-Isfahání, the name of that maid was Shahr-Bánú. Let us now turn to relating the sequel of the account of her Holiness Munírih Khánum, the respected Consort of 'Abdu'l-Bahá -- upon her be the Glory of God. After she had tarried five months in the house of his honour Áqáy-i-Kalím, it was eventually decided that their wedding would be held in the House of 'Abbúd. Her Holiness the Greatest Holy Leaf, Bahá'íyyih Khánum, prepared for the bride a white robe, wherewith she arrayed her form. The Holy Consort of the Blessed Beauty, the Mother of His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá, led Munírih Khánum to the Blessed Beauty -- exalted be His glory -- and she was honoured to come into His presence. Thus did Bahá'u'lláh address the bride: "O My Leaf! O My maidservant! We have chosen and accepted thee for the service of My Most Mighty Branch. This is a token of My Grace, wherewith the treasures of heaven and earth can never be compared ... how many the maidservants -- whether in Baghdád, Adrianople or this Most Great Prison -- who cherished the hope of winning this favour, yet was it not granted them! It behoveth thee to render thanks that thou hast attained unto this most great bounty and favour..." That night a celebratory gathering was held, at which special Tablets were read out. Nabíl-i-Zarandí has many verses on the subject of this fortune-favoured union -- among them the following ode, which he both composed and recited on that blessed night:

> Hail lovers! Joy! The world is like unto a rose garden ... tonight!
>
> With cypress and lily the feast of lovers is bedecked ... tonight!
>
> All things are singing songs of love; all atoms are astir with life:
>
> Thou too O heart vibrate with love, for joyous is the dance ... tonight!
>
> The time for fragrant breaths is come; the time for garden bower to sigh:
>
> For here of hyacinth and rose a myriad blooms are reaped ... tonight!
>
> Behold, the wedding feast hath come! The time to greet the friends is come!
>
> The eyes of the company of Bahá are bright with joy ... tonight!
>
> With lightsome hearts we celebrate the nuptial of God's Mighty Branch:
>
> With waving hands and beating feet we mark our happiness ... tonight!
>
> Behold the cups of gladness drained! behold the nodding flowers kiss!
>
> For by this joy-enhancing tale, the world's attired anew ... tonight!
>
> That same delight that long in Bahá's treasury was hid and stored,
>
> Is now revealed! God's Mystery the Trustee of that store ... tonight!
>
> Not just on earth they foot the dance, in heaven too they wheel about!
>
> For the lovesick nightingale is arrayed in rose's garb ... tonight!
>
> The world's aflood with happiness, 'tis time for joy and liberty,
>
> For the refulgent Eye of Day a veiléd Moon awaits .. tonight!
>
> Nimbly trip the maids of heaven; nor are our mortal lights outdone!
>
> For lo! To earthly frame the soul of joy is to be joined ... tonight!
>
> The spirits all are sanctified, and holy all the mortal forms!
>
> Through window panes the lookers peep to see our gathering ... tonight!
>
> Come minstrel, sound a goodly lay! Strike up on viol, on harp and lyre!
>
> For here alone are gathered friends; from strangers safe are we ... tonight!
>
> Come server, with thy holy wine dispel the lovers' languor quite!
>
> For under Bahá's auspices our joyous feast is held ... tonight!
>
> A blessed Night of Power whereon Twin Shining Orbs of heaven's sphere,
>
> Find in the sign of unity and fellowship their home ... tonight!
>
> The Lordly Branch's season of felicity hath come: O joy!
>
> O happiness! The night of mine own ecstasy is e'en ... tonight!
>
> Ho ancient friend, this boon I pray: my palate sweeten by thy grace:
>
> For crimson, from the flame of joy, like iron glows my heart ... tonight!
>
> Unfurl the panoply of grace, thy tender providence vouchsafe,
>
> The time for mercy, kindness too, *par excellence* is come... tonight!
>
> Spouse of so glorious a mate, most blesséd be thine happiness!
>
> From saying all I wish to say, my tongue is impotent ... tonight!
>
> Yet graciously accept my lay, though it be all in disarray:
>
> E'en gladness for the Godly Branch hath stole my wits away ... tonight!
>
> *** * ***

From this union there resulted eight children, of whom four died in infancy: namely:

Husayn (His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá has said of Husayn: "Children are a tremendous source of delight and solace. I had a son, Husayn Effendi. When he was three or four years old, he used softly to steal into my bed while I was asleep. It had an indescribable savour." *Finis*. ('Abdu'l-Bahá's Travelogue). This Husayn Effendi is the same who as an infant pronounced the letter "Mím" as "Bá'," causing him to enunciate the word "*tamáshá*" as "*tabáshá*." After his passing, a Blessed Tablet was revealed in which Bahá'u'lláh represented the word "*tamáshá*," according to the child's pronunciation, as "*tabáshá*."

Mihdí.

Fu'ádíyyih.

Rúh-Angíz.

Four other daughters, having reached the years of discretion and maturity, chose husbands for themselves, and in due course produced children and grandchildren; their names are as follows:

Díyá'íyyih, who married Mírzá Hádí Afnán-i-Shírází, son of Áqá Siyyid Husayn, son of Hájí Siyyid Abu'l-Qásim Afnán, known as Saqqá-Kháni'í, son of Áqá Mírzá 'Alí, son of Mírzá 'Ábid. A lustrous bloom resulted from this union in the sacred person of his Holiness Shoghi Rabbání, Guardian of the Cause of God, the Chosen Branch of God, exalted be his sovereignty (1275-1336 A.H.S.; 1897-1957 A.D.), who, after the passing of His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá, was, according to the explicit pronouncement of His Will and Testament, established upon the seat of the Most Great Guardianship, and of the Interpretation of the Verses of the Most Mighty and All-Glorious Lord; whereat, pursuant to His promise, East and West were illumined by the lights of his peerless beauty; and the Cause of God enveloped the whole earth. In the year 1937 A.D. he was united in wedlock to the daughter of Mr. Maxwell, her honour Rúhíyyih Khánum, who through the intercession of 'Abdu'l-Bahá was born in 1910 A.D., and was the object of many signal favours.

Another daughter of His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá was her honour Rúhá, who became the wife of Mírzá Jalál, the son of his honour the King of Martyrs.

Another of His daughters was Túbá, who became the wife of Mírzá Muhsin.

The last of His daughters was Munavvar, who was joined in wedlock to Mírzá Ahmad-i-Yazdí.

Briefly, Munírih Khánum lingered on for a while in this world after the passing of His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá, all the while seared and consumed with anguish on account of her separation from the beauteous figure of the Centre of the Covenant; until in the year 1313 A.H.S. that virtuous lady ascended in Haifa to the Abhá Kingdom, and at the sacred behest of his Honour the Guardian of the Cause of God, Shoghi Rabbání -- exalted be his sovereignty -- her mortal remains were interred on Mount Carmel, and a dignified monumental edifice was erected over her resting-place.

The biographical account of Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí, his children and grandchildren, here comes to an end. There is extant a letter which her Holiness Táhirih wrote to Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí from Karbilá. Although the copy of this letter available to me is marred by numerous errors, I shall venture in what follows to cite from it a few sentences in order to enhance the benefit of this narration.

Before setting down the utterances of Táhirih, however, I should like to provide a word of introduction. In this letter occurs the following sentence:

"Do thou return unto the Land of Sád, and warn thy brother of the greatness of the Judgment of Him Who is the Lord of all mankind." By this "brother," whom she instructs the recipient to apprise of the "greatness of the judgment," is intended Áqá Mírzá Ibráhím, who was not at first a believer in the Blessed Cause. The reason why he invited His Holiness the Exalted One to his home was that the Báb was then the guest of Mír Siyyid Muhammad, the Sultánu'l-'Ulamá -- that is, the Imám-Jum'ih -- for whom Áqá Mírzá Ibráhím worked as overseer: in order, therefore, that he might honour the Imám's guest, he appointed the Báb a certain night on which he invited Him to visit him in his home. Briefly, Áqá Mírzá Ibráhím, after the death of his first wife, Khánum Ján, married the wife of his deceased brother, Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí, who was named Khurshíd Begum, but given the title "Shamsu'd-Duhá;" while he also joined his late brother's daughter in wedlock to his own distinguished son, Áqá Mírzá Hasan, the King of Martyrs. Although, before his marriage to Khurshíd Begum, Áqá Mírzá Ibráhím had been to a degree acquainted with the Cause of God, yet was he not included in the circle of the believers. After his marriage to Shamsu'd-Duhá, however, he was moved through association with that believing leaf, convinced as she was of the truth of the Blessed Cause, to embrace it, and thereafter to become settled in his own belief and conviction. Numerous Blessed Tablets were revealed in his honour by the Pen of the Ancient Beauty, transcriptions of some of which are included in the sixth section of this book.

I now proceed to set forth a portion of the letter of her Holiness Táhirih.

*Her utterance, upon her be the Glory of God:*

... O my kind brother, and my true master! My condition is not hid from thee, for thou hast been illumined with the light of faith, and lookest upon the beauteous countenance of the Author of the Bayán. My master! Do thou pray unto thy Lord, and make entreaty unto Him: haply He may bring about thereafter some change whereby we may be enabled to join our Master -- may the spirit of all that dwell in the Kingdom of Names and Attributes be a sacrifice unto His beauteous countenance. Alas, alas! And again alas, alas! And so again, for as long as it may please my Lord! O the grief and the pity of it, that I have failed in my duty towards God! By my father and my mother do I adjure thee, my master, O thou who conjoinest the Twin Names (Muhammad-'Alí) and revealest the Twin Symbols! Do thou return unto the Land of Sád, and warn thy brother of the greatness of the Judgment of Him Who is the Lord of all mankind. Announce unto them the Judgment of God; and make known unto them the Cause of God. Then whoso wisheth, let him believe; and whoso wisheth, let him disbelieve. By the might of my Lord! This warning is naught but a quintessential bounty: for were my Lord to send down fire, and burn us on the instant, it would be but our just desert, inasmuch as we have changed the goodly nature He implanted in us; have charged with falsehood Him Who is the Proof of God; and have differed amongst ourselves, even after He made us a single people and a single faith. Gracious God! Do thou warn them, O my lord, and admonish them, O my master. Yea, by the Lord! God, the True One, is possessed of grace abounding. The decree, verily, hath been fulfilled, and all are commanded to set out, and to delay not, though it be for a single moment. Admonish them, O my lord, to look unto the Book of God, which neither vanity can reach, nor idleness approach. If any be wrongfully slain, we have granted his guardian authority over the assailant; yet let him not slay to excess: he, verily, shall be victoriously assisted. And remove the veil from their eyes... etc.

*Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí*

This blessed personage also was the son of Hájj Siyyid Mihdíy-i-Nahrí, as well as the esteemed brother of Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí. He was born in Isfahán, where he was known for being God-fearing, religiously observant, and reclusive. The divines of the city as a whole did not stint him any form of respect. He enjoyed the confidence of all the spiritual leaders. In particular, the late Siyyid Muhammad-Báqir-i-Shaftí, Hujjatu'l-Islám, who was mentioned in the preceding, evinced towards Áqá Mírzá Hádí the utmost love and kindness -- entertained indeed for him such love and affection that he gave him to wife his cousin on his father's side, Khurshíd Begum, a girl who was educated, sagacious, pious, and accomplished in all respects. Subsequent to this event, Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí tarried for some while in Isfahán, and thereafter removed with his family from Isfahán to the Sacred Thresholds, choosing Karbilá as his home. Whilst there he used to accompany his brother, Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí, who in those days was also resident in Karbilá, into the presence of Hájí Siyyid Kázim-i-Rashtí, and benefit from the illimitable knowledge and learning of that illustrious figure. These two brothers and their families became known in Karbilá for their devotion to the Shaykh and the Siyyid, and were counted among the leading lights of the Shaykhí school. As was mentioned in the account of Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí, on several occasions during this period the two brothers met in the sacred precincts of the shrine of His Holiness the Prince of Martyrs -- peace be upon Him -- His Holiness the Primal Point -- glorified be His mention -- Who was at that epoch hidden behind seventy thousand veils of light, and conceived for Him in their hearts the strongest affection. Many a time Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí remarked to his brother Mírzá Muhammad-'Alí, "Mine heart attesteth that this young Siyyid is the Proof of the Era and the Lord of the Age!" More than anything, it was the wrapt and undivided attention, the peerless and unexampled humility and lowliness so conspicuously evident in the demeanour of His Holiness the Exalted One during His visits to the illumined resting-place of the King of Martyrs -- peace be upon Him; also the extraordinary reverence Siyyid-i-Rashtí observed towards Him, that attracted the gaze of those two discerning brothers towards the Point of Truth. When in due course the Divine Call was raised from Shíráz, Áqá Mírzá Hádí observed to his brother, "Methinks the author of this claim is that same Shírází youth whom we saw aforetime in Karbilá." The two brothers accordingly set off for Shíráz in order to investigate the matter. They arrived after the Báb had already departed on His pilgrimage to the Holy Kaaba; but as fate had it, they chanced to meet there Mullá Husayn-i-Bushrú'í, and, through the guidance of that wellspring of faith and purity, attained to recognition of the Divine Point. Thereafter they, together with a number of the companions including Mullá Husayn, journeyed to Isfahán, and thence to Shíráz, where, as previously mentioned, they benefited for a period, according as circumstances permitted, from His holy presence, before leaving Shíráz at His bidding following the grave disturbances in the city. Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí proceeded, as previously related, to Isfahán, while Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí for his part returned to Karbilá, whence he removed after some time to Isfahán. Subsequent to the Báb's arrival from Shíráz in Isfahán, he enjoyed on numerous occasions the bounty of meeting the Beloved of the World. Later, when His Holiness the Exalted One -- glorified be His most exalted mention -- was incarcerated in the prison of Máh-Kú, Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí, his yearning to gaze upon the beauteous countenance of the Manifestation of God overcoming all patience and restraint, set out for Máh-Kú in order to attain His blessed presence; then, having accomplished his desire, he set off anew for Isfahán. Khurshíd Begum, the wife of Áqá Mírzá Hádí, had tarried all this while in Karbilá, where she received an ample portion from the ocean of the knowledge and wisdom of Siyyid-i-Rashtí, and garnered for herself inestimable benefits. After the death of Siyyid-i-Rashtí, which occurred on the Day of 'Arafih (9 Dhi'l-Hijjih) of the year 1259 in Karbilá, Khurshíd Begum sought seclusion in the house of her Holiness Táhirih, Qurratu'l-'Ayn, who was at that time resident in Karbilá, and, profiting from the abundant grace that had fallen to her lot, quaffed a sufficient draft from the camphor cup of mystic truths. Among the friends, she became known by the title "Shamsu'd-Duhá;" and, through association with her Holiness Táhirih, arrived at the highest level of knowledge and certitude, drawing forth the winning arrow. It chanced one day that, at the instigation of hostile elements, certain vile and abject persons launched an assault upon the house of her Holiness Táhirih, and mistakenly seizing Shamsu'd-Duhá instead of Táhirih, inflicted upon her grievous injury and harm, even stoning her. In brief, Shamsu'd-Duhá accompanied her Holiness Táhirih wherever she went, and continued to do so up to the time of her arrival in Qazvín. Here her husband Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí, who chanced to arrive in the city at the same time on his way back from Máh-Kú to Isfahán, learning of their arrival there, bore her with him to Isfahán. We shall return again hereafter to the story of Shamsu'd-Duhá. To resume, Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí and his brother, after hearing the blessed command of His Holiness the Exalted One "Hasten to the Land of Khá'!," set out, as previously related, for Mashhad, participating *en route* in the Conference of Badasht. At the close of proceedings, they departed with a group of the companions for Mázindarán. As fate had it, they were subjected, upon arriving in Níyálá, to a vicious onslaught by a band of men from the village of Hizár-Jaríb in Mázindarán, who inflicted grievous injury and harm upon a number of the companions, including Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí. After sustaining unnumbered blows, the two Nahrí brothers sought refuge in a ruined building off the beaten track, where Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí yielded up his spirit. The explanation of this event and the manner of its occurrence are more precisely as follows. After the conclusion of the Conference of Badasht, the Báb's disciples set out for Mázindarán. The Blessed Beauty -- exalted be His glory -- gave order for a howdah, and when this was procured, Quddús took a seat on one side and Táhirih on the other. Each day Táhirih composed beautiful odes and stirring, soul-uplifting verses, which the companions walking beside the howdah recited with extraordinary zest and enthusiasm. They traversed the way in a singular state of happiness and exhilaration, dancing, carefree, joyous and enthusiastic, until they reached the village of Níyálá. Here, after spending the night at the foot of a mountain, they were of a sudden fallen upon at daybreak by a group of men from Hizár-Jaríb. The cause of this unexpected assault was partly the intense hatred and enmity, fostered by the doctors and divines, which these men harboured for the companions; partly the preposterous calumnies and outlandish accusations they had heard the people uttering against them, leading them to imagine them worthy of death and destruction; and partly also the strange fear and dread of the Bábís that had taken root in their hearts, inducing them, by way of forestalling an anticipated evil, to launch upon the companions a surprise attack, so that they might at once be rid of the fear of them, and, through massacring them and plundering their wealth, win for themselves a great reward. More particularly, the manifestations of fervour, enthusiasm, joy and jubilation evinced by the companions on the eve of their arrival at the foot of the mountain had kindled in the hearts of those ignorant men a fierce animosity; while the incitement of the 'ulamá and instigation of the religious leaders had further disposed the churlish band to deem them worthy of assault. In fine, these various reasons and causes all combined, with the result that the companions found themselves the objects of attack. Armed with sticks and stones, and other tools and instruments of war and battery, the men of Hizár-Jaríb dealt the companions countless blows, and inflicted upon them incalculable injury and harm. A number of them were wounded, and much of their property was seized as plunder. Since the companions had previously felt in all respects at ease about the place, never expecting so violent an assault and incursion, they were caught completely off their guard, and, powerless to offer resistance, were scattered abroad, each one making off in a different direction. His holiness Quddús headed for Mázindarán. In the midst of all the commotion, while everyone was fleeing to a different quarter, Táhirih remained alone and unprotected. Wishing to defend her, one of the companions, a youth named Mírzá 'Abdu'lláh-i-Shírází, stepped into the arena of sacrifice, unsheathed his sword, and was about to set upon the assailants, when the Blessed Beauty -- exalted be His glory -- bade him refrain from assault and slaughter, and, Himself taking a stance before the attackers, induced them, through effective admonitions and counsels, and unnumbered exhortations and advices, to stay their offensive. "Who is your chief?" He asked them; to which they replied, "Daryá-Bigí is our chief." "I am acquainted with him," Bahá'u'lláh replied, "We have known each other for some time." A number of them then went off to inform Daryá-Bigí, who no sooner heard the name Bahá'u'lláh than he came into His presence, and made profession of his servitude and sincere attachment. The Blessed Beauty requested him to hold back the men from their incursion and assault, and to recover from the assailants, and make restitution of, the property they had looted and plundered. Forthwith executing Bahá'u'lláh's command, Daryá-Bigí recovered from the men all the looted property, and restored it to its owners. The Blessed Beauty commanded that her Holiness Táhirih set out for the district of Núr, travelling by way of Bárfurúsh (which is in these days known as Bábul). Appointing a house and dwelling-place for her abode, Bahá'u'lláh directed Mullá Abú-Turáb-i-Ishtihárdí, who was also present, to convey her to Núr, and there to lodge her in the appointed premises. To resume the account: among the companions who were grievously afflicted in that fearful encounter, receiving numerous wounds and injuries from the fell band of assailants, was Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí. His distinguished brother Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí relates the event as follows: "When this group of rabble and herd of assailants descended upon the faithful companions in Níyálá, bearing off everyone's property and effects as booty and plunder, each one of the companions made off in a separate direction. My brother, Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí, and I too struck out with several other people along a particular route. My brother, Áqá Mírzá Hádí, who had suffered grievous hurt, and received many wounds, was overcome by faintness along the way, and in the extremity of his weakness lost all power of endurance. By sheer force of necessity, we managed by some means to reach a derelict and dilapidated caravanserai, where we took shelter. My brother Áqá Mírzá Hádí passed away the same night in that ruined caravanserai, finding deliverance from the hardships of this world. A number of the companions who had been accompanying me, fearing the assault of the enemy, took flight in the middle of the night, and I was left alone, revolving what to do, and to whom to turn for help in burying my brother. At daybreak I removed my brother's body from the caravanserai, and laid it beside the highway, hoping that someone might arrive who could assist me in this office. I fastened my eyes upon the road, and after an hour a cloud of dust arose in the distance, announcing the approach of a traveller. I waited to see who it might be, at length espying, as the figure drew nearer, that it was a woman. When she arrived she asked, 'Who art thou, and what dost thou here?' I replied, 'I am a stranger in these parts. Last night my brother passed away in this caravanserai, and I am looking for someone to assist me in burying his corse.' 'Be comforted,' the woman replied, 'I shall even now myself assist thee in this task: in sooth, 'twas for this purpose that I came hither.' 'How couldst thou have known what hath here chanced?' I asked; to which she answered, 'Last night that embodiment of saintly purity, Her Holiness Fátimih the Radiant -- may God's peace rest upon Her -- appeared to me in a dream, and addressed me saying: "One of my children hath passed away, forlorn and far from home, in this vicinity: do thou go at once and bury him with fullest dignity and honour." As bidden by that illustrious figure, I immediately set out, and lo! find now the object of my quest; and yield thanks unto God for having granted me this bestowal.' She then set off for the village, and presently returned, bringing with her a company of helpers to perform the rites of shrouding and interment. Having washed my brother in a stream, they wrapped him in a winding-sheet; and since he had repeatedly said, 'Should I die, be sure to bury me on a way frequented by the pilgrims to Karbilá,' we therefore interred him in that same place, which lay on just such a road." This was the essence of what Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí related to his friends and relatives after his return to Isfahán. From Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí and his esteemed consort Khurshíd Begum, styled "Shamsu'd-Duhá," there remained one son and one daughter. Their son, who was named Siyyid 'Alí, married his cousin Rádíyih Begum, the daughter of his paternal uncle Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí, and from this union resulted a son named Mírzá 'Ináyatu'lláh, who still survives; is nowadays resident in Haifa; remains firm in the Cause and unassailable Divine Covenant; and has children of his own. As for the daughter of Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí, who was named Fátimih Begum, she was honoured to be joined in wedlock to Áqá Mírzá Hasan, the King of Martyrs -- upon him be the Glory of God -- and from this union there resulted a number of offspring, to whom reference will be made in the biographical notice and circumstantial account of the King of Martyrs. An account of Fátimih Begum, the daughter of Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí and Shamsu'd-Duhá, and the respected consort of his honour the King of Martyrs, has been bequeathed by the pen of 'Abdu'l-Bahá -- exalted be His praise -- in the work *Memorials of the Faithful*, the which I now take opportunity to set forth.

*His most sweet utterance:*

*The Consort of his Honour the King of Martyrs*

Among the women who came out of their homeland was the sorrowing Fátimih Begum, widow of the King of Martyrs. She was a holy leaf of the Tree of God. From her earliest youth she was beset with uncounted ordeals. First was the disaster which overtook her noble father in the environs of Badasht, when, after terrible suffering, he died in a desert caravanserai, died hard -- helpless and far from home. // The child was left an orphan, and in distress, until, by God's grace, she became the wife of the King of Martyrs. But since he was known everywhere as a Bahá'í, was an impassioned lover of Bahá'u'lláh, a man distracted, carried away, and since Násiri'd-Dín Sháh thirsted for blood -- the hostile lurked in their ambush, and every day they informed against him and slandered him afresh, started a new outcry and set new mischief afoot. For this reason his family was never sure of his safety for a single day, but lived from moment to moment in anguish, foreseeing and dreading the hour of his martyrdom. Here was the family, everywhere known as Bahá'ís; their enemies, stony-hearted tyrants; their government inflexibly, permanently against them; their reigning Sovereign rabid for blood. // It is obvious how life would be for such a household. Every day there was a new incident, more turmoil, another uproar, and they could not draw a breath in peace. Then, he was martyred. The Government proved brutal and savage to such a degree that the human race cried out and trembled. All his possessions were stripped away and plundered, and his family lacked even their daily bread. // Fátimih spent her nights in weeping; till dawn broke, her only companions were tears. Whenever she gazed on her children, she would sigh, wearing away like a candle in devouring grief. But then she would thank God, and she would say: "Praised be the Lord, these agonies, these broken fortunes are on Bahá'u'lláh's account, for His dear sake." She would call to mind the defenceless family of the martyred Husayn, and what calamities they were privileged to bear in the pathway of God. And as she pondered those events, her heart would leap up, and she would cry, "Praise be to God! We too have become companions of the Prophet's Household." // Because the family was in such straits, Bahá'u'lláh directed them to come to the Most Great Prison so that, sheltered in these precincts of abounding grace, they might be compensated for all that had passed. Here for a time she lived, joyful, thankful, and praising God. And although the son of the King of Martyrs, Mírzá Abdu'l-Husayn, died in the prison, still his mother, Fátimih, accepted this, resigned herself to the Will of God, did not so much as sigh or cry out, and did not go into mourning. Not a word did she utter to bespeak her grief. // This handmaid of God was infinitely patient, dignified and reserved, and at all times thankful. But then Bahá'u'lláh left the world, and this was the supreme affliction, the ultimate anguish, and she could endure no more. The shock and alarm were such that like a fish taken from the water she writhed on the ground, trembled and shook as if her whole being quaked, until at last she took leave of her children and she died. She rose up into the shadowing mercy of God and was plunged in an ocean of light. Unto her be salutations and praise, compassion and glory. May God make sweet her resting-place with the outpourings of His heavenly mercy; in the shade of the Divine Lote Tree may He honour her dwelling.

*Khurshíd Begum "Shamsu'd-Duhá"*

Now that the story of the life of Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí has reached its conclusion, an account should be given of his respected consort. To this end I set forth the description which His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá -- exalted be His sovereignty -- has provided concerning that beatific leaf in His work *Memorials of the Faithful*.

*His most sweet utterance:*

Khurshíd Begum, who was given the title of "Shamsu'd-Duhá," was mother-in-law to the King of Martyrs. This eloquent, ardent handmaid of God was the cousin on her father's side of the famous Muhammad-Báqir of Isfahán, widely celebrated as chief of the 'ulamás in that city. When still a child she lost both her parents, and was reared by her grandmother in the home of that famed and learned mujtahid, and well trained in various branches of knowledge, in theology, sciences and the arts. // Once she was grown, she was married to Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí; and since she and her husband were both strongly attracted to the mystical teachings of that great luminary, the excellent and distinguished Siyyid Kázim-i-Rashtí, they left for Karbilá, accompanied by Mírzá Hádí's brother, Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí. Here they used to attend the Siyyid's classes, imbibing his knowledge, so that this handmaid became thoroughly informed on subjects relating to Divinity, on the Scriptures and on their inner meanings. The couple had two children, a girl and a boy. They called their son Siyyid 'Alí and their daughter Fátimih Begum, she being the one who, when she reached adolescence, was married to the King of Martyrs. // Shamsu'd-Duhá was there in Karbilá when the cry of the Exalted Lord was raised in Shíráz, and she shouted back, "Yea, verily!" As for her husband and his brother, they immediately set out for Shíráz; for both of them, when visiting the Shrine of the Imám Husayn, had looked upon the beauty of the Primal Point, the Báb; both had been astonished at what they saw in that transplendent Face, in those heavenly attributes and ways, and had agreed that One such as this must indeed be some very great being. Accordingly, the moment they learned of His Divine summons, they answered: "Yea, verily!" and they burst into flame with yearning love for God. Besides, they had been present every day in that holy place where the late Siyyid taught, and had clearly heard him say: "The Advent is nigh, the affair most subtle, most elusive. It behoves each one to search, to inquire, for it may be that the Promised One is even now present among men, even now visible, while all about Him are heedless, unmindful, with bandaged eyes, even as the sacred Traditions have foretold." // When the two brothers arrived in Persia they heard that the Báb had gone to Mecca on a pilgrimage. Siyyid Muhammad-'Alí therefore left for Isfahán and Mírzá Hádí returned to Karbilá. Meanwhile Shamsu'd-Duhá had become friends with the "Leaf of Paradise," sister to Mullá Husayn, the Bábu'l-Báb. Through that lady she had met Táhirih, Qurratu'l-'Ayn, and had begun to spend most of her time in close companionship with them both, occupied in teaching the Faith. Since this was in the early days of the Cause, the people were not yet afraid of it. From being with Táhirih, Shamsu'd-Duhá profited immeasurably, and was more on fire with the Faith than ever. She spent three years in close association with Táhirih in Karbilá. Day and night, she was stirred like the sea by the gales of the All-Merciful, and she taught with an eloquent tongue. // As Táhirih became celebrated throughout Karbilá, and the Cause of His Supreme Holiness, the Báb, spread all over Persia, the latter-day 'ulamás arose to deny, to heap scorn upon, and to destroy it. They issued a fatvá or judgment that called for a general massacre. Táhirih was one of those designated by the evil 'ulamás of the city as an unbeliever, and they mistakenly thought her to be in the home of Shamsu'd-Duhá. They broke into Shamsu'd-Duhá's house, hemmed her in, abused and vilified her, and inflicted grievous bodily harm. They dragged her out of the house and through the streets to the bazaar; they beat her with clubs; they stoned her, they denounced her in foul language, repeatedly assaulting her. While this was going on, Hájí Siyyid Mihdí, the father of her distinguished husband, reached the scene. "This woman is not Táhirih!" he shouted at them. But he had no witness to prove it, and the farráshes, the police and the mob would not let up. Then, through the uproar, a voice screamed out: "They have arrested Qurratu'l-'Ayn!" At this, the people abandoned Shamsu'd-Duhá. // Guards were placed at the door of Táhirih's house and no one was allowed to enter or leave, while the authorities waited for instructions from Baghdád and Constantinople. As the interval of waiting lengthened out, Táhirih asked for permission to leave for Baghdád. "Let us go there ourselves," she told them. "We are resigned to everything. Whatever happens to us is the best that can happen, and the most pleasing." With government permission, Táhirih, the Leaf of Paradise, her mother and Shamsu'd-Duhá all left Karbilá and travelled to Baghdád, but the snake-like mass of the populace followed them for some distance, stoning them from a little way off. // When they reached Baghdád they went to live at the house of Shaykh Muhammad-i-Shibl, the father of Muhammad-Mustafá; and since many crowded the doors there was an uproar throughout that quarter, so that Táhirih transferred her residence elsewhere, to a lodging of her own, where she continually taught the Faith, and proclaimed the Word of God. Here the 'ulamás, shaykhs and others would come to listen to her, asking their questions and receiving her replies, and she was soon remarkably well known throughout Baghdád, expounding as she would the most recondite and subtle of theological themes. // When word of this reached the government authorities, they conveyed Táhirih, Shamsu'd-Duhá and the Leaf of Paradise to the house of the Muftí and here they remained three months until word as to their case was received from Constantinople. During Táhirih's stay at the Muftí's, much of the time was spent in conversations with him, in producing convincing proofs as to the Teachings, analysing and expounding questions relative to the Lord God, discoursing on the Resurrection Day, on the Balance and the Reckoning, unravelling the complexities of inner truths. // One day the Muftí's father came in and belaboured them violently and at length. This somewhat discomfited the Muftí and he began to apologize for his father. Then he said: "Your answer has arrived from Constantinople. The Sovereign has set you free, but on condition that you quit his realms." The next morning they left the Muftí's house and proceeded to the public baths. Meanwhile Shaykh Muhammad-i-Shibl and Shaykh Sultán-i-'Arab made the necessary preparations for their journey, and when three days had passed, they left Baghdád; that is, Táhirih, Shamsu'd-Duhá, the Leaf of Paradise, the mother of Mírzá Hádí, and a number of Siyyids from Yazd set out for Persia. Their travel expenses were all provided by Shaykh Muhammad. // They arrived at Kirmánsháh, where the women took up residence in one house, the men in another. The work of teaching went on at all times, and as soon as the 'ulamás became aware of it they ordered that the party be expelled. At this the district head, with a crowd of people, broke into the house and carried off their belongings; then they seated the travellers in open howdahs and drove them from the city. When they came to a field, the muleteers set them down on the bare ground and left, taking animals and howdahs away, leaving them without food or luggage, and with no roof over their heads. // Táhirih thereupon wrote a letter to the Governor of Kirmánsháh. "We were travellers," she wrote, "guests in your city. 'Honour thy guest,' the Prophet says, 'though he be an unbeliever.' Is it right that a guest should be thus scorned and despoiled?" The Governor ordered that the stolen goods be restored, and that all be returned to the owners. Accordingly the muleteers came back as well, seated the travellers in the howdahs again, and they went on to Hamadán. The ladies of Hamadán, even the princesses, came every day to meet with Táhirih, who remained in that city two months. There she dismissed some of her travelling companions, so that they could return to Baghdád; others, however, accompanied her to Qazvín. // As they journeyed, some horsemen, kinsfolk of Táhirih's, that is, her brothers, approached. "We have come," they said, "at our father's command, to lead her away, alone." But Táhirih refused, and accordingly the whole party remained together until they arrived in Qazvín. Here, Táhirih went to her father's house, and the friends, those who had ridden and those who had travelled on foot, put up at a caravanserai. Mírzá Hádí, the husband of Shamsu'd-Duhá, had gone to Máh-Kú, seeking out the Báb. On his return, he awaited the arrival of Shamsu'd-Duhá in Qazvín, after which the couple left for Isfahán, and when they reached there, Mírzá Hádí journeyed on to Badasht. In that hamlet and its vicinity he was attacked, tormented, even stoned, and was subjected to such ordeals that finally, in a ruined caravanserai, he died. His brother, Mírzá Muhammad-'Alí, buried him there, along the roadside. // Shamsu'd-Duhá remained in Isfahán. She spent her days and nights in the remembrance of God and in teaching His Cause to the women of that city. She was gifted with an eloquent tongue; her utterance was wonderful to hear. She was highly honoured by the leading women of Isfahán, celebrated for piety, for godliness, and the purity of her life. She was chastity embodied; all her hours were spent in reciting Holy Writ, or expounding the Texts, or unravelling the most complex of spiritual themes, or spreading abroad the sweet savours of God. // It was for these reasons that the King of Martyrs married her respected daughter and became her son-in-law. And when Shamsu'd-Duhá went to live in his princely house, day and night the people thronged its doors, for the leading women of the city, whether friends or strangers, whether close to her or not, would come and go. For she was a fire lit by the love of God, and she proclaimed the Word of God with great ardour and verve, so that she became known among the non-believers as the "Fátimih the Radiant of the Bahá'ís." // And so time passed, until the day when the "She-Serpent" and the "Wolf" conspired together and issued a decree, a fatvá, that sentenced the King of Martyrs to death. They plotted as well with the Governor of the city so that among them they could sack and plunder and carry off all that vast treasure he possessed. Then the Sháh joined forces with those two wild animals; and he commanded that the blood of both brothers, the King of Martyrs and the Beloved of Martyrs, be spilled out. Without warning, those ruthless men: the She-Serpent, the Wolf, and their brutal farráshes and constabulary -- attacked; they chained the two brothers and led them off to prison, looted their richly furnished houses, wrested away all their possessions, and spared no one, not even infants at the breast. They tortured, cursed, reviled, mocked, beat the kin and others of the victims' household, and would not stay their hands. // In Paris, Zillu's-Sultán related the following, swearing to the truth of it upon his oath: "Many and many a time I warned those two great scions of the Prophet's House, but all to no avail. At the last I summoned them one night, and with extreme urgency I told them in so many words: 'Gentlemen, the Sháh has three times condemned you to death. His farmáns keep on coming. The decree is absolute and there is only one course open to you now: you must, in the presence of the 'ulamás, clear yourselves and curse your Faith.' Their answer was: 'Yá Bahá'u'l-Abhá! May our lives be offered up!' Finally I agreed to their not cursing their Faith. I told them all they had to say was, 'We are not Bahá'ís.' 'Just those few words,' I said, 'will be enough; then I can write out my report for the Sháh, and you will be saved.' 'That is impossible,' they answered, 'because we are Bahá'ís. Yá Bahá'u'l-Abhá! Our hearts hunger for martyrdom! Yá Bahá'u'l-Abhá!' I was enraged, then, and I tried, by being harsh with them, to force them to renounce their Faith, but it was hopeless. The decree of the rapacious She-Serpent and Wolf, and the Sháh's commands, were carried out." // After those two were martyred, Shamsu'd-Duhá was hunted down, and had to seek a refuge in her brother's house. Although he was not a believer, he was known in Isfahán as an upright, pious and godly man, a man of learning, an ascetic who, hermit-like, kept to himself, and for these reasons he was highly regarded and trusted by all. She stayed there with him, but the Government did not abandon its search, finally discovered her whereabouts and summoned her to appear; the evil 'ulamás had a hand in this, joining forces with the civil authorities. Her brother was therefore obliged to accompany Shamsu'd-Duhá to the Governor's house. He remained without, while they sent his sister into the women's apartments; the Governor came there, to the door, and he kicked and trampled her so savagely that she fainted away. Then the Governor shouted to his wife: "Princess! Princess! Come here and take a look at the 'Fátimih the Radiant of the Bahá'ís!'" // The women lifted her up and put her in one of the rooms. Meanwhile her brother, dumbfounded, was waiting outside the mansion. Finally, trying to plead with him, he said to the Governor: "This sister of mine has been beaten so severely that she is at the point of death. What is the use of keeping her here? There is no hope for her now. With your permission I can get her back to my house. It would be better to have her die there, rather than here, for after all, she is a descendant of the Prophet, she is of Muhammad's noble line, and she has done no wrong. There is nothing against her except her kinship to the son-in-law." The Governor answered: "She is one of the great leaders and heroines of the Bahá'ís. She will simply cause another uproar." The brother said: "I promise you that she will not utter a word. It is certain that within a few days she will not even be alive. Her body is frail, weak, almost lifeless, and she has suffered terrible harm." // Since the brother was greatly respected and trusted by high and low alike, the Governor released Shamsu'd-Duhá in his custody, letting her go. She lived for a while in his house, crying out, grieving, shedding her tears, mourning her dead. Neither was the brother at peace, nor would the hostile leave them alone; there was some new turmoil every day, and public clamour. The brother finally thought it best to take Shamsu'd-Duhá away on a pilgrimage to Mashhad, hoping that the fire of civil disturbances would die down. // They went to Mashhad and settled in a vacant house near the Shrine of the Imám Ridá. // Because he was such a pious man the brother would leave every morning to visit the Shrine, and there he would stay, busy with his devotions until almost noon. In the afternoon as well, he would hasten away to the Holy Place, and pray until evening. The house being empty, Shamsu'd-Duhá managed to get in touch with various women believers and began to associate with them; and because the love of God burned so brightly in her heart she was unable to keep silent, so that during those hours when her brother was absent the place came alive. The Bahá'í women would flock there and absorb her lucid and eloquent speech. // In those days life in Mashhad was hard for the believers, with the malevolent always on the alert; if they so much as suspected an individual, they murdered him. There was no security of any kind, no peace. But Shamsu'd-Duhá could not help herself: in spite of all the terrible ordeals she had endured, she ignored the danger, and was capable of flinging herself into flames, or into the sea. Since her brother frequented no one, he knew nothing of what was going on. Day and night he would only leave the house for the Shrine, the Shrine for the house; he was a recluse, had no friends, and would not so much as speak to another person. Nevertheless there came a day when he saw that trouble had broken out in the city, and he knew it would end in serious harm. He was a man so calm and silent that he did not reproach his sister; he simply took her away from Mashhad without warning, and they returned to Isfahán. Here, he sent her to her daughter, the widow of the King of Martyrs, for he would no longer shelter her under his roof. // Shamsu'd-Duhá was thus back in Isfahán, boldly teaching the Faith and spreading abroad the sweet savours of God. So vehement was the fiery love in her heart that it compelled her to speak out, whenever she found a listening ear. And when it was observed that once again the household of the King of Martyrs was about to be overtaken by calamities, and that they were enduring severe afflictions there in Isfahán, Bahá'u'lláh desired them to come to the Most Great Prison. Shamsu'd-Duhá, with the widow of the King of Martyrs and the children, arrived in the Holy Land. Here they were joyously spending their days when the son of the King of Martyrs, Mírzá Abdu'l-Husayn, as a result of the awful suffering he had been subjected to in Isfahán, came down with tuberculosis and died in 'Akká. // Shamsu'd-Duhá was heavy of heart. She mourned his absence, she wasted away with longing for him, and it was all much harder because then the Supreme Affliction came upon us, the crowning anguish. The basis of her life was undermined; candle-like, she was consumed with grieving. She grew so feeble that she took to her bed, unable to move. Still, she did not rest, nor keep silent for a moment. She would tell of days long gone, of things that had come to pass in the Cause, or she would recite from Holy Writ, or she would supplicate, and chant her prayers -- until, out of the Most Great Prison, she soared away to the world of God. She hastened away from this dust gulf of perdition to an unsullied country; packed her gear and journeyed to the land of lights. Unto her be salutations and praise, and most great mercy, sheltered in the compassion of her omnipotent Lord. *Finis*.

Section Two

The Father, Brothers and Sisters

Of the Twin Shining Lights

And

The Children and Grandchildren of Each

**
*Áqá Mírzá Ibráhím ***

The other son of Hájí Siyyid Mihdíy-i-Nahrí was named Áqá Mírzá Ibráhím. He ranked among the notables of Isfahán, and was held in the highest esteem by the Imám-Jum'ih, the government, and all other dignitaries. God bestowed upon him numerous offspring; to whit: four sons and a daughter, of whom notice will be taken later in these pages. The most famous of them were Áqá Mírzá Husayn (the Beloved of Martyrs), and Áqá Mírzá Hasan (the King of Martyrs), who in faith and certitude are peerless and unparalleled; who in self-sacrifice and detachment are unexampled; who in the sight of the incomparable God are accounted as among the near ones; and who within the pale of the Blessed Cause are possessed of a lofty rank and an exalted degree. Numerous Tablets revealed by the Pen of the Blessed Beauty in honour of these two high-born brothers attest their exalted station, and the favour they enjoy in the Court of Him Who is the King, the All-Knowing. I shall now provide a short account of each of these twin blissful, brilliant lights, after which I shall relate in detail the story of their martyrdom.

*Áqá Mírzá Husayn, The Beloved of Martyrs*

As mentioned, this illustrious figure, who was born in the year 1251 A.H., was the august scion of Áqá Mírzá Ibráhím, the son of Hájí Siyyid Mihdíy-i-Nahrí. His honour the Beloved of Martyrs was joined in wedlock to Fátimih Sultán Begum, and from this union resulted four children. After the passing of Fátimih Sultán Begum, a number of Tablets were revealed in her honour, two of which are as follows.

*Prayer for forgiveness revealed by the pen of His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá for the consort of his honour the Beloved of Martyrs:*

* Alláh-u-Abhá! *

O my God! O my God! This is a leaf that was stirred by the breaths blowing from the meads of Thy Lordship; and this is a maidservant who was illumined by the lights that gleamed and shone forth from the horizon of Thy Divinity, and who believed in Thy beauty; accepted Thy words; sought the guidance of Thy Verses; and beheld Thy signs. She was related to a tree that sprang up in the garden of Thy knowledge; became verdant through the bounty of the cloud of Thy mercy; blossomed with the flowers of Thy bestowal; brought forth the fruits of Thy love; weathered every tempest, storm and hurricane in Thy path; and took root and grew strong in the meads of Thy kingdom, through Thy grace and loving-kindness.
