Thereafter this maidservant that was conjoined to him remained, O my God, abased and humiliated for love of Thee amidst a ferocious band of predatory creatures and ravening wolves, bearing every affliction, sustaining every hardship, and enduring a cruelty of treatment such that pens are powerless to describe it. All this, O my God, she suffered in Thy path and for love of Thee, until her days came to an end, her dreams came true, and she returned unto Thee; ended her journey within the borders of Thy kingdom; arrived at the gate of Thy heavenly dominion; and took refuge in the shelter of Thy mercy. O my Lord! Honour her last resting-place, that in the immortal Paradise the Maids of Holiness may celebrate her arrival, and in the Abhá Paradise the Vestals of Sanctified and Blissful Communion may extend to her a joyous welcome. Confer Thou patience and consolation upon her offspring, the apples of her eye, at whose grief and sorrow; distress and desolation; affliction, anguish and bereavement the eyes of the Concourse on High run down with tears. Thou, verily, art the All-Bountiful, the Most Merciful. 'Ayn 'Ayn

*Photograph of his honour the Beloved of Martyrs, Áqá Mírzá Husayn*

* Alláh-u-Abhá! *

At the passing and ascension of that bird of the garden of certitude all hearts are sorrowful and afflicted; yet since that holy and spiritual leaf ascended from the vale of tears unto the apex of the loving-kindness of the Abhá Kingdom, and soared up from the darksome tribulations of this nether realm unto the illumined world of supernal happiness, all her offspring and relatives should be consoled. For, praise be to God, that tranquil leaf believed with perfect assurance in the Beauty of God; was serenely confident of His bounty; bore the most grievous tribulations in His pathway; and dwelt under the protection of a blessed soul through whom the kingdom of existence is bathed in light. Praise be to God too that she left behind her souls who are sheltered beneath the shadow of the Word of Divine Unity; who seek illumination from the Kingdom of Mercy; who are watched over by the Eye of Loving-kindness; and who are surrounded by the favours of the One True God. Upon you be His Glory. 'Ayn 'Ayn

*The Children of the Beloved of Martyrs*

1\) Fátimih Begum, who married a Muslim man named Áqá Mihdí Hájí Rasúl. When her illustrious father fell into the clutches of his enemies and the opponents of the Cause she suffered indescribable distress, succumbing, as a result of the innumerable calamities she witnessed, to a grievous malady. Her husband tended her throughout the course of a whole year, but finally, ere she was quite thirty, she passed away. Although her husband was a Muslim, he loved his wife unreservedly. In a Tablet addressed to him His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá -- exalted be His praise -- states the following.

*His most sweet utterance:*

O thou whom God hath honoured through alliance to the Beloved of Martyrs! Favours are of two kinds: the first is expected, and the second is unexpected. Expected favours are such as are known of in advance; whereas unexpected favours are such as are vouchsafed without question or entreaty; with no prior knowledge or expectation; and purely through the loving-kindness and special favour of God. Now hath the One True God made thee, unwitting and inadvertent as thou wast, the loving son-in-law of a holy being through the brightness of whose radiant brow the horizon of Supreme Sacrifice is effulgent, and through the lamp of whose delightful countenance the glass of Surpassing Bounty is illumined. In the realm of the Abhá Kingdom the throne of his grandeur is established; and amidst the company of the Supreme Concourse he is crowned with a diadem of hallowed glory. From the beginning of maturity until the time when thou didst become a believer thou neither hadst any expectation of such a favour, nor realized that such a bounty had fallen to thy lot. Therefore must thou appreciate with heart and soul the value of these bestowals, and yield with every breath a hundred thousand thanks. Since that leaf who hath drawn nigh unto the Court of Oneness hath suffered divers shocks that are beyond the power of human endurance, and in consequence whereof her physical temple hath been overcome by weakness, thou must ever provide such means as shall conduce to her peace and tranquillity, so that she may at least be in some sort comforted, and the injurious impressions left upon her heart may be mitigated. By the righteousness of God! To gladden her heart is to lend cheer to the hearts of the Supreme Concourse; while to comfort her is to impart consolation to the company of the Celestial Host. Upon thee be the Glory of God. 'Ayn 'Ayn

In another Tablet He says.

*His most sweet utterance:*

O thou holy and luminous leaf! Truly, in the path of God thou wast at all times the dawning-place of calamities and the focus of afflictions. Thou didst drink no draught of water in tranquillity, nor live a day without uttering a heart-consuming sigh. No night didst thou rest on the bed of comfort, nor for a moment didst thou taste the honey of tranquillity of heart and peacefulness of soul. Yet be not saddened, neither be thou sick at heart: for if placidity of heart, tranquillity of spirit, and bodily repose were truly beloved, praiseworthy and desirable, His Holiness the Prince of Martyrs would assuredly have sought and yearned after them; while all the Prophets and holy ones would have chosen them for themselves. Know therefore that calamities suffered in the path of the True One are a bounty; and contentment under His decree is among the characteristics of the people of God. ... 'Ayn 'Ayn

2\) Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alí, the son of the Beloved of Martyrs, was at the time of the latter's martyrdom four years of age. Later he was joined in wedlock to one of the grand-daughters of the sister of the King of Martyrs.

3\) Another son of the Beloved of Martyrs was Mírzá Ahmad, who at the time of his illustrious father's martyrdom was eleven years old. He married a woman, not wholly convinced of the truth of the Cause, named Tal'at, who bore him children. After the passing of Áqá Mírzá Ahmad, a Tablet was revealed for her from the sacred Pen of His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá -- exalted be His praise -- in which He counsels her to show forth faithfulness, and to devote herself to the education of her children so that they might be gathered under the shadow of the Cause. This lady did not, however, heed the Divine admonition, and came to an unhappy end. The text of that Blessed Tablet is as follows.

*His most sweet utterance:*

*The Land of Sád*

He is God

*The faithful and well-assured Maidservant of God, Consort of him who hath returned unto a seat of truth, Áqá Mírzá Ahmad, upon her be the Glory of God, the Most Glorious*

He is the Most Glorious, the Most Glorious

O maidservant of the Lord, thou goodly and luminous leaf! Although this calamity was so grievous that a mountain of iron would not have strength and patience to endure it, nor would the mightiest structure be able to withstand it, yet is the wisdom of the All-Glorious Lord hidden and concealed. Uncouth souls, however, are incapable of travelling and journeying in this vast immensity, nor do they advert to the truth of the mysteries of the One True God. At all events, thou must make patience and fortitude thine inner and outer raiment, and be content with the decree of God. The gaze of His loving-kindness is turned upon thee, and the glances of the Abhá Kingdom surround thy whole being. If thou conduct thyself with faithfulness, and, God willing, comport thyself in such wise as to bring consolation to the hearts, and ensure for the children a goodly upbringing and education, it will be a source of the greatest joy and happiness. In the sight of the True One, faithfulness is the greatest of all matters. 'Ayn 'Ayn

*Photograph of Hájj Mírzá Muhammad-Sádiq, eldest son of the Beloved of Martyrs*

4\) Mírzá Muhammad-Sádiq, the eldest of the children of his honour the Beloved of Martyrs, was at the time of his illustrious father's martyrdom fifteen years of age. He subsequently married the daughter of his honour the King of Martyrs, whose name was Sakínih Begum, and their union resulted a year later in the birth of a daughter, whom they named Khánum Díyá'. Not long thereafter the respected wife of Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-Sádiq, Sakínih Begum, took ill and winged her flight to the Abhá Kingdom. When news of this event reached the Most Holy Court, a Tablet was revealed for Mírzá Muhammad-Sádiq to the effect that he and the bereaved relatives of the martyrs were to have the honour of visiting those blessed precincts. Accordingly, he, together with Khurshíd Begum "Shamsu'd-Duhá;" Fátimih Begum, wife of the King of Martyrs; Khánum Afandí, sister of the aforesaid late Sakínih Begum; their two brothers; the handmaid of the King of Martyrs, named Hubbíyyih; Hanná-Sáb, the steward of the King of Martyrs; and a man-servant -- all were honoured to come into the most sacred presence of Bahá'u'lláh; and, in meeting Him Who is the King, the All-Knowing, the pangs of the vexations and calamities that had afflicted them were assuaged. This sojourn in the Holy Land was the first bounty that fell to the lot of Mírzá Muhammad-Sádiq after the passing of his wife. In brief, they tarried a while in those sacred purlieus until, when the period of their visit came to an end, they returned to Isfahán. Here, after organizing and arranging his affairs, Mírzá Muhammad-Sádiq married his second wife, named Túbá Khánum, who was the grand-daughter of his honour Mishkín-Qalam. This marriage took place ten years after the passing of his first wife, and resulted in numerous offspring.

A Blessed Tablet was revealed by the pen of His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá -- exalted be His praise -- in honour of the consort of Mírzá Muhammad-Sádiq and her children, which reads as follows.

*His most sweet utterance:*

*Isfahán. The respected Consort of the late Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-Sádiq; the Maidservant of God Rúhá; the Maidservant of God Húríyyih; the Maidservant of God Farah-Angíz; the Maidservant of God Aqdas; the Maidservant of God 'Adhrá; Husayn Áqá; Hasan Áqá, upon them by the Glory of God, the Most Glorious*

* He is the Most Glorious *

O ye survivors of two holy and blessed souls! Your letter was received. Its contents made clear and evident that through the protection and help of the Ancient Beauty ye remain, after the passing of his honour Sádiq, the august martyr's scion, firm and steadfast in the Cause of God. Ye are related to that servant of the Perspicuous Light, his honour Mishkín. That illustrious soul spent his whole life in humble, self-effacing, loving and sincere devotion to the Cause of God. He was the incarnation of spirit and the embodiment of light; and in outward perfections was distinguished from all his contemporaries. When he perfumed his nostrils with the sweet fragrance of the All-Merciful, he became completely selfless and detached from all else but the True One, sacrificing his own rest and comfort in the path of God. He migrated from Írán to the territory of the Ottomans, and, having dwelt for a while in 'Iráq, hastened to Rumelia. From thence he travelled to the Great City of Istanbul, where he occupied himself with service. He became famed throughout all parts, and was bound in fetters for his love of God. Being subsequently banished from the Great City, he attained in Gallipoli the sacred presence of Bahá'u'lláh, and, accompanying Him on His journey thence, passed his time on board ship with the utmost joy and gladness. Upon our arrival in 'Akká, he was borne off to Cyprus, and confined in the citadel of Famagusta, where, under conditions of the utmost difficulty and hardship, he remained long captive. When the island came into the possession of the just state -- that is to say, the English government -- he was set free. At once he hastened to the Most Great Prison, and joined the companions of Bahá'u'lláh. Day and night he was occupied in transcribing and arranging the Sacred Tablets, and in producing calligraphic renderings of the Greatest Name. These pieces spread throughout all parts, so that in Írán and Túrán, Europe and America, Asia and Africa -- there is no city where some example of his penmanship is not to be found. Most of these pieces are of the Greatest Name. One of the Persian ministers in Paris even related the following: "Ere proceeding to Paris I made first a quick visit to Russia. In the city of Moscow, I was in a state of the utmost sorrow, pain and despair, until, upon entering the home of one of my friends, a highly respectable personage, my eye fell upon a tableau suspended in the upper part of the room, which had been executed with the utmost skill by his honour Mishkín. It bore the words 'Yá Bahá'u'l-Abhá!' When my gaze fell upon the Greatest Name, occupying as it did the place of honour in the room of this great personage, I was all at once overcome by a feeling of immense joy and happiness. To this day the memory of that joy and happiness abideth with me; nor shall I ever forget it." The person who related this was the minister Mu'ayyiru'l-Mamálik, the son-in-law of Násiri'd-Dín Sháh. He attained the blessing of belief and certitude, and whilst in Paris was occupied with various services. Ye are likewise related to that brilliant candle, his honour Sádiq. That blessed soul beheld his illustrious father, his honour the Beloved of Martyrs, a sacrificial victim on the altar of love. It is evident, therefore, what injuries he sustained. Yet he remained under all circumstances patient, and in all trials constant, until he took his flight unto the Abhá Kingdom, the infinite world. Ye are the relations of these two blessed and holy souls, and very dear in the sight of 'Abdu'l-Bahá. I therefore supplicate at the Blessed Threshold that, under the shadow of God's loving providence, and in the shelter of His protection, ye may be shielded and preserved from all visitations and afflictions, and that ye may keep the shining lamp of those two illustrious souls ever luminous and refulgent; that ye may follow their example, and be worthy offspring of those illustrious forbears. Firdawsí in the Sháh-Námih saith of Kay-Khusraw:

This happy one doth of two kings signs bewray: * Of Afrásíyáb, likewise of Kávús-Kay

Upon you be the Glory of the Most Glorious

'Abdu'l-Bahá 'Abbás

4 Sha'bán 1339, Haifa

In brief, Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-Sádiq, the first child of his honour the Beloved of Martyrs, was born in 1278 A.H., and after completing sixty years on this mortal plane, during which he distinguished himself with all manner of spiritual and moral attainments, he ascended on the first of Shavvál of the year 1338 A.H. in Isfahán to the Divine world. His illumined grave is in the Takhtih-Fúlád district of the city. Thither a company of the friends bore his corse with all due ceremony and honour. Ere his passing, Mírzá Muhammad-Sádiq had willed that a structure be reared over his and the martyrs' graves. His descendants accordingly took measures to implement his wish, and raised up an imposing edifice. But the unregenerate divines, supported by base members of the populace, objected to the structure, and persuaded the governor of Isfahán, who was a military commander, to destroy it and remove the graves. The ignorant miscreants launched an assault upon the burial ground, breaking in pieces the stones and glazed tiles of the holy graves; destroying the mausoleum; and removing all the graves of the friends in its vicinity. When this incident was recounted to His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá -- exalted be His praise -- a Blessed Tablet was revealed, which reads as follows.

*His most sweet utterance:*

* He is God *

Thy detailed letter, dated the first of Muharram 1339, was received. Its perusal gave rise to painful sensations in my heart. Assuredly the friends of God are deeply affected by this incident in Isfahán, for inevitably the hearts of God's chosen ones are grieved by the violations wrought by enemies of the Cause. Yet, when one looketh at the reality of the matter, this incident in Isfahán affordeth also the greatest proof of the exaltation of those blessed souls, and a testimony to the abiding glory of the martyrs in the path of God. Observe ye that after the Prince of Martyrs -- may my life be a sacrifice unto Him -- was martyred in the days of the Umayyads, they utterly effaced and obliterated all traces from the place where that incident had occurred. They went so far as to plough and cultivate the desert so that the site of his martyrdom might be completely lost. Thus it was that for seventy years all traces of the place where he had been martyred were erased. The Umayyads even posted watchmen round about, for fear lest anyone should come within a farsang's distance in order to pay their respects. During this entire seventy-year period it was quite impossible to approach the site of martyrdom; and if anyone were a devout believer, only from a distance of ten farsangs would he turn towards the site of martyrdom, and pay his respects -- this too with the greatest of caution. In like manner, the illumined grave of His Holiness the Commander of the Faithful -- peace be upon Him -- was effaced and obliterated. After the 'Abbásids had extirpated the Umayyad Caliphate, and themselves mounted the throne of sovereignty, they permitted visits to the scene of martyrdom; and the lovers of His Holiness reared there various structures betokening the sanctity of that spot. After a lengthy interval, a binding edict was issued in the reign of the 'Abbásid Caliph Mutavakkil that those holy edifices should be a second time destroyed. Again they were utterly effaced and obliterated; the desert was ploughed; and all around was laid waste; nor was anyone allowed to come within even ten farsangs of the spot. So matters continued until the government, premiership and ministries fell into the hands of the Buwayhids, who were lovers of the Prince of Martyrs -- may my life be a sacrifice unto Him. Such power and ascendancy did they acquire as to deprive the 'Abbásid Caliphate of any influence: all affairs were concentrated in the hands of the Buwayhids, while the Caliph was left in his palace to busy himself with his own concerns. Under the Buwayhids the site of martyrdom was once again restored; while the illumined sepulchre of His Holiness the Commander of the Faithful was likewise raised up for the third time. 'Adudu'd-Dawlih, who is the greatest of the Buwayhid line, even renewed the Iranian monarchy, assuming the title of "Pádisháh." In the Friday sermon, after the mention of the Caliph himself came that of the kingship of 'Adudu'd-Dawlih in this guise. That day the Iranians in Baghdád celebrated as a festival, exclaiming "Praise be to God that the Iranians, having been reduced to utter nothingness, have once more raised aloft their standard!" That day too in the palace of 'Adudu'd-Dawlih outside Baghdád a musical festivity was arranged, and to the accompaniment of harp and carillon, melody and song, with stamping feet, waving arms, and clapping hands, the assembled company passed the day until late afternoon. Among the guests were a number of Iranian ministers, who put to 'Adudu'd-Dawlih the following request: "This day marketh the renewal of the Iranian monarchy, and is worthy of the utmost joy. Praise be to God, all manner of musical festivities are afoot. We pray thee, therefore, graciously to grant leave and permission that, according to the former custom of the Iranians, this auspicious day be celebrated with flask and goblet." 'Adudu'd-Dawlih replied: "The drinking of wine is agreeable in rain: 'Grateful is rain's pitter patter to topers.' Today there is no rain." After a little while a cloud appeared, followed by a heavy downpour. They said: "O king! The rain hath come! Fate and destiny have fulfilled thy bidding! No further obstacle remaineth." In brief, a new festivity got underway, and the wine-cup circulated among the guests. Meanwhile 'Adudu'd-Dawlih was taken ill. A violent pain gripped his intestines, and around dawn he gave up hope of life. They asked him: "O reverend sire! What testament hast thou to make?" He replied, "What testament should I make? I have no wish save this, that ye bury me like a sentinel at the threshold of His Holiness the Commander of the Faithful, and inscribe upon my grave: 'And their dog stretched forth his forelegs at the threshold.'" Behold, then, the latter end of these vicissitudes! Know thou of a certainty therefore that those pure graves shall become so exalted as to vie with Saturn. In the Cause of God, destruction is in itself the cause of construction, while the essence of abasement is the cause of the greatest glory. Being without name or trace is a token of the sublimest majesty. "Leave them to disport themselves with their vain pursuits." Upon thee be the Glory of the Most Glorious.

24 Safar 1339, Haifa, 'Abdu'l-Bahá 'Abbás

There now follows the Blessed Tablet of the Most Holy Abhá Beauty -- exalted be His grandeur -- in honour of the august scion of his honour the Beloved of Martyrs, the late Hájj Mírzá Muhammad-Sádiq, upon him be the Paradise of God's Good Pleasure.

*His honour Mírzá Sádiq, upon him be the Glory of God*

He is God, exalted be He; wisdom and utterance are His estate. Sád, Dál, Qáf. The memorial of the mercy of God unto the one who quaffed the choice wine of belief, and turned unto the horizon of Him Who is the Most Compassionate, holding fast unto His cord, clinging to the hem of His robe, steadfastly serving His Cause, and gazing upon the lights of His Countenance, until he arrived in His presence, and attained unto that which the Most Compassionate hath sent down in the Furqán, and in the Evangel, the Torah and the Psalms. Of Himself, and by Himself, the Tongue of Grandeur testifieth that there is no God but Him, the Help in Peril, the Self-Subsisting. He hath come down from the heaven of Proof with the standards of Perspicuous Utterance. Blessed be whoever witnesseth, and answereth His summons, and woe betide every heedless one, shut out as by a veil! O thou who hast come before this Wronged One, and dwellest now in His vicinity! He, verily, beareth witness that thou hast attained unto the promised reunion, and set thyself towards the True One at a time when all men have turned aside, except such as God, the Lord of all that was and shall be, was pleased to guide. Thou hast directed thy footsteps unto the Furthermost Goal and the Loftiest Summit, and hast soared up on the wings of detachment to the Lord of all creation, until thou didst stand before a Gate that hath been flung open before the dwellers of both the seen and the unseen realms. Render thou thanks unto God for that the Pen of the Most High so testifieth of thee on this moonlit night, when the Wronged One is seated upon His throne, and uttereth that which draweth the people nigh unto a glorious station. Blessed art thou, inasmuch as thou hast chosen thy destination, and set out upon thy journey, in such wise that neither the vagaries of the rulers nor the veils of the learned could deter thee; and hast left thy home trusting wholly in God, thy Lord, the All-Glorious, the All-Loving. We pray God to confirm and assist thee to do that which is pleasing and acceptable unto Him; and to cause to appear from thee in the world that which shall last as long as His Most Excellent Names and His Most Exalted Attributes shall last. Powerful is He to do that which He pleaseth through His word "Be," and it is. Praise be to God that thou hast hearkened, hast attained, and hast set thyself towards Him at a time when all have risen up to oppose Him. I beseech the One True God graciously to assist thee to preserve this most exalted station. He is the Almighty, the Most Powerful. The letter his honour Amín -- upon whom be My glory and My loving-kindness -- sent to thee was perused. The love and affection he expressed for thee and the members of thy household occasioned joy and gladness. We pray God to assist and succour him. He, verily, is the Most Gracious, the All-Bountiful. In truth, his honour Amín is steadfast in faith, and constant in rendering service. Blessed are they who are adorned with the adornment of love for the Twin Martyrs, for thee, and for thy kindred. Ye, verily, have an eminent position in the sight of God, the Lord of the worlds. Blessed be whoever turneth towards you; attaineth the bounty of your love; and heareth that which your tongue uttereth concerning the Cause of God, the Almighty, the All-Wise. The Glory of Our presence rest upon you, and upon Amín, and upon all that are staunch and steadfast, and all that are unwaveringly firm in their belief, and faithful to their trust.

Hereafter follows the prayer for forgiveness revealed by the pen of the Centre of the Covenant in honour of Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-Sádiq -- upon him be 9.

* He is God *

O my God, O my God! Not a day hath passed over 'Abdu'l-Bahá that hath not brought with it for him some dire calamity, nor any night hath ended wherein he hath not heard tidings of some grievous affliction such as to cause hearts to break, souls to melt, tears to flow, and sighs to ascend to heaven. O my Lord! This exalted offshoot of the tree of Supreme Sacrifice, the Beloved of Martyrs, was sore beset by tempests, and battered by gales, until at length he was uprooted by the hand of destiny, and extirpated by the assault of life's allotted term. O my Lord! This offshoot was once ripe and succulent, fresh and verdant through the outpourings of the cloud of Thy favours, and astir through the breeze blowing from the heaven of thy loving-kindness. He bore tribulations in Thy path, and was patient under adversities in Thy love. O my Lord! The wicked doers despoiled him, wrongfully, offensively, high-handedly and tyrannically, of his wealth and chattels, and ever, throughout his life, assailed him with arrows, spears and lances, and goaded him with lashing tongues, while the abject tormented him with their gloating and censure; neither did they weary of inflicting harm upon him, or cease from their enmity and oppression. Yet he remained patient under all manner of afflictions, and thankful in adversity, comporting himself with dignity amongst all people, oblivious alike of the blame of the blamer, and the dominion of the reviler. Rather, he stood as firm as the mountains before the iniquity of the people of error, and ever invoked Thee with all lowliness and fervour, saying: "Praised be Thou, O my God, for that Thou hast made me the heir of the Beloved of Martyrs, and the especial object of Thy mercy in this city that hath made such show of hatred and animosity. O my Lord! Make me patient under Thy trials; thankful under Thy decree; and steadfast in Thy Covenant and Testament, so that I may not be shaken by the onslaught of doubts, nor my feet be caused to slip by the calumnies of them that are shut out as by a veil from Thee; nay, may I rather rend asunder the veils, and behold the lights of Thy Beauty beaming from the kingdom of Thy signs. O my Lord! The veiled ones murmur privily night and day, while the breakers of Thy Covenant confer in whispers both at eventide and at dawn. Make me then a mighty barrier in the face of those ill-wishers, and an impregnable stronghold capable of resisting the dominion of those recreants. Protect me, then, from every wicked and mendacious aggressor, and from every debased and profligate assailant that feareth not the Day of Judgment. O my Lord! Make firm my steps on the Sirát; strengthen me in the pathway of deliverance; and grant that I may be numbered during the days of my life with Thy sincere servants, and after my passing with those who attain unto Thy favours. O my Lord! Rain down upon me the showers of Thy mercy; submerge me in the oceans of Thy forgiveness; illumine my countenance with the light of Thy pardon and Thy bestowal; rear for me a frequented fane within the precincts of Thy mercy; cause me to ascend unto the kingdom of Thy favours; and grant me a refuge from the torment of the ring-leaders of tyranny and oppression, that I may repose beneath the shadow of the Lote Tree of Thy mercifulness, and find shelter in the cave of Thy singleness. O my Lord! I am poor, enrich me from the treasury of Thy kingdom; captive, release me; broken, restore me, O Thou Healer of my spirit, of my heart, and of my mind; and lift me up unto the kingdom of Thy tender mercy." O my Lord! He, verily, left behind him children and grand-children: keep them safe by Thine aid and protection; guard them under the watchful glances of the eyes of Thy Lordship; and assist them to follow in the footsteps of their noble father, so that they may remain firm in their faith, and shun the people of error and rebellion. Guard them from the malice of those people who walk in the way of the Evil Whisperer, that they may be signs of guidance amidst mankind, and offshoots of the tree that was cut down in Thy path by the swords of oppression and hatred. Thus may their characters conform to their descent, and they may become the secrets of their sires and grandsires. Make theirs a true, not figurative, relation, that it be not said "O Noah! He, truly, is not of thy kindred: he, truly hath wrought unrighteously." O my Lord! Assist them; rear them up; and render them victorious over the heedless people. O my Lord! This august offshoot of that noble tree was, in truth, a faithful servant at the threshold of Thy Lordship, and turned his face wholly unto Thy noble countenance. Surround him, then, with Thine effulgent grace, so that he may attain unto a most glorious recompense; be immersed in the sea of light in the world of mysteries; become a dweller of the Most Exalted Paradise; enjoy the delights of the Garden of Refuge; and, like unto the birds upon the branches of the Lote Tree beyond which there is no passing, hymn ever anthems in celebration of Thy praise and Thy sanctity. Thou, verily, art the Ever-Forgiving; and Thou, verily, art the Benevolent, the Merciful, the Compassionate. 'Abdu'l-Bahá 'Abbás

*Mishkín-Qalam*

Incidentally to this relation mention was made of the late Mishkín-Qalam, whose grand-daughter, Túbá Khánum, became the consort of Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-Sádiq, the son of His honour the Beloved of Martyrs; wherefore, to supplement the respected readers' information, a brief account will now also be provided of his auspicious life.

Mishkín-Qalam was one of the outstanding early believers, and notable members of that privileged company who dwelt in close proximity to Bahá'u'lláh, and circled about His throne. His blessed name was Mírzá Husayn, and he was a native of Isfahán. Edward Browne has mistakenly characterized him in the introduction to the book *Nuqtatu'l-Káf* as "Khurásání" -- that is, a native of Khurásán. The circumstantial account and biographical description of him bequeathed by the pen of His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá -- exalted be His praise -- in *Memorials of the Faithful* is as follows.

*His most sweet utterance:*

*Mishkín-Qalam*

*Photograph of Mishkín-Qalam*

Among the exiles, neighbours, and prisoners there was also a second Mír 'Imád, the eminent calligrapher, Mishkín-Qalam. He wielded a musk-black pen, and his brows shone with faith. He was among the most noted of mystics, and had a witty and subtle mind. The fame of this spiritual wayfarer reached out to every land. He was the leading calligrapher of Persia and well known to all the great; he enjoyed a special position among the court ministers of Tihrán, and with them he was solidly established. He was famed throughout Asia Minor; his pen was the wonder of all calligraphers, for he was adept at every calligraphic style. He was besides, for human virtues, a bright star. // This highly accomplished man first heard of the Cause of God in Isfahán, and the result was that he set out to find Bahá'u'lláh. He crossed the great distances, measured out the miles, climbing mountains, passing over deserts and over the sea, until at last he came to Adrianople. Here he reached the heights of faith and assurance; here he drank the wine of certitude. He responded to the summons of God, he attained the presence of Bahá'u'lláh, he ascended to that apogee where he was received and accepted. By now he was reeling to and fro like a drunkard in his love for God, and because of his violent desire and yearning, his mind seemed to wander. He would be raised up, and then cast down again; he was as one distracted. He spent some time under the sheltering grace of Bahá'u'lláh, and every day new blessings were showered upon him. Meanwhile he produced his splendid calligraphs; he would write out the Most Great Name, "Yá Bahá'u'l-Abhá!," with marvellous skill, in many different forms, and would send them everywhere. // He was then directed to go on a journey to Constantinople, and set out with his honour Sayyáh. When he reached that Great City, the leading Persians and Turks received him with every honour at first, and they were captivated by his jet black, calligraphic art. He, however, began boldly and eloquently to teach the Faith. The Persian ambassador lurked in ambush; betaking himself to the Sultán's vizirs he slandered Mishkín-Qalam. "This man is an agitator," the ambassador told them, "sent here by Bahá'u'lláh to stir up trouble and make mischief in this Great City. He has already won over a large company, and he intends to subdue still more. These Bahá'ís turned Persia upside down; now they have started in on the capital of Turkey. The Persian Government put 20,000 of them to the sword, hoping by this tactic to quench the fires of sedition. You should awaken to the danger; soon this perverse thing will blaze up here as well. It will consume the harvest of your life; it will burn up the whole world. Then you can do nothing, for it will be too late." // Actually that mild and submissive man, in that throne city of Asia Minor, was occupied solely with his calligraphy and his worship of God. He was striving to bring about not sedition but fellowship and peace. He was seeking to reconcile the followers of different faiths, not to drive them still further apart. He was of service to strangers and was helping to educate the native people. He was a refuge to the hapless and a horn of plenty to the poor. He invited all comers to the oneness of humankind; he shunned hostility and malice. // The Persian ambassador, however, wielded enormous power, and he had maintained close ties with the ministers for a very long time. He prevailed on a number of persons to insinuate themselves into various gatherings and there to make every kind of false charge against the believers. Urged on by the oppressors, spies began to surround Mishkín-Qalam. Then, as instructed by the ambassador, they carried reports to the Prime Minister, stating that the individual in question was stirring up mischief day and night, that he was a trouble maker, a rebel and a criminal. The result was, they jailed him and they sent him away to Gallipoli, where he joined our own company of victims. They despatched him to Cyprus and ourselves to the 'Akká prison. On the island of Cyprus, his honour Mishkín was held prisoner in the citadel at Famagusta, and in this city he remained, a captive, from the year 85 till 94. // When Cyprus passed out of Turkish hands, Mishkín-Qalam was freed and betook himself to his Well-Beloved in the city of 'Akká, and here he lived encompassed by the grace of Bahá'u'lláh, producing his marvellous calligraphs and sending them about. He was at all times joyous of spirit, ashine with the love of God, like a candle burning its life away, and he was a consolation to all the believers. // After the ascension of Bahá'u'lláh, Mishkín-Qalam remained loyal, solidly established in the Covenant. He stood before the violators like a brandished sword. He would never go half way with them; he feared no one but God; not for a moment did he falter, nor ever fail in service. // Following the ascension he made a journey to India, where he associated with the lovers of truth. He spent some time there, making fresh efforts every day. When I learned that he was getting helpless, I sent for him at once and he came back to this Most Great Prison, to the joy of the believers, who felt blessed to have him here again. He was at all times my close companion. He had amazing verve, intense love. He was a compendium of perfections: believing, confident, serene, detached from the world, a peerless companion, a wit -- and his character like a garden in full bloom. For the love of God, he left all good things behind; he closed his eyes to success, he wanted neither comfort nor rest, he sought no wealth, he wished only to be free from the defilement of the world. He had no ties to this life, but spent his days and nights supplicating and communing with God. He was always smiling, effervescing; he was spirit personified, love embodied. For sincerity and loyalty he had no match, nor for patience and inner calm. He was selflessness itself, living on the breaths of the spirit. // If he had not been in love with the Blessed Beauty, if he had not set his heart on the Realm of Glory, every worldly pleasure could have been his. For wherever he went, his many calligraphic styles would have been a substantial capital, and his great accomplishments would have brought him attention and respect from rich and poor alike. But he was hopelessly enamoured of man's one true Love, and thus he was free of all those other bonds, and could float and soar in the spirit's endless sky. // Finally, when I was absent, he left this darksome, narrow world and hastened away to the land of lights. There, in the haven of God's boundless mercy, he found infinite rewards. Unto him be praise and salutations, and the Supreme Companion's tender grace.

*Photograph of the Apostles of Bahá'u'lláh: 1 -- Mírzá Músáy-i-Kalím 2 -- Badí'-i-Khurásání (bearer of the Tablet to the Sháh) 3 -- The King of Martyrs 4 -- Hájí Amín-i-Ardikání 5 -- Abu'l-Fadá'il-i-Gulpáygání 6 -- Varqá the martyr 7 -- Mírzá Mahmúd-i-Furúghí 8 -- Hájí Ákhund the Hand 9 -- Fádil-i-Qá'iní 10 -- Hájj Mírzá Muhammad-Taqí, Vakílu'd-Dawliy-i-Afnán 11 -- Ibn-i-Abhar 12 -- Nabíl-i-Zarandí 13 -- Shaykh Kázim-i-Samandar 14 Muhammad-Mustafáy-i-Baghdádí 15 -- Mishkín-Qalam 16 -- Mírzá Hasan-i-Adíb 17 -- Shaykh Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Qá'iní 18 Zaynu'l-Muqarrabín 19 -- Ibn-i-Asdaq*

Mírzá Husayn, known as "Mishkín-Qalam," was the son of the late Hájí Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Tájir-i-Isfahání. He was born in Isfahán in 1227 A.H., and after reaching maturity set about acquiring the rudiments of education customary in that age. He possessed a quite extraordinary capacity, native wit, and intelligence. While he was still young his father resolved to choose for him his life's partner, and contracted for Mírzá Husayn a marriage with a girl named Ághá Begum. The result of this union was a daughter, Sháh Begum, who was later joined in wedlock to a merchant named Áqá Mírzá Javád, known as Ántík-Furúsh, the son of Mírzá Abu'l-Hasan-i-Tájir. From this match was born a daughter, Túbá Khánum, who became afterwards the wife of Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-Sádiq, the son of his honour the Beloved of Martyrs. They in turn brought forth goodly offspring, whose names were listed individually above. Let us now pursue the biographical account of Mishkín-Qalam, which is the subject of these remarks. The father of Mishkín-Qalam, the late Hájí Muhammad-'Alí, was engaged in trade, and had dealings with reputed merchants both far and near. By chance, one of the merchants with whom he had dealings in Tihrán (or, by another account, in Mashhad), received from him a large quantity of merchandise, but for long remitted no payment; neither did he despatch any goods in return to the Hájí in Isfahán, nor was any word to be had of him. In order to discover how it fared with his business partner, Hájí Muhammad-'Alí decided to pay him a visit, and accordingly set off for Tihrán and Mashhad. Upon investigation, it emerged that his partner had become bankrupt and gone out of business. This intelligence discomfited the Hájí beyond measure -- to the degree indeed that he did not return to Isfahán, but bade farewell to life. His son, after receiving news of the passing of his father, himself set out for Tihrán in order to settle the accounts, and determine with the bankrupt party what course to take. At this period Mírzá Husayn was proficient in the various styles of penmanship; and was unrivalled throughout Írán for the exquisite beauty of his compositions and designs. Among his skills was the ability to produce on paper with his own fingernail the finest calligraphic designs, which he executed in an elegant white script. This writer has seen examples of his fingernail scripts. He was also, much to the amazement and bewilderment of the beholder, able to make sketches of people on paper by this same method. Whilst in Tihrán, he happened one day to be seated in the shop of an acquaintance when, with great pomp and circumstance, the procession of the Grand Vizir passed by. In the short space of time ere he disappeared from view, Mishkín-Qalam drew his likeness on a piece of paper with his fingernail, and handed it to his companion. The latter showed it to the Grand Vizir as he returned by the same route, and presented to him Mírzá Husayn. The Grand Vizir took him to the court, and, having again tested his abilities, introduced him to the Sháh. The Sháh extolled his skill, and established him in the position of tutor to the Valí-'Ahd, who was resident in Tabríz. He also bestowed upon Mírzá Husayn the title "Mishkín-Qalam," and lavished upon him numerous favours. Mishkín-Qalam sent his income to his family in Isfahán; but after a while his wife Ághá Begum travelled to Tihrán, and besought the Sháh to release her husband. After much coming and going, the Sháh granted Mishkín-Qalam leave to proceed to Isfahán, albeit only for a temporary and limited sojourn, that he might attend to the needs of his family. Mishkín-Qalam therefore returned to Isfahán; and it is to this visit that is owed the birth of another child, Mírzá 'Alí-Akbar. The latter subsequently married in the Holy Land, and produced a number of offspring, who, according to reports, are now resident in Baghdád, Damascus and Syria. In brief, Mishkín-Qalam's leave of absence came to an end, and, as bidden, he set out for Tihrán; but, receiving on the way the Divine Summons from Siyyid Mihdí, he became a believer in the Cause. So ardently indeed did he embrace it that, detached from all else but God, and forsaking all worldly attachments such as rank and wealth, he proceeded instead to Baghdád, and thence to Adrianople, where he was honoured to enter the Court of the presence of His Holiness Bahá'u'lláh -- exalted be His glory. The Sháh, becoming aware of his protracted absence, appointed persons to seek after him; but they could find no trace of him. As all this was in train, Mishkín-Qalam's wife, as previously mentioned, bestowed her daughter Sháh Begum in marriage upon Áqá Mírzá Javád. This latter, who was engaged in trade, had dealings and connections with the King of Martyrs and the Beloved of Martyrs, while at the same time his father was connected with the circle of the Imám-Jum'ih, for whom he worked as overseer. Áqá Mírzá Javád was sorrowed and troubled by the absence of Mishkín-Qalam. One day, the King of Martyrs and his brother, learning of the state of his heart, and of the fretful turmoil occasioned him by the disappearance of his father-in-law, whom he believed to be lost without trace, sought to assuage his anguish. "It being now more than twenty years since Mishkín-Qalam disappeared without trace," Áqá Mírzá Javád courteously informed them, "His wife is much afflicted by his absence; and my wife too grieveth her father's absence. The more diligently we search for him, the more hopeless seemeth our quest." The brothers now asked him whether, if he saw a picture of Mishkín-Qalam, he would recognize him. "Yes indeed," he replied. They then showed him a picture of Mishkín-Qalam, and put his mind at rest that Mishkín-Qalam was alive, unharmed, and in good health. Mírzá Javád took back the picture to his grieving womenfolk, and cheered and comforted them with the tidings that Mishkín-Qalam was well. He took them to the home of the King of Martyrs and the Beloved of Martyrs, where arrangements were in due course made for the removal of the wife and son of Mishkín-Qalam to the Holy Land, and for their journey thither. Meanwhile Mishkín-Qalam's daughter and her husband moved in with the family of the two respected brothers; and these arrangements proved so congenial that in the end, as we have previously related, Áqá Mírzá Javád's daughter Túbá Khánum became united in wedlock with Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-Sádiq, the eldest son of his honour the Beloved of Martyrs. In fine, Mishkín-Qalam was the object of surpassing favour; and after the ascension of the Ancient Beauty remained firm in the Covenant and Testament of God. Up to the ascension of the Blessed Beauty he signed his finished calligraphic works "Thrall of the Gate of Bahá', Mishkín-Qalam;" while thereafter, during the period of the Covenant and Testament, he signed them "Thrall of the Thrall of Bahá, Mishkín-Qalam." In the porch of one of the major cemeteries of Tabríz, which is situated near a famous Imám-Zádih, is an inscription, consisting of a very extensive ode, in the hand of Mishkín-Qalam, and signed also in his name. Mírzá Husayn "Mishkín-Qalam" was, as mentioned, born in 1227 A.H. After joining him in the Holy Land, his wife Ághá Begum sewed some of the sacred garments worn by the Blessed Beauty and the dwellers of the Tabernacle of Grandeur, and there at length passed away. Mishkín-Qalam ascended to the Abhá Kingdom in 1327 A.H. The person who had taught him the Faith was Áqá Siyyid Mihdí, while in the case of his daughter, Sháh Begum, and his son-in-law, the late Áqá Mírzá Javád, this office was performed by Hájí Mírzá Haydar-'Alíy-i-Isfahání, author of the works *Bihjatu's-Sudúr* and *Dalá'ilu'l-'Irfán*. Sháh Begum was born in 1252 A.H., and, full a hundred and nine years old, passed away in Muharram of the year 1361 A.H. in Tihrán, where she was laid to rest in the Eternal Rose Garden of the Land of Tá'. Many delightful tales and anecdotes are preserved of Mishkín-Qalam, which there is not here space to record. Most of these are, however, current upon the tongues, and familiar from the discourse, of the friends of God. A number of Tablets were revealed for Mishkín-Qalam by the sacred pens of Their Holinesses Bahá'u'lláh and 'Abdu'l-Bahá, which are available to the body of the friends.

*Áqá Mírzá Hasan, The King of Martyrs*

Another son of Áqá Mírzá Ibráhím, the son of Hájí Siyyid Mihdíy-i-Nahrí, was Áqá Mírzá Hasan, who was born in 1252 A.H., and subsequently styled by the pen of the Ancient Beauty -- exalted be His glory -- the King of Martyrs, the title by which he became known. As previously recorded, after the passing of Áqá Mírzá Hádíy-i-Nahrí, Áqá Mírzá Ibráhím wed Shamsu'd-Duhá, the wife of his deceased brother; and bestowed also her daughter, Fátimih Begum, on his middle son, Áqá Mírzá Hasan, the King of Martyrs.

The King of Martyrs was born in the year 1252 A.H. An account of Fátimih Begum has already been provided. At all events, the line of Áqá Mírzá Hasan, the King of Martyrs, was continued by a number of offspring.

The eldest of these was a boy named Jalál, who at the time of his father's martyrdom was in his twelfth year. Subsequent to that event, he travelled to the Holy Land; became the object of Divine bounty; and was honoured to become the son-in-law of His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá, begetting offspring of his own, as was touched upon in the account provided of the respected Consort of His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá.

Another child of the King of Martyrs was a girl by the name of Sakínih Begum, who, as previously recorded, was joined in wedlock to Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-Sádiq, the son of his honour the Beloved of Martyrs. Khánum Díyá', subsequently the wife of Mírzá 'Ináyatu'lláh-i-Isfahání, was the fruit of this union, as was also previously mentioned.

*Photograph of his honour the King of Martyrs, Áqá Mírzá Hasan*

Another child of the King of Martyrs was a daughter by the name of Khánum Afandí, who in the Holy Land wedded Siyyid Yahyá, the brother of the respected Consort of the Centre of the Covenant and Testament, and the son of Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí. The result of this union was a daughter named Khurshíd Khánum. At the time of her father's martyrdom in Isfahán Khánum Afandí was seven years old.

Another child of the King of Martyrs was 'Abdu'l-Husayn, who after his father's martyrdom proceeded to the Holy Land, where, at the age of eighteen, he passed away.

*Áqá Mírzá Ismá'íl*

Another of Áqá Mírzá Ibráhím's sons was Mírzá Ismá'íl, who was somewhat younger than the Twin Shining Lights. At the time when these latter were seized upon, he too was apprehended and made a prisoner; but on the fifth day he was released from confinement, and so spared death and martyrdom. This fact has been expressly mentioned in the Mathnaví of the late 'Andalíb, an article by Abu'l-Fadá'il, and other sources. Some of the relatives of the Twin Shining Lights have mentioned that the Imám-Jum'ih, wishing to extinguish a debt of eighteen thousand túmáns he owed them, and fearing lest the younger brother, who had emerged from prison, should set about recovering this sum, in order to appease and silence him cast a shawl about his shoulders, and announced that Áqá Mírzá Ismá'íl had become a Muslim. Never, however, did the latter utter a word that could by any means be construed as signifying that he had recanted, or lost his belief in the wondrous Divine Cause. In evidence of this is a Blessed Tablet and supplication for forgiveness revealed in his honour by the sacred pen of 'Abdu'l-Bahá, in which, after rendering thanks and praise to God, He intercedes for him as follows.

*His most sweet utterance:*

... and now, O my God, I turn from praising Thee to asking Thy forgiveness, Thy pardon and Thy favour for the one who was related to Thee, who put his reliance in Thee, and who hastened unto Thee that haply he might attain Thy presence; the namesake of him that was chosen to be slain, Thy servant Ismá'íl. O my Lord! O my Lord! He was a believer in Thee and in Thy signs; was tormented at the hands of Thine enemies, and afflicted during Thy days by reason of his relation to Thee, until at length death fastened upon him its claws; the Hour set down its burdens; and he returned unto Thee, lowly, humble, and submissive, hoping for Thy pardon and for reunion with Thee. O my Lord! O my Lord! Give him to drink from the cup of forgiveness; bestow upon him Thy kindness and favour; cause him to enter the Kingdom of Mercy, O Thou Lord of Grace and Bounty! Thou, verily, art the Mighty, the Loving. Thereafter I beseech Thee, falling on my face to the dust, bowing down and prostrating myself before Thee, O my Gracious and All-Bountiful Lord, to forgive him who was related to the Door of Thy Oneness, who supplicated the Court of Thy Mercifulness, and who was wont to guard against, and hide from, the assault of the enemies, until the end drew nigh; the time to bid farewell approached; and, forsaking all save Thee, he responded unto Thy call. His heart grew lowly; his love surged; his eyes ran down with tears in this extremity; and he sighed as one distraught with love during those days, such was his yearning towards Thee. He begged forgiveness for his transgression, trusting in Thee; and pronounced his confession before the remnant of God's mercy, declaring: "I, verily, am returning unto my Lord, the All-Bountiful, who beholdeth me in this perplexity. Thou, verily art the Ever-Forgiving, the All-Glorious, the Most Generous, the Loving." 'Ayn 'Ayn

The truth of his condition is in reality and actual fact made completely clear and evident by the tenor and expressions of the above Blessed Tablet. Áqá Mírzá Ismá'íl married the daughter of Mírzá Taqí, son of Mírzá Muhammad-Shafí'-i-Khú'í, who was among the circle of those close to the Imám-Jum'ih, Mír Siyyid Muhammad. Mírzá Taqí, his daughter Rabábih Sultán, and the rest of his relations were all Muslims and inimical to the Cause. According to the received account, this Mírzá Taqí, the father-in-law of Áqá Mírzá Ismá'íl, wrote on his son-in-law's behalf a letter to the Imám-Jum'ih, explicitly denying that he was affiliated with the Cause of God, or a believer in the Divine Law. Confirming its tenor, the Imám and the Shaykh wrote in the margin of the letter that Mírzá Ismá'íl was a Muslim, and sent him a ceremonial robe. By this means Rabábih Sultán and her father saved their relation by marriage from imprisonment and other trials. But in any case, this measure was to no avail, for only about a year after the martyrdom of the Twin Shining Lights Áqá Mírzá Ismá'íl passed away. His wife and family were outside the Cause, nor has any mention survived of them. In the supplication for forgiveness revealed for Mírzá Ismá'íl by the sacred pen of 'Abdu'l-Bahá, the two expressions that he was "wont to *guard against*, and *hide from*, the assault of the enemies" indicate that during that period of testing he was attempting to dissimulate and conceal his belief. Assuredly, however, God's merciful favours after his passing will ensure the forgiveness of his sins, and the pardon of his transgressions. In a Blessed Tablet revealed from the pen of the Ancient Beauty -- exalted be His glory -- in honour of Áqá Mírzá Áqáy-i-Afnán, known as Núri'd-Dín, the following has been revealed in respect of Mírzá Ismá'íl.

*His exalted Utterance:*

As for what thou didst write concerning his honour Mírzá Ismá'íl, his letter was presented in its entirety. He said: "In some of what he hath mentioned he is right. He should be treated with kindliness and forbearance." A most sublime and holy Tablet was also revealed especially for him from the firmament of Grace, and despatched hence. Do thou o'erlook it, and send it on. Our Lord, truly, is the Ordainer, the All-Knowing, the All-Wise. The aforenamed Mírzá hath however fallen into great perplexity. Although he is right in asserting that the Cause is great, inasmuch as it is related unto God, yet were he to immerse himself a while in the ocean of awareness he would perceive that it is, and ever shall be, the finest, the greatest, the truest, the most perfect, and the most exalted of all matters in the world. *Finis*.

*Áqá Mírzá Kázim*

Another of Mírzá Ibráhím's children was a boy named Mírzá Kázim. Having attained the blessing of faith and certitude, he ascended to the eternal world in his twenty-fifth year. As was recorded in the account of her Holiness the respected Consort of the Centre of the Divine Covenant and Testament, when deprived of the companionship of his cousin Fátimih Khánum, the daughter of his paternal uncle Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí, he succumbed to a malady, closed his eyes to this world, and removed to the everlasting realm. Áqá Mírzá Kázim was buried in Sar-Qabr Áqá, where his resting-place is known.

*Khadíjih Sultán Begum*

Another child of Áqá Mírzá Ibráhím was a girl named Khadíjih Sultán Begum, who, after attaining the years of maturity and discretion, was joined in wedlock to a wealthy Muslim by the name of Hájí 'Abdu'l-Ghaffár, thereafter enjoying, as was the custom in that period, the attendance of numerous men and women servants. A number of sons and daughters resulted from this union, as for example Zahrá Begum, who married a Muslim merchant, from whom she had numerous offspring: among them Liqá'íyyih Khánum, who became the wife of Mírzá Muhammad-'Alí, son of his honour the Beloved of Martyrs, and who, as previously mentioned, produced five children of her own, namely, Mírzá Ibráhím, Mírzá Ismá'íl, Mírzá Hasan, Ridváníyyih and Munavvar Khánum, all of them lovers of the Cause; and another named Munavvar Khánum. Another of Khadíjih Sultán's daughters, Fátimih Begum, wed after her mother's passing one Mírzá Áqá, brother of Mírzá Fadlu'lláh Malik, and had two daughters, Munírih and Tal'at by name, who do not associate with the friends. A third daughter of Khadíjih Sultán Begum, whose name was Gawhar Begum, passed away at the age of fifteen as the result of an apoplectic fit. Khadíjih Begum bore also two sons, namely Mírzá Muhammad-'Alí and Mírzá 'Abdu'l-Husayn, both of whom passed away in their infancy. Her husband too passed on after suffering the collapse of his business in Tihrán. Of necessity Khadíjih Sultán then travelled from Tihrán to Isfahán, where finally, still only in her thirty-fifth year, she ascended to the Abhá Kingdom. After the martyrdom of her beloved brothers she was immensely downcast and disconsolate. Ruffians dragged her from the home of her Muslim husband, and carried her before Zillu's-Sultán; but when the governor tried to persuade her to recant, Khadíjih Sultán stood firm. He then returned her to the home of her husband, who bore her off with him from Isfahán to Tihrán, where he fixed his abode. She subsequently rendered services in the field of protecting the property of the martyrs and caring for their survivors, before passing away in Isfahán, where she was buried in Takhtih-Fúlád. After her death, a Visitation Tablet was revealed for her by the pen of the Ancient Beauty -- exalted be His glory -- which reads as follows.

*His exalted Utterance:*

*The sister of the King of Martyrs, upon her be the Glory of God, the Most Glorious*

The first praise that emanated from the Tongue of Majesty and Grandeur rest upon thee, O most exalted leaf, thou who didst shine forth above the horizon of the Cause in the days of God, the Lord of all names! I testify that thou didst turn towards the Supreme Horizon, and didst attain unto that which most of the people have not attained. Thou, verily, didst bear grievous hardships and trials in the path of God; yet wast thou so steadfast in the Cause that neither the blame of the blamers nor the denial of the disbelievers could deflect thee from His way. Blessed art thou, and whosoever visiteth thy grave, and reciteth over thee that which hath been revealed by the Pen of God, the Exalted, the Most Glorious, He Whom the wrong-doers have imprisoned in the fortress-town of 'Akká. I testify moreover that thou didst yield up thy spirit by reason of thy separation, after thou hadst been ablaze with the fire of yearning in the days of God, the Lord of the Judgment Day. Woe betide a people who have not recognized thy worth, nor quaffed the cup of thy remembrance; and happy whosoever commemorateth thee in the words wherewith God Himself hath spoken in the Books and Tablets! *Finis*.

Section Three

A Brief Account of the Martyrdom

Of the Twin Shining Lights

*Photographic views of the interior of the house of the King of Martyrs and the Beloved of Martyrs*

*Description of the Martyrdom of the Twin Shining Lights*

The manner of the martyrdom of the Twin Shining Lights, and the reason for the occurrence of this fearful event, are said to be as follows. Mír Muhammad-Husayn, who had become after his brother the Sultánu'l-'Ulamá, the Imám-Jum'ih of Isfahán, appointed Áqá Mírzá Hasan and Áqá Mírzá Husayn to act as overseers and agents of his affairs and transactions, exactly as their own father, Áqá Mírzá Ibráhím, had, as previously mentioned, acted as overseer and personal agent of the previous Sultánu'l-'Ulamá and Imám-Jum'ih. For a while these two brothers financed all remittances made by the Imám, and in addition expended a huge sum in acquiring for him properties, estates and lands, setting these payments against his name in their accounts. At length they bethought them to reckon up the figures, and, after careful investigation, discovered that they had disbursed the sum of eighteen thousand túmáns in remittances on his behalf. They accordingly reported their findings to the Imám, and requested payment of the debt he owed them. The Imám, who worshipped no other god but Mammon, voiced outwardly no objection, and made arrangements for the auditing of the accounts. The process of auditing extended over forty days, during which period, night and day, the Imám, his dependents, scribe, secretary, servants and relations, under the pretext of seeing to the accounts, were for the most part accommodated in the home of the two brothers, and regaled at their table of bounty. At the end of these forty days, the Imám, finding the accounts to be accurate, promised to settle the debt he owed them. All these proceedings, however, were merely for the sake of appearance, for inwardly he was scheming to deter the two brothers by any means from pursuing their claim, or else to light upon some means of compassing their downfall, or even death. As it happened, a festivity that had been arranged to mark the betrothal of Fátimih Begum, the daughter of the Beloved of Martyrs, to Áqá Mihdí Hájí Rasúl took place at this very time, extending over an entire week. The staging of this festivity served only to inflame the envy and hatred of the Imám, and he resolved to find an accomplice in his scheme of disposing of his faithful creditor. By chance, he met one day in the public bath the well-known mujtahid Shaykh Báqir, who would afterwards be styled by the pen of the Most Sacred Abhá Beauty the "Wolf;" bared to him the secret design of his heart; and, agreeably with the principle that "The devils, verily, inspire unto their followers specious words beguilingly," induced the Shaykh, through the tempting prospect of laying hands upon the wealth and property of those two brothers, to become a partner in his scheme. Then and there in the public bath they decided by what dastardly means they would accomplish their plan of doing away with those intimates of the Divine Threshold; and resolved to make the Zillu's-Sultán, Mas'úd Mírzá, the governor-general of Isfahán and of other extensive areas of Írán, their confidential associate in this weighty undertaking. Having left the public bath, each one busied himself with his own part in the heinous plan, looking the while for a suitable opportunity to put it into effect. Among other measures, they succeeded, after meeting with Zillu's-Sultán, in enlisting him too as their accomplice. They fixed the seventeenth day of Rabí'u'l-Avval, which is by one account the Birthday of His Holiness the Apostle of God, as that on which they would lay hold upon those twin apples of the Chaste One's eye, and intimates of the Threshold of God and His Apostle. When the appointed day arrived, the three brothers went to see the Imám-Jum'ih in order to offer him their congratulations on the occasion of the Birthday of the Apostle of God -- may the blessings of God and His salutations be upon Him. The three in question were Áqá Mírzá Ismá'íl, Áqá Mírzá Hasan and Áqá Mírzá Husayn, brief accounts of each of whom have been provided in the foregoing. At this reception, which was thronged by high and low alike, Áqá Muhammad-Báqir-i-Mudarris, who was a respected, grave and eminent figure, the father-in-law of Zillu's-Sultán, and an acquaintance and friend of the illumined brothers, warned Áqá Mírzá Hasan, "Get thee out of this inauspicious gathering with all possible haste, and hide thee in some safe place, for the Imám is resolved that thou shouldst die." Another of his acquaintances who was also present in the gathering confirmed these words. At once, and without informing anyone, Áqá Mírzá Hasan left for the home of Áqá Muhammad-Báqir-i-Mudarris. The two other brothers remained in the gathering; but after a while arose, and sought the Imám's permission to depart. This the Imám refused to grant, saying, "I have business with you." "Where is Áqá Mírzá Hasan?" he asked. They replied, "He hath already left." The Imám was displeased, and sent off several officials with instructions to lay hold upon him wherever they should find him, and bring him before him. The officials, accompanied by a crowd of miscreants and ruffians, went first to the home of Áqá Mírzá Hasan, beat upon the door, and demanded to be informed of his whereabouts. The members of his household replied that he had gone to the home of the Imám and not yet returned. The pitiless band entered the house to search it, not sparing to commit certain indignities; but finding there no trace of Áqá Mírzá Hasan, proceeded to other homes and locations. Finally, at the direction of certain trouble-makers, they turned their steps towards the home of Áqá Muhammad-Báqir-i-Mudarris. The Shaykh and the Imám sent a message to the governor in the following terms: "Áqá Mírzá Hasan is hidden in the home of Áqá Báqir: do thou command him to deliver him into our hands." Dismayed by this communication, the governor sent word to Áqá Báqir. Upon hearing what had happened, Áqá Báqir was sorely troubled, and at once departed for his home. Having there related the story to Áqá Mírzá Hasan, he proceeded with the latter on horseback to the government house. The moment Zillu's-Sultán saw Áqá Mírzá Hasan, he assailed him with recriminations and reproaches, and bade him curse and execrate his faith. Áqá Mírzá Hasan however remained silent. Zillu's-Sultán again repeated his demand, this time more vehemently than before. Still Áqá Mírzá Hasan preserved his silence. The governor grew angry, and once more charged him to execrate his faith. When this behest met again only with silence, he became irate, and exclaimed, "Methinks the accusation that thou art a Bahá'í is true!" Áqá Báqir, wishing to allay the governor's wrath, said, "Áqá Mírzá Hasan is for the moment unsettled by this incident: it would be better for thee to shew him forbearance, and to defer this matter to another time." Zillu's-Sultán retorted, "It is not so. He meaneth rather to shew me disrespect: for this reason he deigneth not to reply." This said, with a switch he had in his hand he dealt many violent blows upon the head and face of that quintessence of piety, thereafter ordering his bloodthirsty myrmidons completely to strip that oppressed one of his raiment, and to cast him headlong into prison. Hearing of this, his family immediately sent him fresh raiment. When news of the apprehension of Áqá Mírzá Hasan reached the Imám, he sent the two other brothers to prison also. The Twin Shining Lights conveyed to their spouses a message to this effect: "Be ye patient and thankful in adversity, and under no circumstances have recourse to, or seek redress from, the Shaykh and the Imám." Their families and relatives accordingly refrained from any action of this sort. The merchants of Isfahán, however, who had transactions and dealings with those two wronged ones, and were anxious to preserve their investments, sent numerous telegrams to the Sháh of Írán, Násiri'd-Dín Sháh. A telegram came from the Sháh to Zillu's-Sultán instructing him to send the prisoners to Tihrán. In the meantime, the Shaykh and the Imám prosecuted their seditious plan, seeking to inflame the passions of the people. Zillu's-Sultán chose a group of merchants, and sent them to the prison in order to admonish the captives. Following his directions, the merchants bespake them, "Write down the names of all those with whom ye have an account." Those prisoners and angelic harbingers of the Kingdom made answer, "The people with whom we have dealings are extremely numerous, and the accounts are all set down in the trading house ledger." Returning to the governor, the merchants reported to him what had passed. Zillu's-Sultán ordered the trading ledger to be brought before him; and when this was produced, kept it with him and examined it, exclaiming in admiration at the order and method of their work. In the course of his examination he became aware that the Imám-Jum'ih owed them the sum of 18,000 túmáns, and understood why the Imám was so insistent upon their death. Successive telegrams arrived for the governor from the Sháh, conveying in respect of the prisoners an emphatic recommendation that under no circumstances should they be harmed. At all events, five days passed from the time of their apprehension without further incident; then, on the sixth day, their younger brother, Áqá Mírzá Ismá'íl -- in order, so it is said, to arrange for the payment of a sum of money to Zillu's-Sultán -- was released from confinement and set free; while the two brothers whose destiny the pen of fate had decreed as martyrdom remained in prison. Since the Imám knew that the Sháh was opposed to the death of the two brothers, and had ordered their transference to Tihrán, he sought to incite the populace, and along with Shaykh Báqir and other accomplices raised the cry of "*Wá Sharí'atá!*" -- "Woe to the Sharí'ah!" The people of Isfahán shut up their shops; and finally, on 23 Rabí'u'l-Avval 1296 A.H., in the manner circumstantially set forth in the Mathnaví of 'Andalíb and the works of Abu'l-Fadá'il-i-Gulpáygání, those two heroic souls met each a martyr's death. In the arena of martyrdom, the two brothers vied with each other who should be the first to lay down his life. In the end, Áqá Mírzá Hasan drained the cup of martyrdom before his elder brother; nor did his elder brother delay long in joining him. One of the poems of Na'ím contains the following line:

Did any wronged one, save in Isfahán, * Reward the instrument of his own death?

The allusion in this verse is to his honour the King of Martyrs, Áqá Mírzá Hasan, who at the hour of his martyrdom bestowed upon his executioner a ring he had in his hand, saying: "His Holiness Bahá'u'lláh instructeth us to kiss the hand of the murderer. Here, take thou this ring as a remembrance of me." After execution had been made upon them, a cord was tied to the feet of those two enraptured worshippers in the precincts of the Best-Beloved, and they were dragged hither and thither about the city. Finally, the two bodies were cast beneath the gallows in the central square. A crowd of malefactors and miscreants rushed upon the home of those two ardent candles in the assembly of blissful reunion with the Beloved; carried off all they could find, sacking also their business chamber; and handed over the greater part of the plunder to the Imám-Jum'ih. The lamentation of the bereaved members of the two households was such as none could bear to hear. The Shaykh and the Imám sent to them this message: "If the voice of your lamentation be raised, every form of tribulation shall fall upon your heads." Thus precluded from weeping openly, those wronged ones gathered in cellars where, softly and silently, they allowed their tears to flow. The bodies of the two martyrs remaining the while where they had been cast at the foot of the gallows, ill-wishers would fain have suspended them from the gallows; but Áqá Báqir-i-Mudarris prevented this design, and, going to Zillu's-Sultán, obtained permission to bury them. The Shaykh and the Imám decreed that those luminous corses should not be buried ere three days had passed. Áqá Báqir then paid another visit to Zillu's-Sultán, as a result of which the governor issued a strongly worded farmán ordering the burial to proceed. Ere this could be carried out, however, a group of seminary students, acting under orders from the Shaykh and the Imám, tied a cord to the feet of the two martyrs, took them away, and cast them into a ruined place, where they knocked down a wall over their bodies. After exerting further efforts, Áqá Báqir finally succeeded in having those hallowed bodies laid to rest. What the members of their households suffered in the meantime He Who is the Inaccessible Truth is alone aware. A hint of their woes has nevertheless been conveyed in poetic form in the Mathnaví of 'Andalíb-i-Láhíjání -- may the mercy of God rest upon him.

It is related that on the fifth day of the wronged brothers' confinement Zillu's-Sultán received from them the sum of nine thousand túmáns, and released from prison their younger brother Áqá Mírzá Ismá'íl so that he might obtain, and make payment of, a further three thousand túmáns, and also bring raiment to the prisoners. Having attended to these matters, Mírzá Ismá'íl would have returned to the prison, but his wife opposed his wish, and sent the money and raiment by another means. It is also well known that Zillu's-Sultán did not presume himself to give the order for their death, and that the Imám, the Shaykh and a number of others inscribed and sealed the fatvá decreeing their execution, and passed it on to him. The governor even tried moreover to hide his executioner, hoping by this means to forestall the event, for he was fearful of the Sháh's wrath. But Shaykh Muhammad-Taqíy-i-Najafí, the son of Shaykh Báqir, rolling up his sleeve amidst an assemblage of people, cried out: "I myself am prepared to shed their blood!" However this may be, it is related that Zillu's-Sultán too received from the Imám and the Shaykh a considerable share of the looted and pillaged property of the wronged martyrs. Leading those who ransacked the home and commercial chamber of the martyrs was the secretary and scribe of the Imám-Jum'ih, Hájí Shaykh Ahmad. The value of their property, jewels and possessions borne off as plunder was quite prodigious, and beyond reckoning. There remained in the house no vessel wherewith to give the children a drink of water, nor any scrap of food wherewith to appease the pangs of hunger. The tradespeople refused even to sell them any comestibles. At the time of their martyrdom Áqá Mírzá Husayn, the Beloved of Martyrs, was forty-five years of age, while the King of Martyrs, Áqá Mírzá Hasan, was a little over forty-three. The writer has heard the late Ghulám-Ridá Khán-i-Tawfíq, who was one of the early believers of Isfahán, relate the following: "On the day of the martyrdom of those twin luminaries I was a witness of the fearful business, and saw with my own eyes the bodies of the King of Martyrs and the Beloved of Martyrs fallen, stripped and naked, upon the ground. They had upon them only the scantiest covering. The sides of the twin beatific martyrs had turned black and blue from the blows which, with the soles of heavy boots, those bent on vengeance were inflicting upon the God-intoxicated pair." The Ancient Beauty -- exalted be His glory -- has stigmatised the slayer of the twin martyrs -- that is, the Imám-Jum'ih -- as the "second 'Ubaydu'lláh;" and, in a Tablet to Zaynu'l-Muqarrabín, affirms:

Through the appearance of but one of the deeds of the She-Serpent, the second 'Ubaydu'lláh hath been made manifest; and he hath committed that wherefrom the first 'Ubaydu'lláh shrank. *Finis*.

The 'Ubaydu'lláh here alluded to is the son of Zíyád Ibn-i-Abí-Hi, at whose command His Holiness the Prince of Martyrs, Husayn Ibn-i-'Alí -- peace be upon Him -- was martyred in Karbilá. The story of that event is sufficiently well known. In the course of this same Tablet Bahá'u'lláh avers:

... For whatever property hath been plundered in the Land of Sád, and for each drop of blood that hath been unjustly shed therein, innumerable souls have departed from this world, and shall depart hereafter, ... Sleep hath not overtaken the One True God, nor ever shall it. He is awake and aware, and the wrongdoers shall assuredly taste the bitter fruits of their actions. *Finis*.

Remarkably, in the famine of 1288, which afflicted the people of Írán after the martyrdom of Badí', these two brothers had shown the utmost kindness towards the people of Isfahán in general, saving a great multitude from starvation and hunger. In particular, they had lavished much kindness and assistance on a person by the name of Ramadán, who was the executioner. Yet still the people were prepared to countenance so grievous a wrong against their twin benefactors. Abu'l-Fadá'il says in the book *Fará'id*:

*The author's words (page 726):*

One of the merchants of Isfahán by the name of Muhammad-Taqí related the following: "When in 1296 A.H. there occurred the event of the martyrdom of the Twin Shining Lights, Al-Hasan and Al-Husayn -- may God sanctify their resting-place -- I happened to go on the afternoon of 23 Rabí'u'l-Avval, which was the actual day of their martyrdom, on a business errand to Julfá, the Christian quarter, where I found all the inhabitants downcast and sorrowful at this momentous event. During my visit a foreign maid asked me why these two honourable Siyyids had been put to death. I replied, 'Because they were of the Bábí sect.' 'I know they were Bábís,' she persisted, 'But what good came of putting them to death? Were they not descendants of your Prophet, and famed for the excellence of their character and the righteousness of their conduct?' I replied, 'Our 'ulamá regard their death as a deed meriting a Divine recompense, and account it as a means of drawing nigh unto His Holiness the Lord of Lords.' Upon hearing these words, she looked at me in astonishment, and exclaimed, 'Woe to you, O unaccountable people! If slaying the lineage of the Prophet be among your good deeds, how then must be your bad deeds?' With this, she quickly averted her countenance, and with the utmost speed hastened in the direction of her home ..." *Finis*.

*Facsimile of a letter written on the Guardian's behalf to the Spiritual Assembly of Isfahán. The text of the letter is as follows: "Isfahán. The holy Spiritual Assembly, may God strengthen its pillars. // The petition presented by those Trustees of the Merciful dated (4 / 6 / 1317) arrived at the sacred court of his Holiness the Guardian of the Cause of God -- may our souls be a sacrifice unto him -- and the matters detailed therein concerning the house of his honour the King of Martyrs and of his honour the Beloved of Martyrs, which is accounted among the historic Holy Sites, and which, after the ascension of his honour Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-Sádiq, the scion of his honour the Beloved of Martyrs, was divided amongst the heirs; whereas now a part of the heirs, such as Áqá Husayn-i-Mahbúbí, and Áqá Hasan-i-Mahbúbí; the Maidservant of God Qamar Khánum, the spouse of Áqá Husayn-i-Mahbúbí; the Maidservant of God Aqdas Khánum-i-Páyandih; and the Maidservant of God 'Adhrá' Khánum, have each contributed their own share to the Cause; while the Spiritual Assembly has purchased another portion of the share of the heirs -- all these matters were explained and made clear in the refulgent presence of the Guardian. // These great services and illustrious undertakings were highly extolled in the luminous court, while each one of the contributors was privileged and honoured to receive the wondrous expressions of his mercy and love. This present was written at his blessed command. // 19 Shahru'l-Qawl 95, 11 December 1938, Núri'd-Dín Zayn. // Perused, the servant of His Threshold, Shoghi"*

According to one account, the younger brother Áqá Mírzá Ismá'íl, having with the help of Mudarris succeeded in retrieving the pure bodies from under the rubble of the collapsed wall, washed them in the waters of the River Níyásarm, which is among the branches of the Záyandih Rúd, and buried them in Takhtih-Fúlád. There is today in the Hazíratu'l-Quds of Isfahán a stone slab upon which, it is said, the bodies of the twin martyrs were placed for the purpose of this final ablution. The private dwelling of the King of Martyrs and the Beloved of Martyrs is today in the possession of the Cause.

*Reminiscences of their Honours the King of Martyrs and the Beloved of Martyrs*

*Transcribed from Colonel 'Ináyatu'lláh-i-Suhráb's notebook of reminiscences*
