The names of Queen Catherine of Imperial Russia, Hajjáj Ibn-i-Yúsuf-i-Thaqafí, Tamerlane, and the like are recorded and preserved in the history books as examples of the type; yet the cruelties of Zillu's-Sultán were more numerous and grievous than those wrought by any of these. It is related that when, after the martyrdom of the Twin Shining Lights, the prince and Shaykh Báqir fell out over the distribution of their property, Shaykh Báqir set out for Najaf, and, for fear of the prince, forsook Isfahán. During his father's reign, Zillu's-Sultán attempted to do away with him, and to succeed him on the throne of Írán. In this hope, he despatched his special companion Hájí Sayyáh to 'Akká with a message for Bahá'u'lláh, promising that if He would order the Bahá'ís to support him, and if he were in consequence to win the crown, he would grant the Bahá'ís official recognition, and do this and that besides. Hájí Sayyáh arrived in His blessed presence, and delivered the message of Zillu's-Sultán; to which the Blessed Beauty replied: "We have no involvement in political affairs: Our gaze is fixed upon the hearts of men, not upon the governance of lands." When Hájí Sayyáh, whom Bahá'u'lláh has referred to in a Blessed Tablet as "ignorant," relayed this response to Zillu's-Sultán, the prince was wroth, and rose up in opposition to the Cause of God. Amongst other measures, he authorized the martyrdoms of their honours Mullá Kázim-i-Tálkhúnchi'í, and of the King of Martyrs and the Beloved of Martyrs, and inflicted much harm on the friends. The Ancient Beauty -- exalted be His glory -- has set forth these matters in detail in a Blessed Tablet, and referred to Zillu's-Sultán as a "Fading Shadow." Zillu's-Sultán was finally disappointed in his hopes of sovereignty, and incurred the wrath and anger of his father, who removed extensive territories from his control, so that had perforce to content himself with the governorship of Isfahán.

"Fate of the She-Serpent"

Intended by the expression is Mír Siyyid Husayn, the Imám-Jum'ih of Isfahán, who, with the aid of Shaykh Báqir-i-Isfahání, brought about the martyrdom of the Twin Shining Lights. In the Tablet of the Proof the Ancient Beauty -- exalted be His glory -- has styled the Imám-Jum'ih the "She-Serpent," and Shaykh Báqir the "Wolf." An account of the martyrdom of the Twin Shining Lights is provided under the heading "Bloodthirsty." The Wolf and the She-Serpent both suffered the evil consequences of their actions, as has been mentioned in the Divine Tablets; amongst them one in which Bahá'u'lláh says.

*His exalted Utterance:*

Observe thou how in the Land of Sád that malignant monster who was the cause and instigator of the martyrdom of the Twin Shining Lights was overtaken by the direst torment, to the degree that all the members of his household shunned him. *Finis*.

Likewise in another Tablet He has described how Divine chastisement and displeasure descended upon the She-Serpent and the Wolf.

*His Precious Words:*

O Habíb! Rejoice thou, inasmuch as He Who is the Ever-Forgiving hath forgiven thee, and purified thee from all sins. Thy Lord is of a truth the Gracious. He hath visited upon the She-Serpent an afflictive outpouring of his wrath, and left him under the scourges of his own iniquitous and loathsome deed. He, verily, was the foundation of the evil and the root thereof. Ere the requital of the hereafter We subjected him to a recompense here below such that the denizens of the Fire sought refuge therefrom with God, the Almighty, the All-Powerful. The blasts of chastisement assailed him from all sides: this was ere the departure of his spirit; thereafter the avenging angels drove him to the lowest depths of Hell. (*Until He saith, blessed and exalted be He*) We visited the Wolf with an agony inconceivable to all but God, the Lord of the worlds; and this was in the first year after he opposed My Name Há'. Thy Lord, verily, is the Stern Avenger. By My life! No medicine shall heal him, nor aught that is in the kingdom of creation cure him. His anguish shall but grow, not lessen, until he returneth unto his abode: then shall he behold what no description, nor the most discerning reckoner, can depict. Before him We laid hold upon the Chief, through the power of Our might, even as in bygone centuries We have laid hold upon others yet greater than he, and I am the Elucidator, the All-Informed."

There are many Tablets from the Blessed Beauty describing how Divine displeasure descended upon the She-Serpent and the Wolf. In his book *Bihjatu's-Sudúr*, the late Hájí says touching the slayers of the King of Martyrs and the Beloved of Martyrs.

*His words, peace be upon him:*

After the martyrdom of his honour the King of Martyrs -- may my life be a sacrifice for the drops of his and his brother's blood -- which was on account of the property and wealth of those two martyrs in the path of God, the Imám-Jum'ih, having been rendered blind and dumb as a judgment upon him for his wrongful action, fell out with his excellency Zillu's-Sultán, and stirred up the people against the government. It was on the fortieth day after the death of these two wronged martyrs that his excellency sent the Imám-Jum'ih, by constraint and as a captive, out of Isfahán, so fulfilling the purport of the stern admonition sent down to him from the pen of the One in Whose grasp are the reins of all affairs: "Ere long will the breaths of chastisement seize thee from all sides, even as they seized others before thee. Wait thou ...." *Finis*.

His holiness the Guardian of the Cause of God includes in his Centennial Tablet to the American friends a passage to this effect: "Shaykh Muhammad-Báqir, whom His Holiness Bahá'u'lláh has named in the Tablet of the Proof the 'Wolf,' and to whom He has also addressed the rebuke 'Thou art even as the last trace of sunlight upon the mountain-top,' yielded up his life in the utmost wretchedness, misery, regret and remorse. Mír Muhammad-Husayn, styled the 'She-Serpent,' whom the Ancient Beauty has called more merciless and wicked than the oppressor of the Land of Taff, was in those same days banished and exiled from Isfahán; wandered from village to village in search of refuge; and was suddenly afflicted by a malady which caused his body to emit a putrid odour, whose rankness was such that even his wife and daughter would not approach him. In this woeful state he passed away; and since none of the inhabitants of the place dared to bury him, he was finally interred by a few porters." pp. 232 -- 233. *Finis*.

The grave of Mír Muhammad-Husayn the "She-Serpent," the brother of Siyyid Muhammad, the Sultánu'l-'Ulamá', the Imám-Jum'ih, is in a part of Isfahán called "Sar-Qabr Áqá." The year of the Sultánu'l-'Ulamá's death has been given as 1291 A.H. Mír Muhammad-Husayn was the brother of the Imám-Jum'ih, and succeeded him in that post. He passed away as the result of a strange illness in a state of the utmost abasement. It has not been possible to obtain the year of his death, for in the notices of graves and shrines no mention has been made of his grave. When, in order to investigate the matter, recourse was had to his resting-place in the Sar-Qabr Áqá cemetery, no stone was found upon his grave. The allusion in the name "Sar-Qabr Áqá" is to Mír Siyyid Muhammad, the great Imám-Jum'ih, whose grave is situated there -- that is, in a desert place which now goes by his name. After the martyrdom of the Twin Shining Lights, Zillu's-Sultán and the Imám-Jum'ih fell out over the distribution of the property. On several occasions the prince invited the Imám-Jum'ih to a special hunting-ground known as Qámishlú, and, before a host of people who were assembled there with him, and in the presence of the Imám-Jum'ih himself, prompted his personal jester, Mírzá Hasan-i-Santúrí, to make fun of the Imám by mimicking his form and appearance. Finally, a prodigious sore appeared on the Imám's neck, which some say was a cancer; and it was not long thereafter that he ended his earthly existence. In a Tablet revealed from the pen of the Blessed Beauty, and which was included in the preceding, it is clearly stated that the suffering and travail of the She-Serpent began in that same first year after the martyrdom of the Twin Shining Lights; while it is commonly accepted that his death occurred in the second year after the martyrs' death: that is, in the year 1298 A.H.

*************

*****

*Facsimile of a Tablet of 'Abdu'l-Bahá to Khánum Díyá', the daughter of Mírzá Muhammad-Sádiq from his first wife, Sakínih Begum. The text of the Tablet is as follows: "The well-assured Leaf, the Maidservant of God Khánum Díyá', upon her be the Glory of God // He is God // O thou wronged one in the path of Bahá! I read what thou didst write, and grieved for thy sorrowful state. But one should offer thanks that all these afflictions befell in the path of God, and that in affliction thou art the heir of two martyrs and the memorial of two precious souls in the Court of the Majestic Lord. Wherefore be not sad, be not despondent. Thou art of the house of Truth, and a member of the family; thou art at home, and an intimate in the eyes of all the household. Beware lest thou be downcast, or utter sighs and moans. This separation will pass, and -- God willing -- thou shalt enter the circle of those favoured in the Court of God. Be assured thereof. The everlasting world is thine. 'Ayn 'Ayn // Do thou deliver to the beloved of 'Abdu'l-Bahá Áqá Mírzá 'Ináyatu'lláh a loving greeting, and also to the maidservant of God his mother. 'Ayn 'Ayn"*

The Blessed Tablet known as the Epistle to the Son of the Wolf was revealed from the pen of the Ancient Beauty -- exalted be His glory -- for the aforementioned Áqá Najafí, and was delivered to him in an indirect manner by one of the friends of God. It is commonly believed that the original of the Tablet is in the possession of his children. His children and grandchildren live today in Isfahán, where they hold many estates, lands and properties: as the poet said: "He bore the infamy, others the gold." The iniquities and improper behaviour of this man are also a cause of astonishment to people of discernment. There is no doubt that Áqá Najafí was in the habit of consuming alcoholic beverages several times a day. Ardishír Hizárí, who is one of the staunch and steadfast friends, and among the sterling, noble and detached believers of Pársí origin, related to the writer the following: "During the period when Áqá Najafí held sway in the city, I was engaged in Isfahán as an apprentice in the commercial chamber of Arbáb Jamshíd, the well-known Pársí merchant. One day my master instructed me to go to Áqá Najafí, and deliver to him a message by word of mouth. I duly went to him, delivered the message, and received his reply. As I was leaving he called me back, and whispered softly in my ear: 'Tell Arbáb Jamshíd: Áqá Najafí saith that his supply of drink is finished, and that as soon as possible he should procure a replenishment, and despatch it hither.' When I conveyed this message to Arbáb Jamshíd he was annoyed, and indignantly exclaimed, 'Gracious God! How much drink must this fellow consume for it to have gone so quickly! I sent him a crate not three days since, and here he is again saying "My drink is finished"!' With this, my master sent off to Áqá Najafí another crate of drink." In fine, people's property, lives and reputations were entirely in the hands of this man. Yet in nooks and corners there were perspicacious and discerning people who reprehended his deeds and actions, and contrived by diverse means to make him the object of their barbs. It is related that Karím-i-Shírakí, the well-known jester of the Sháh, stayed for a while in Isfahán. Whenever Áqá Najafí went abroad on his ass, people would rush upon him from all sides so as to kiss his hand, and pandemonium would ensue. Some of those who could not reach the prelate would even kiss the hooves and tail of his ass. One day, when Áqá Najafí was mounted on his ass, and the people were as usual rushing upon him in order to kiss his hand, Karím-i-Shírakí drew nigh to the prelate, and, having thrown his arm around the ass's neck, began kissing its head and ears, exclaiming in a loud voice, 'O Áqá! O our refuge! O our pontiff! O Áqá! May I be a sacrifice for thine ass's head!' Áqá Najafí took the meaning of the jibe, but did not betray it upon his countenance. Siyyid Sadru'd-Dín-i-Dirakhshán, who is a learned and knowledgeable Bahá'í of Isfahán, and a member of the Sacred Spiritual Assembly of that city, related to the author on the authority of his illustrious father, the late Hujjatu'l-Islám Áqá Siyyid Javád -- peace be upon him -- that during the period when Áqá Najafí held sway in the city, one of its liberal-minded divines, who was a shrewd observer, feigned madness so that he might express freely whatsoever was in his heart. The name of this man, who was a true Muslim, and averse to the hypocritical displays of the likes of Áqá Najafí, was Áqá Siyyid Hasan. For the most part, he would collect loose leaves of the Qur'án, and miscellaneous papers, and dispose them about himself, inventing for each of them some suitable tale, which he then used as a pretext to reveal his true intentions. It was Áqá Najafí's practice to go each day to the Masjid-i-Sháh in order to give instruction to the students of religious sciences. One day, the aforesaid late Áqá Siyyid Hasan, who was known as Áqáy-i-Masjid-Hakímí, was seated before the entrance of the Masjid-i-Sháh and humming to himself, whilst holding in his hands some pages of the Qur'án. As he was thus engaged, Áqá Najafí appeared in the distance, affording, since a crowd of religious scholars and disciples were following in his rear, a living representation of the Verse "... who, when he heareth the tread of sandals following behind him, waxeth greater in his own esteem than Nimrod." When, upon arriving at the entrance of the mosque, his eyes fell on Áqá Siyyid Hasan-i-Masjid-Hakímí, Áqá Najafí, who was carrying beneath his arm a quantity of books, asked: "Áqá Siyyid Hasan, what dost thou here?" The Siyyid, summoning a smile to his lips, replied: "Áqá! I was reading the Qur'án, and thinking that these Divine Verses must surely have some external object in the world to which they particularly apply. Now the answer to this question hath become clear to me, for the particular object of this Verse, which I was but now reading, hath been palpably realized before my eyes." Áqá Najafí asked, "Which is the Verse whose object hath been made apparent?" Holding the page of the Qur'án before his eyes, Áqá Siyyid Hasan read out: "Those who were charged with the Torah, then bore it not, are like unto an ass laden with books." "Áqá," he continued, "When I saw thee coming with these books beneath thine arm, I beheld the object of this Verse plainly revealed before me." Áqá Najafí preserved his silence and went his way. He also related that one cold winter's day, when Áqá Najafí was performing his ablutions using water from the pool of the Masjid-i-Sháh, which had completely frozen over, Áqá Siyyid Hasan-i-Masjid-Hakímí came up and said: "Áqá! It is bitterly cold!" Áqá Najafí replied: "It is indeed, but in the path of God what issue can be taken with such hardships?" Áqá Siyyid Hasan replied, "If the water of this pool were heated, it would be a boon to those of us who are poor." "How might the water of this pond be heated?" Áqá Najafí inquired. Áqá Siyyid Hasan replied, "Áqá, that might very easily be accomplished." "How?" asked Áqá Najafí. Áqá Siyyid Husayn replied "I know how it can be done." "How?" Áqá Najafí persisted. "Áqá!" the latter replied, "Do thou bid the believers go and break open the grave of thy late father, bring him hither and inter him near this pool. The heat of his grave will then instantly cause the water to boil." The believers laughed, while Áqá Najafí preserved his silence. Many stories of this kind have been related of him.

After the martyrdom of the Twin Shining Lights, the Blessed Tablet of the Proof, addressed to both the Wolf and the She-Serpent, issued from the pen of His Holiness the Most Compassionate, and it is in this work that He bestowed upon them these titles. I provide the text of that Tablet as the next item in this notebook. Another Tablet, bearing the name of the son of the Wolf, issued from the pen of the Blessed Beauty for Áqá Najafí, and was conveyed to him by the friends of Isfahán. That Tablet, which is very extensive, has since been printed and published, and is available to the friends.

*The Blessed Tablet of the Proof*

*Revealed from the Pen of the Ancient Beauty*

*Glorified be His Most Great Name*

He is the Almighty, the Ordainer, the All-Knowing, the All-Wise

The winds of hatred have encompassed the Ark of Bathá (Mecca), by reason of that which the hands of the oppressors have wrought. O Báqir! Thou hast pronounced sentence against them for whom the books of the world have wept, and in whose favour the scriptures of all religions have testified. Thou, who art gone far astray, art indeed wrapt in a thick veil. By God Himself! Thou hast pronounced judgement against them through whom the horizon of faith hath been illumined. Unto this bear witness They Who are the Dawning-Places of Revelation and the Manifestations of the Cause of thy Lord, the Most Merciful, Who have sacrificed Their souls and all that They possessed in His Straight Path. The Faith of God hath cried everywhere, by reason of thy tyranny, and yet thou disportest thyself and art of them that exult. There is no hatred in Mine heart for thee nor for anyone. Every man of learning beholdeth thee, and such as are like thee, engulfed in evident folly. Hadst thou realized that which thou hast done, thou wouldst have cast thyself into the fire, or abandoned thine home and fled into the mountains, or wouldst have groaned until thou hadst returned unto the place destined for thee by Him Who is the Lord of strength and of might. O thou who art even as nothing! Rend thou asunder the veils of idle fancies and vain imaginings, that thou mayest behold the Day-Star of knowledge shining from this resplendent Horizon. Thou hast torn in pieces a remnant of the Prophet Himself, and imagined that thou hadst helped the Faith of God. Thus hath thy soul prompted thee, and thou art truly one of the heedless. Thine act hath consumed the hearts of the Concourse on high, and those of such as have circled round the Cause of God, the Lord of the worlds. The soul of the Chaste One (Fátimih) melted, by reason of thy cruelty, and the inmates of Paradise wept sore in that blessed Spot. // Judge thou fairly, I adjure thee by God. What proof did the Jewish doctors adduce wherewith to condemn Him Who was the Spirit of God (Jesus) when He came unto them with truth? What could have been the evidence produced by the Pharisees and the idolatrous priests to justify their denial of Muhammad, the Apostle of God when He came unto them with a Book that judged between truth and falsehood with a justice which turned into light the darkness of the earth, and enraptured the hearts of such as had known Him? Indeed thou hast produced, in this day, the same proofs which the foolish divines advanced in that age. Unto this testifieth He Who is the King of the realm of grace in this great Prison. Thou hast, truly, walked in their ways, nay, hast surpassed them in their cruelty, and hast deemed thyself to be helping the Faith and defending the Law of God, the All-Knowing, the All-Wise. By Him Who is the Truth! Thine iniquity hath made Gabriel to groan, and hath drawn tears from the Law of God, through which the breezes of justice have been wafted over all who are in heaven and on earth. Hast thou fondly imagined that the judgement thou didst pronounce hath profited thee? Nay, by Him Who is the King of all Names! Unto thy loss testifieth He with Whom is the knowledge of all things as recorded in the Preserved Tablet. When thou didst pen thy judgement, thou wast accused by thy very pen. Unto this doth bear witness the Pen of God, the Most High, in His inaccessible station. // O thou who hast gone astray! Thou hast neither seen Me, nor associated with Me, nor been My companion for the fraction of a moment. How is it, then, that thou hast bidden men to curse Me? Didst thou, in this, follow the promptings of thine own desires, or didst thou obey thy Lord? Produce thou a sign, if thou art one of the truthful. We testify that thou hast cast behind thy back the Law of God, and laid hold on the dictates of thy passions. Nothing, in truth, escapeth His knowledge; He, verily, is the Incomparable, the All-Informed. O heedless one! Hearken unto that which the Merciful hath revealed in the Qur'án: "Say not to every one who meeteth you with a greeting, 'Thou art not a believer.'" Thus hath He decreed in Whose grasp are the kingdoms of Revelation and of creation, if thou be of them that hearken. Thou hast set aside the commandment of God, and clung unto the promptings of thine own desire. Woe, then, unto thee, O careless one that doubtest! If thou deniest Me, by what proof canst thou vindicate the truth of that which thou dost possess? Produce it, then, O thou who hast joined partners with God, and turned aside from His sovereignty that hath encompassed the worlds! // O foolish one! Know thou that he is truly learned who hath acknowledged My Revelation, and drunk from the Ocean of My knowledge, and soared in the atmosphere of My love, and cast away all else besides Me, and taken firm hold on that which hath been sent down from the Kingdom of My wondrous utterance. He, verily, is even as an eye unto mankind, and as the spirit of life unto the body of all creation. Glorified be the All-Merciful Who hath enlightened him, and caused him to arise and serve His great and mighty Cause. Verily, such a man is blessed by the Concourse on high, and by them who dwell within the Tabernacle of Grandeur, who have quaffed My sealed Wine in My Name, the Omnipotent, the All-Powerful. O Báqir! If thou be of them that occupy such a sublime station, produce then a sign from God, the Creator of the heavens. And shouldst thou recognize thy powerlessness, do thou rein in thy passions, and return unto thy Lord, that perchance He may forgive thee thy sins which have caused the leaves of the Divine Lote Tree to be burnt up, and the Rock to cry out, and the eyes of men of understanding to weep. Because of thee the Veil of Divinity was rent asunder, and the Ark foundered, and the She-Camel was hamstrung, and the Spirit (Jesus) groaned in His sublime retreat. Disputest thou with Him Who hath come unto thee with the testimonies of God and His signs which thou possessest and which are in the possession of them that dwell on earth? Open thine eyes that thou mayest behold this Wronged One shining forth above the horizon of the Will of God, the Sovereign, the Truth, the Resplendent. Unstop, then, the ear of thine heart that thou mayest hearken unto the speech of the Divine Lote Tree that hath been raised up in truth by God, the Almighty, the Beneficent. Verily, this Tree, notwithstanding the things that befell it by reason of thy cruelty and of the transgressions of such as are like thee, calleth aloud and summoneth all men unto the Sadratu'l-Muntahá and the Supreme Horizon. Blessed is the soul that hath gazed on the Most Mighty Sign, and the ear that hath heard His most sweet Voice, and woe to whosoever hath turned aside and done wickedly. // O thou who hast turned away from God! Wert thou to look with the eye of fairness upon the Divine Lote Tree, thou wouldst perceive the marks of thy sword on its boughs, and its branches, and its leaves, notwithstanding that God created thee for the purpose of recognizing and of serving it. Reflect, that haply thou mayest recognize thine iniquity and be numbered with such as have repented. Thinkest thou that We fear thy cruelty? Know thou and be well assured that from the first day whereon the voice of the Most Sublime Pen was raised betwixt earth and heaven We offered up Our souls, and Our bodies, and Our sons, and Our possessions in the path of God, the Exalted, the Great, and We glory therein amongst all created things and the Concourse on high. Unto this testify the things which have befallen Us in this Straight Path. By God! Our hearts were consumed, and Our bodies were crucified, and Our blood was spilt, while Our eyes were fixed on the horizon of the loving-kindness of their Lord, the Witness, the All-Seeing. The more grievous their woes, the greater waxed the love of the people of Bahá. Unto their sincerity hath borne witness what the All-Merciful hath sent down in the Qur'án. He saith: "Wish ye, then, for death, if ye are sincere." Who is to be preferred, he that hath sheltered himself behind curtains, or he that hath offered himself in the path of God? Judge thou fairly, and be not of them that rove distraught in the wilderness of falsehood. So carried away have they been by the living waters of the love of the Most Merciful, that neither the arms of the world nor the swords of the nations have deterred them from setting their faces towards the ocean of the bounty of their Lord, the Giver, the Generous. // By God! Troubles have failed to unnerve Me, and the repudiation of the divines hath been powerless to weaken Me. I have spoken, and still speak forth before the face of men: "The door of grace hath been unlocked and He Who is the Dayspring of Justice is come with perspicuous signs and evident testimonies, from God, the Lord of strength and of might!" Present thyself before Me that thou mayest hear the mysteries which were heard by the Son of 'Imrán (Moses) upon the Sinai of Wisdom. Thus commandeth thee He Who is the Dawning-Place of the Revelation of thy Lord, the God of Mercy, from His great Prison. // Hath leadership made thee proud? Peruse thou what God hath revealed to the Sovereign ruler, the Sultán of Turkey, who hath incarcerated Me in this fortified stronghold, so that thou mayest be informed of the condition of this Wronged One, as decreed by God, the One, the Single, the All-Informed. Art thou happy to see the abject and worthless as thy followers? They support thee as did a people before them, they that followed Annas, who, without clear proof and testimony, pronounced judgement against the Spirit. // Peruse thou the Kitáb-i-Íqán and that which the All-Merciful hath sent down unto the King of Paris and to such as are like him, that thou mayest be made aware of the things that have happened in the past, and be persuaded that We have not sought to spread disorder in the land after it had been well-ordered. We exhort, wholly for the sake of God, His servants. Let him who wisheth turn unto Him, and him who wisheth turn aside. Our Lord, the Merciful, is verily the All-Sufficing, the All-Praised. O concourse of divines! This is the day whereon nothing amongst all things, nor any name amongst all names, can profit you save through this Name which God hath made the Manifestation of His Cause and the Dayspring of His Most Excellent Titles unto all who are in the kingdom of creation. Blessed is that man that hath recognized the fragrance of the All-Merciful and been numbered with the steadfast. Your sciences shall not profit you in this day, nor your arts, nor your treasures, nor your glory. Cast them all behind your backs, and set your faces towards the Most Sublime Word through which the Scriptures and the Books and this lucid Tablet have been distinctly set forth. Cast away, O concourse of divines, the things ye have composed with the pens of your idle fancies and vain imaginings. By God! The Day-Star of Knowledge hath shone forth above the horizon of certitude. // O Báqir! Read and call thou to mind that which was said of old by a believer of thy stock: "Will ye slay a man because he saith my Lord is God, when He hath already come to you with signs from your Lord? If he be a liar, on him will be his lie, but if he be a man of truth, part of what he threateneth will fall upon you. In truth God guideth not him who is a transgressor, a liar." // O thou who art gone astray! If thou hast any doubt concerning Our conduct, know thou that We bear witness unto that whereunto God hath Himself borne witness ere the creation of the heavens and of the earth, that there is none other God but Him, the Almighty, the All-Bounteous. We testify that He is One in His Essence, One in His attributes. He hath none to equal Him in the whole universe, nor any partner in all creation. He hath sent forth His Messengers, and sent down His Books, that they may announce unto His creatures the Straight Path. // Hath the Sháh been informed, and chosen to close his eyes to thine acts? Or hath he been seized with fear at the howling of a pack of wolves who have cast the Path of God behind their backs and followed in thy way without any clear proof or Book? We have heard that the provinces of Persia have been adorned with the adornment of justice. When We observed closely, however, We found them to be the dawning-places of tyranny and the daysprings of injustice. We behold justice in the clutches of tyranny. We beseech God to set it free through the power of His might and His sovereignty. He, verily, overshadoweth all that is in the heavens and on earth. To none is given the right to protest against anyone concerning that which hath befallen the Cause of God. It behoveth whosoever hath set his face towards the Most Sublime Horizon to cleave tenaciously unto the cord of patience, and to put his reliance in God, the Help in Peril, the Unconstrained. O ye loved ones of God! Drink your fill from the well-spring of wisdom, and walk ye in the garden of wisdom, and soar ye in the atmosphere of wisdom, and speak forth with wisdom and eloquence. Thus biddeth you your Lord, the Almighty, the All-Knowing. // O Báqir! Rely not on thy glory, and thy power. Thou art even as the last trace of sunlight upon the mountain-top. Soon will it fade away, as decreed by God, the All-Possessing, the Most High. Thy glory and the glory of such as are like thee have been taken away, and this verily is what hath been ordained by the One with Whom is the Mother Tablet. Where is he to be found who contended with God, and whither is gone he that gainsaid His signs, and turned aside from His sovereignty? Where are they who have slain His chosen ones and spilt the blood of His holy ones? Reflect, that haply thou mayest perceive the breaths of thine acts, O foolish doubter! Because of you the Apostle lamented, and the Chaste One cried out, and the countries were laid waste, and darkness fell upon all regions. O concourse of divines! Because of you the people were abased, and the banner of Islam was hauled down, and its mighty throne subverted. Every time a man of discernment hath sought to hold fast unto that which would exalt Islam, ye raised a clamour, and thereby was he deterred from achieving his purpose, while the land remained fallen in clear ruin. // Consider the Sultán of Turkey! He did not want war, but those like you desired it. When its fires were enkindled and its flames rose high, the government and the people were thereby weakened. Unto this beareth witness every man of equity and perception. Its calamities waxed so great that the smoke thereof surrounded the Land of Mystery and its environs, and what had been revealed in the Tablet of the Sultán was made manifest. Thus hath it been decreed in the Book, at the behest of God, the Help in Peril, the Self-Subsisting. "Verily, we are God's, and unto Him shall we return." // O My Supreme Pen! Leave Thou the mention of the Wolf, and call Thou to remembrance the She-Serpent whose cruelty hath caused all created things to groan, and the limbs of the holy ones to quake. Thus biddeth Thee the Lord of all names, in this glorious station. The Chaste One hath cried out by reason of thine iniquity, and yet thou dost imagine thyself to be of the family of the Apostle of God! Thus hath thy soul prompted thee, O thou who hast withdrawn thyself from God, the Lord of all that hath been and shall be. Judge thou equitably, O She-Serpent! For what crime didst thou sting the children of the Apostle of God, and pillage their possessions? Hast thou denied Him Who created thee by His command "be, and it was"? Thou hast dealt with the children of the Apostle of God as neither 'Ád hath dealt with Húd, nor Thamúd with Sálih, nor the Jews with the Spirit of God, the Lord of all being. Gainsayest thou the signs of thy Lord which no sooner were sent down from the heaven of His Cause than all the books of the world bowed down before them? Meditate, that thou mayest be made aware of thine act, O heedless outcast! Ere long will the breaths of chastisement seize thee, as they seized others before thee. Wait, O thou who hast joined partners with God, the Lord of the visible and the invisible. This is the day which God hath announced through the tongue of His Apostle. Reflect, that thou mayest apprehend what the All-Merciful hath sent down in the Qur'án and in this inscribed Tablet. This is the day whereon He Who is the Dayspring of Revelation hath come with clear tokens which none can number. This is the day whereon every man endued with perception hath discovered the fragrance of the breeze of the All-Merciful in the world of creation, and every man of insight hath hastened unto the living waters of the mercy of His Lord, the King of Kings. O heedless one! The tale of the Sacrifice (Ishmael) hath been retold, and he who was to be offered up hath directed his steps towards the place of sacrifice, and returned not, by reason of that which thy hand hath wrought, O perverse hater! Didst thou imagine that martyrdom could abase this Cause? Nay, by Him Whom God hath made to be the Repository of His Revelation, if thou be of them that comprehend. Woe betide thee, O thou who hast joined partners with God, and woe betide them that have taken thee as their leader, without a clear token or a perspicuous Book. How numerous the oppressors before thee who have arisen to quench the light of God, and how many the impious who murdered and pillaged until the hearts and souls of men groaned by reason of their cruelty! The sun of justice hath been obscured, inasmuch as the embodiment of tyranny hath been stablished upon the throne of hatred, and yet the people understand not. The children of the Apostle have been slain and their possessions pillaged. Say: Was it, in thine estimation, their possessions or themselves that denied God? Judge fairly, O ignorant one that hath been shut out as by a veil from God. Thou hast clung to tyranny and cast away justice; whereupon all created things have lamented, and still thou art among the wayward. Thou hast put to death the aged, and plundered the young. Thinkest thou that thou wilt consume that which thine iniquity hath amassed? Nay, by Myself! Thus informeth thee He Who is cognizant of all. By God! The things thou possessest shall profit thee not, nor what thou hast laid up through thy cruelty. Unto this beareth witness thy Lord, the All-Knowing. Thou hast arisen to put out the light of this Cause; ere long will thine own fire be quenched, at His behest. He, verily, is the Lord of strength and of might. The changes and chances of the world, and the powers of the nations, cannot frustrate Him. He doeth what He pleaseth, and ordaineth what He willeth through the power of His sovereignty. Consider the she-camel. Though but a beast, yet hath the All-Merciful exalted her to so high a station that the tongues of the earth made mention of her and celebrated her praise. He, verily, overshadoweth all that is in the heavens and on earth. No God is there but Him, the Almighty, the Great. Thus have We adorned the heaven of Our Tablet with the suns of Our words. Blessed the man that hath attained thereunto and been illumined with their light, and woe betide such as have turned aside, and denied Him, and strayed far from Him. Praised be God, the Lord of the worlds.

Section Nine

What the Early Believers

And Those Informed of the Matter

Have Written Concerning the Martyrdom of the

Twin Shining Lights

*A portion of the Treatise*

*Of the Late Abu'l-Fadá'il-i-Gulpáygání Describing the Martyrdom of the*

*Twin Shining Lights*

The late Abu'l-Fadá'il Muhammad Ibn-i-Muhammad-Ridáy-i-Gulpáygání, known as "Abu'l-Fadl," has provided, in a treatise he wrote in answer to one who had inquired concerning the attempt of the Bábís on the life of the Sháh, a description of the martyrdom of the Twin Shining Lights. A portion of his treatise, taken from the notebook of Áqáy-i-Fadlu'lláh-i-Shahídí (the son of his honour Ustád 'Alí-Akbar-i-Mi'már-i-Yazdí, who was martyred in 1321 A.H. in Yazd), is now provided.

*His words, upon him be 9:*

Áqá Mírzá Hasan-i-Isfahání, who was a fruitful bough of the 'Alawite tree, was around the time of his martyrdom seeking repayment of a sum of nearly eighteen thousand túmáns owed him by Mír Muhammad-Husayn, the Imám-Jum'ih of Isfahán. The inner baseness and depraved character of the latter induced him, however, in repayment of the Mírzá's favours, to seek rather to compass the death of that noble Siyyid; and to this end he thought long and hard, and trod in his mind many tortuous and dusty byways, in order to determine how to put his scheme into effect. One day he consulted with Shaykh Báqir in the public bath, and the outcome of the just deliberations of these reverend Shí'ite clerics was that they should seek, through the lure of the Mírzá's wealth, to awaken the avarice of his excellency Zillu's-Sultán, and by this means make him a party to their scheme. Thus it was that the agents of the government and the representatives of the Sharí'ah colluded and collaborated in the Mírzá's death. When on the seventeenth of Rabí'u'l-Avval, which is the anniversary of the Prophet's birthday, the three brothers -- Mírzá Muhammad-Husayn, Mírzá Muhammad-Hasan, and Mírzá Ismá'íl -- paid a courtesy call on the Imám-Jum'ih in his home, as is customary on feast days, that ill-conditioned, hard-hearted scoundrel, judging the moment to be propitious to his design, gave orders for the two illustrious brothers to be arrested; and, having sent after the Mírzá, who was not then present, had him arrested also, and despatched those three peerless siblings to his excellency Zillu's-Sultán, who likewise ordered them to be imprisoned. During the days of their captivity, he several times summoned the Mírzá and spoke with him. On one such occasion he addressed him thus: "The Imám-Jum'ih and the rest of the 'ulamá' are aggrieved that thou hast embraced the new Cause." The Mírzá replied: "In sooth, 'tis true; but the cause of the Imám-Jum'ih's enmity towards me is rather that for several years I provided his and his family's food and clothing expenses, for the which he is indebted to me: and since I have in these days requested repayment of my debt, I have become afflicted with his enmity." The prince replied: "Granted that this is true, do thou nevertheless renounce this faith, and curse the leaders of this religion." The Mírzá remained silent, whereupon Zillu's-Sultán, taking the matter to an extreme, asserted: "By the salt of his most exalted royal majesty I swear, and by the pure soul of the Commander of the Faithful I vow: if thou wilt pronounce this curse, there is no respect in which I shall stint thee my succour and support. I shall recover thy due from the Imám-Jum'ih, and see to it that thine enemies are confounded and frustrated." Again that embodiment of steadfastness gave no reply. Finally, the prince conjured him: "By my life, I charge thee to curse them!" Yet still his words had no effect. Seeing how the matter lay, the prince waxed wroth, and asked: "Why then wilt thou not curse them?" The Mírzá replied: "Were his excellency to know what I know, he would not have laid upon me such a charge." Upon hearing these words, an extraordinary change became apparent in his temper; he was seized with great ire, and the fire of savagery and the heat of passion so blazed within him that he forsook his natural state, and his countenance turned a livid hue. He several times reached for his sword and half withdrew it from its sheath. Finally he took up a riding-switch and so plied it about the head and face of that mountain of steadfastness that his blessed features were injured. In the same interrogation Mírzá Muhammad-Husayn also refused to renounce his faith through execration. Tenderness of years and youth prevented Mírzá Ismá'íl however from emulating his two happy-starred brothers; and, by renouncing the true Faith, he secured his release and freedom. The Imám-Jum'ih and others became now apprehensive lest the Mírzá's holy lineage or personal wealth should operate to ensure his safety and deliverance. In order, therefore, that they might complete this business, they resolved once more to take counsel together, and, in the meeting subsequently convened, agreed after consultation to submit a report of the matter to his highness the Sháh, and to obtain at the same time permission to put the Mírzá to death. They accordingly despatched a telegram to Hájí Mullá 'Alíy-i-Kandí, who is today the leading cleric in Tihrán, requesting him to represent to his most exalted royal majesty that the divines of Isfahán, in token of their allegiance to his most exalted imperial majesty, had apprehended and imprisoned two Bábís, and entreated that he grant them leave to put them to death, so that they might through this service be accounted among those who demonstrated their allegiance to the crown in very fact. When this telegram reached the Sháh, he ordered his excellency Zillu's-Sultán to send them in chains to Tihrán. After reading this decree, Zillu's-Sultán made no haste to have the Mírzá put to death. The Imám-Jum'ih and Shaykh Báqir knew however that the Sháh would not wish to be involved in the death of those two innocents, and that their journey to the capital city would without doubt result in their release. Wherefore, having for a third time taken counsel together, they jointly resolved to carry the matter through by a general insurrection. Accordingly, on 23 of Rabí'u'l-Avval 1296, they first ordered the different guilds of traders in the bazaar to shut up shop; then the divines, such as the Imám-Jum'ih; Shaykh Báqir; Hájí Mírzá Háshim, the son of the previous Imám-Jum'ih; Mírzá 'Abdu'l-Javád, the son of Áqá Muhammad-Mihdíy-i-Kalbásí; Áqá Siyyid 'Alíy-i-Burújirdí, and other learned doctors of Isfahán -- more than fifty all told -- each one mounted upon his steed, and accompanied by a huge crowd of ne'er-do-wells and insurrectionaries, tradespeople and shopkeepers from the bazaar, made their way to the government citadel. With cries of "Woe to the Faith!" "Woe to the Sharí'ah!," they stirred up a prodigious tumult in the city, to the degree that a reliable authority has related that their savage cries could be heard even as far away as Qal'iy-i-Gaz, three farsangs distant from Isfahán. His excellency Zillu's-Sultán, who was at this time in the bath, was perturbed by the people's clamour; and upon inquiring who they were, and what had caused them to congregate and assemble in this manner, was informed, "It is the 'ulamá and their followers. They are saying: 'We can no longer exercise patience and rest quiet in the matter of the Mírzá: we entreat his excellency to have him put to death, for otherwise we shall ourselves carry through this business by main force.'" Emerging from the bath, his excellency Zillu's-Sultán sent for the divines, who, after coming into his presence, presented to him their demand. The prince advised them: "These have committed naught prejudicial or treasonable to the state. Your argument with them is over a religious and a confessional matter; whereas I am not concerned with matters connected to the Faith. Moreover, his most exalted royal majesty hath commanded me to despatch them to Tihrán. Consequently I am unable to pass upon them the sentence of death." The divines then said: "We will issue their death sentence, and ourselves answer to the Sháh for the proceeding;" to which the prince replied: "I will not delegate to my executioner the task of carrying out your sentence." A few of the 'ulamá', among them Shaykh Muhammad-Taqí, the son of Shaykh Báqir, known as Shaykh Najafí, now rolled up their sleeves in the assembly, exclaiming, "We ourselves shall with our own hands put them to death." When the prince saw how bold and intrepid were these stony-hearted petitioners in demanding the innocents' blood, he said, "In that case, do ye write down their death sentence upon a sheet of paper, and deposit it with me, that it may be my warrant." The 'ulamá' accordingly wrote out as directed a document importing the necessity of having the Mírzá put to death, and some sixty people, more or less, all of whom were present and assembled in that meeting, affixed their seal and testimony to the deed, and sent it to the prince. When the prince set eyes upon that document, he ordered that the foundation of loftiness and glory be felled by the axe of tyranny, and the fruitful sapling of nobility and honour be cut down by the sword of oppression and enmity. Once the order had been issued for the execution of those twin shining stars of the heaven of holy lineage, the demon-hearted executioners brought them forth from their place of confinement, and beside the "Tálár-i-Tavílih" building, which is a well-known edifice of Isfahán, and a monument of the Safavid monarchy, spilt their blood, which was the very spirit of purity and the soul of virtue, upon the ground. After their martyrdom, a cord was tied about their feet, and their pure bodies were dragged to the foot of the gallows. The prince ordered a number of farráshes to keep watch around their corses, and to prevent the people from throwing stones at them and from perpetrating other indignities they had been seen committing. Those two pure temples remained that day until the evening fallen upon the dust at the foot of the gallows. At this hour, Shaykh Báqir ordered the bodies to be placed beneath an arch near the Níyásarm river, and the arch to be knocked down over them. Thereafter, his excellency Zillu's-Sultán, having summoned Mírzá Ismá'íl to appear before him, ordered him to bury those two pure bodies, and the latter accordingly laid them to rest in the Takhtih-Púlád cemetery. *Finis*.

***********

**

The Account of the Late

Mírzá Asadu'lláh

Fádil-i-Mázindarání

Concerning

The Martyrdom of the

Twin Shining Lights

*An Account of the Martyrdom of the Twin Shining Lights*

*By the Pen of the Late Mírzá Asadu'lláh Fádil-i-Mázindarání*

The two famed martyrs, alike of the spirit and of the flesh, were descendants of the pure prophetic line, and beauteous and fragrant blossoms of the garden of Ahmad. They were named after their holy Ancestor's two martyred grandsons, Hasan and Husayn, and were born, as they resided, in Isfahán, one of the magnificent cities of Írán. In honour, glory and wealth they were distinguished above their peers; while in the firmament of the Cause of His Holiness Bahá'u'lláh they were as twin shining stars. During the years 1844 to 1845, which witnessed the first breaking of the dawn of Truth, the heaven of this family was illumined by the supernal lights, and its members were dignified and honoured with the raiments of Divine guidance. In 1846 when, as a result of the clamour raised by the Muslim divines, and the ensuing governmental decree, His Holiness the Báb forsook his native town of Shíráz for Isfahán, and those in that city who panted after the fount of knowledge, and earnestly sought for religious truth, had the bounty of meeting Him, and of satisfying their thirst, their esteemed father, Mírzá Ibráhím, invited Him one night to his home, and illumined his humble abode with the lights of his august Visitor's presence. That night the showers of the clouds of bounty and bestowal rained down without pause, and the choicest delicacies of His heavenly table continually descended. From thenceforth the lights of guidance by degrees completely irradiated the spiritual horizons of that family, so that they became renowned for their affiliation to this Cause, and grew in the Divine garden into a lofty palm and a fruitful tree. When His Holiness Bahá'u'lláh, pursuant to the Iranian government's decree of banishment, was exiled from that land, and in 1852 His blessed train arrived with kingly splendour in Baghdád; and when, for a period of twelve years, that city became a dawning-place of the lights of guidance, a centre for the diffusion of truth, and a resort for religionists and philosophers, those two brothers journeyed thither on one occasion from Isfahán, and for a few hours entered into His holy presence, attained the paradise of nearness and reunion; and quaffed from the sweet palatable stream flowing from the Divine fountain. They became inebriated with the soul-reviving wine of the love of God; obtained all they wished for; and realized their highest hopes. Of the Source of grace and bounty they besought that they might scale the heights of glory and wealth; and their petition was honoured with acceptance and assent. When they returned to Isfahán, they each, like royal falcons, soared up on twin mighty pinions to the very apex of earthly and heavenly advancement. They were seen to have become a "new creation," and a wondrous spirit shone effulgently in their holy temples. Their faces betokened gladness at the gifts and favours of God, and their hearts were a mirror of the lights of the All-Glorious. They had each a countenance bright and shining, and a character like unto a rose-garden. A sweet tongue and an eloquent and grateful utterance were theirs; and a treasury of mind and heart filled with the precious gems of knowledge and spiritual sensibilities, so that they were rich in the plenty of God, and thereby able to dispense with all save Him. Yet with all this, they became celebrated too for their material prosperity; and in consequence of the commerce in which they were engaged, wealth and riches poured in continually and ceaselessly from all sides, whence they acquired rank, glory and great affluence; their business operations extended far and wide; and their trade relations with other cities were uninterrupted. They were turned to as authorities on important civic matters, while people of stature and eminence, whether influential divines, wealthy merchants or dignitaries of state, associated and entered into dealings with them, looked to them for help and support, and held them in esteem. At the same time, they were a shelter for the poor, and a refuge for the orphaned and widowed. They were a rescuer of the fallen, and a boundless treasure to the abject and destitute. They were a healer of the sick, and a harbourer of those without victuals and provisions for their journey. They disappointed no petitioner, and were a source of hope unto the despondent. The door of their bounty and generosity stood open before all, to the degree that in the year when Isfahán was stricken with famine, these two brothers, through dispensing money, storing up supplies, and giving charitably to all, saved the people from starvation, and delivered them from perdition. Although in that era the fanatical outcry of the people against the Bahá'ís was exceeding fierce -- the influential Muslim divines issued sentences declaring them heretics and condemning them to death, sentences which the government of the time, whether willingly or not, carried into effect, so that each day the world would hear of new forms and varieties of slaughter, rapine and incarceration directed against the Bahá'ís, especially in Isfahán, which occupied in this respect a foremost rank among the cities of Írán, inasmuch as a very wealthy and hugely influential Muslim divine of the city had on numerous occasions, whether by edict or incitement, caused the death of many members of this community, and even as a wolf, which steals off the inoffensive lambs one by one, then stains its claws and teeth with their blood, had spilt the blood of a host of innocent victims, whence he was designated the "Wolf" (and now he and his son, who succeeded to his position, are famed by the appellations "Wolf" and "Son of the Wolf," and a long and weighty epistle wherein Bahá'u'lláh rebuked him has become known throughout the world under the title "The Book of the Shaykh," or the "Epistle to the Son of the Wolf"); the government of Isfahán was moreover under the wilful sway of a despotic, oppressive, voluptuous, and covetous prince, who executed many people without cause, and stole incalculable wealth, a man who seemed to derive peculiar pleasure from shedding the blood of the oppressed, and visiting cruelty upon the innocent (and were the full tale of the oppression and cruelty of this divine and governor to be set forth, it would of itself fill up a thick and bulky volume) -- *at such a time as this,* the aforementioned two brothers kept the door of their home open to the generality of the believers, nor ever for a moment were remiss in discharging their Bahá'í obligations, or had any fear or apprehension of anyone. Nay rather, in that city they were the standard-bearers of this Blessed Cause, and the defenders of the victims of oppression; and whenever the enemy launched an attack or assault against any of these wronged ones, they would contrive by various means to protect them, and strive to ensure their safety. Their home was moreover accounted a place of resort for Bahá'í travellers, whether arriving or departing, and for the holding of formal assemblies and gatherings. They had absolutely no fear or trepidation of the enmity of the divines, or of the possibility of a general onslaught of the people, and were so inebriated with the wine of love and faith that they were entirely oblivious of self. They seemed not to be walking on earth, but to be soaring in a different and limitless world, as is indeed attested by a petition they submitted to His Holiness Bahá'u'lláh during the days of His sojourn in Baghdád, wherein they expressed their wish for martyrdom, and for the shedding of their blood, so that the tree of the Cause of God might grow, and they themselves ascend unto the Divine Kingdom. Truth to say, in the world of the soul and the heart they had already, when they made this request, arrived at the station of true martyrdom -- that is, of detachment from their own wishes and desires, and selflessness in the Will of the True One. In brief, matters continued in this wise for a period, until, in 1879, there occurred the fearful and extraordinary event of their martyrdom, the manner of which is as follows. There was in that same city of Isfahán another hugely influential Muslim leader whom the autocratic and all-powerful governor viewed with respect. This cleric, designated by Bahá'u'lláh as the "She-Serpent" ("An adornéd serpent filled with venom") had dealings with the two brothers, and his property was entrusted to their supervision. They had gradually lent him considerable sums of money, amounting perhaps to as much as twenty thousand dollars, and in the stated year, requested of him repayment of these debts. At this, a satanic notion came into the cleric's head, and he determined, by accusing them of affiliation to the Cause of God, to make away with them; and, so far from paying back the aforesaid debts, wrongfully to lay hands on their vast wealth, and to appropriate it to himself. Accordingly he broached in private his satanic notion with the aforesaid wolfish Shaykh, and the latter, by revealing his own unmitigated enmity and bloodthirstiness of disposition, satisfied him that he might rely upon his concurrence and collaboration. With a single purpose therefore they determined to put this dastardly deed into effect. Together, they met with the prince-governor; removed the seal from this hidden secret; and represented to him that the said brothers, on account of the importance of their station, their widespread popularity, and their excellent parts, would gradually attract and allure the people to the Cause of His Holiness Bahá'u'lláh, and so bring about the demise of Islám. "Wherefore," they impressed upon him, "It is incumbent upon thee to take measures so that we may eradicate and suppress them." The prince at first demurred, seeking by various pretexts to excuse himself from the course proposed. "In the first place," he said, "No one hath witnessed any improper action on their part; rather are they adorned with virtues and actions well-pleasing alike to God and to man. For what crime, then, should they suffer torment?" They replied: "There is no greater crime than to depart from the fold of Islám, and enter under the shadow of a new Cause." "In the second place," the prince continued, "They are children of His Holiness Muhammad, whence a responsibility is entailed before the Court of God." Again they replied: "We are to answer for it, and the burden thereof is upon our necks, inasmuch as we are the real perpetrators." The She-Serpent, whose heart was impatient and restless for their death, placed his hand upon his neck as if to say, "I shall bear this burden." "In the third place," the prince persisted, "They have dealings with merchants throughout the land, and this course will undoubtedly involve them in loss, and provoke from them loud cries of protest. The Sháh too may call me to account." To this however they made reply, "We take full responsibility for the proceeding, and shall undertake in writing ourselves to answer to the traders and to the Sháh." So saying, they wrote out with their own hand a document, and deposited it with the prince, tempting him at the same time with the assurance that a bountiful share of the victims' vast wealth would fall to his lot. Thus it was that the prince too became their accessary and confidant, and they determined to prosecute the business together, resolving for the time being to keep it secret, so that none should become apprised of it in advance. So the matter stood until the anniversary of the birth of His Holiness Muhammad -- a regular feast day, on which it is customary for people to pay courtesy visits to the governor and to the divines. Among the great company of people from different ranks of society who assembled in the home of the She-Serpent on that day, a body of the prince-governor's henchmen were also present. These awaited but the opportunity to put into effect a preconcerted plan, which was that when, in accordance with the aforesaid custom, the two brothers came to pay their compliments to the divine, they were first rudely to accost, and then to arrest, them. It chanced that the elder of the two brothers, the Beloved of Martyrs, arrived in the assembly with their younger brother, and while they were seated and occupied with drinking tea, the She-Serpent began fiercely to berate them, saying, "Ye are Bahá'ís, infidels, unclean, and misleaders of the Muslims." By his order, they were then set upon, and the cups of tea taken from their hands, and cast away; after which the prince's farráshes dragged them in the most demeaning manner into their master's presence. The She-Serpent, being uninformed of the whereabouts of the illustrious middle brother, the King of Martyrs, now detailed a company to go in search of him and seek him out; and when at length word arrived that the object of their quest was in the home of one of the well-known divines of the city, he sent thither a company of his own henchmen to arrest him. The divine in question, however, bore the utmost love towards the King of Martyrs; was well pleased alike by his thoughts, his manners and his attainments; and observed towards him the utmost respect and consideration. The King of Martyrs had moreover informed him upon his arrival that a fearful event would that day befall, occasioned by the scheming of the She-Serpent, who, purely out of covetousness for his wealth, had taken decisive measures to compass his death. His host, emboldened by his family connection with the prince, had in response offered him a refuge in his home; and was declaring with the utmost assurance that he would inform the prince of the She-Serpent's scheme, nor allow aught untoward to take place, when of a sudden the tumult of the She-Serpent's henchmen who had come in search of him reached their ears. The divine charged his servants to refuse their demand, and they accordingly returned to the She-Serpent, and explained to him what had happened. He in turn relayed this information to the prince, insisting that the recusant divine should be admonished. The prince forthwith despatched to the latter's house one of his most important and redoubtable functionaries, accompanied by more than a hundred pages, with strict orders to arrest the King of Martyrs; and at the same time directed to the divine a letter in which he warned him to desist from extending his protection, inasmuch as to oppose the determination and pleasure of such influential clerics would entail the most baneful consequences. When these rough and overbearing officials descended upon that house, and the divine saw how matters stood, he perforce with the utmost sorrow and regret apprised the King of Martyrs of the turn events had taken. The latter, with no trace of sorrow or vexation, but evincing rather perfect contentment, at once hied forth from his refuge, and hastened unto the arena of sacrifice in the path of love. When he emerged, that group of officials encompassed him on all sides and hauled him before the governor as though he were a dangerous felon. In this woeful and piteous condition, as he was being thus borne along, the mass of the people, coming out into the streets and squares, bit their fingers in token of bewilderment, and chastened wonder at the inconstancy of fortune, whilst a few among them wept. He, however, showed forth such joy and thankfulness that one might have thought him to be on his way to a festive and gladsome celebration. When they drew nigh the imposing government building, he bestowed, in front of the assembled throng, his watch and a few items of his precious raiment as a bounty upon that same functionary of the prince, and begged his pardon, for, as he explained, "At present this is all I have to give." Upon entering the presence-chamber of the prince-governor, he found his brothers already there before him. The moment he arrived, the governor began to expostulate with him, and fell to reproving and maligning his religion, his creed and his belief. To this, however, he replied with the utmost courtesy: "Excellency, wert thou too to know what I know, never wouldst thou allow such unbefitting language, and such improper expressions, to pass thy lips." At this the prince was exceeding wrathful, and with violent cuffs and swipes of a cane, struck and injured his hands, head, face and body, sternly charging him to renounce his belief in the Cause of God, and to speak of it in unseemly terms. His victim however preserved his silence, thereby only further inflaming the prince's ire. The aforementioned divine who bore love towards him was also present, and when he saw that from excess of thirst the tongue of the King of Martyrs clave to his palate, he ordered water to be brought. The latter took the water, and with perfect decorum sated his thirst. In the utmost bewilderment and amazement, the prince bespake the divine: "'Tis wonderful: even under such circumstances he forgoeth not the courtesy and good manners which are his badge!" At all events, when he observed how firm was the foundation of the brothers' faith, and how steadfast they were in their belief and conviction, with unbridled anger he ordered that they be consigned to prison, and there held fast in heavy chains and fetters. In the course of executing this order, his rapacious farráshes stripped them of their raiment. The She-Serpent for his part despatched a party of his minions to ransack their home. These, having brutally herded their wives, family and children -- who in the extremity of terror were trembling and weeping -- into a corner, and set ruffians to watch over them, kept them pent up, hungry and parched, while they looted and plundered whatever money, jewels, and furnishings the house contained. They then proceeded to their commercial chamber, and likewise bore off all they came across. The She-Serpent also sent someone to the prison, who confiscated any documents and instruments he discovered in the folds of the brothers' clothing; and he similarly seized from the bazaar all records of claims they had upon money-changers and others. He even impounded on that day a camel train of theirs, which he appropriated to himself. During the period of their confinement, merchants throughout the land sent numerous telegrams to the prince-governor advising him that if harm were inflicted upon them it would entail financial loss, and lead to a wholesale weakening of trade. It was bruited that the Sháh too sent a telegram to the prince, instructing him to despatch the captives to Tihrán. But the lust of Mammon, and dread of the consequences of crossing the Wolf and the She-Serpent, eventually decided him instead to expedite their execution, and to place the responsibility for it entirely upon the shoulders of the two divines, only advising receipt of the telegram after the deed was done. Although during the said period he several times urged the two distinguished brothers to renounce their allegiance to the Cause of God, and so ensure their safety and deliverance, representing to them, "Say but this, 'We are not Bahá'ís,' and it shall be sufficient;" while on each occasion those two prodigies of steadfastness raised the cry of "Yá Bahá'u'l-Abhá!," and in resonant tones proclaimed, "Our highest wish is to sacrifice our lives for the love of the Manifestation of Truth, and to spread His Divine teachings;" so that finally he was content to take from them an exorbitant sum, and to overlook the demand that they either renounce their faith or face execution; and even received part of the sum agreed upon, which amounted to nearly thirteen thousand dollars, and granted the youngest brother Mírzá Ismá'íl permission to go forth from the prison in order to procure the remaining sums -- *yet the Wolf and the She-Serpent,* who were in the utmost haste to have them put to death, convened on the seventh day of their confinement a meeting in the presence of the prince, to which they invited a host of learned jurisprudents, and there urgently pressed him to carry through his original intention; unreservedly took upon themselves whatever responsibility, earthly or heavenly, might be involved; and signed and sealed the sentence condemning those two noble souls to death. "Ye then are the slayers," the prince announced; and with this he bade his swordsman carry out whatever the two oppressors might demand, while these in turn bade him expedite the execution. The executioner, his blade unsheathed, entered the prison just as the illustrious elder was addressing to his younger brother the following words of counsel: "O my brother! It is I whose death these oppressors principally seek. If thou but say, 'The Bahá'í is my brother, and not myself,' thou shalt be saved; and after my death thou shalt maintain and care for my poor wife, family and little ones;" to which the latter replied, "O my brother! The One True Beloved will Himself look after our survivors in the best possible manner; whereas the requirement of fidelity is that we two brothers should be together in all the worlds of God." At first the swordsman, who had for years been regaled at the table of their bounty and generosity, besought their pardon, and begged them, "Speak but the word of denial so that ye may be saved, and I for my part spared the need to commit so deplorable a deed." "Thou art under orders," the King of Martyrs addressed him, "Go therefore about thy business." That sanguinary tyrant accordingly led out the great-hearted pair to the scene of execution, where he firmly blindfolded them. As soon as he wished to commence his work, however, each of the twain strove to be the first to suffer martyrdom, and to hasten from this world ahead of his companion, while the other resisted his demands; and so their altercation continued for well nigh an hour. The Wolf and the She-Serpent, who, pacing together on a place of vantage, were complacently and expectantly engaged in converse, gazing the while upon this affecting spectacle, determinedly signalled to the swordsman forthwith to perform his duty and complete his task. Accordingly, while the two brothers, with their arms about each other's necks, were exclaiming "Yá Bahá'u'l-Abhá!," and yielding thanks unto God, he smote off their heads, and their blessed bodies fell to the earth, and became covered in gore and dust. A cord was then affixed to their feet, and they were dragged over the ground to the Maydán-i-Sháh, where they were deposited for the mass of the people to stare upon. Later, they were dragged from thence, and set in a waste spot beneath a massive and ruined wall, which was then knocked down over them so that their bodies were contused and crushed together by its weight, and concealed from view. In the dead of night, however, their younger brother, Mírzá Ismá'íl, accompanied by several of his servants, extricated those precious bodies from beneath the earth, debris and rubble, and bore them off to a spot beside the Záyandih Rúd outside the city, where he washed and shrouded them. In order to excavate a grave for them, he had brought with him also a party of workmen, and these, while expressing apprehension and alarm lest anyone should notice them, and the matter come to the attention of the Wolf and the She-Serpent, nevertheless dug two separate graves, interred the two corses in that spot, and, still in a state of the utmost fear and perturbation, returned to their homes. In their blindness of heart, the oppressors, especially the wolfish Shaykh, imagined that by slaying these two distinguished souls they would be able to extirpate the Cause of God; and that by uprooting these twin fruitful trees, no trace or sign of the Divine orchard would remain. In the wake of this incident spies were set about to the end that if any Bahá'í were discovered to have entered the city, or if any meeting or gathering, or other appearance or manifestation of activity, were observed, all those involved would be put to death. Emphatic instructions were likewise issued to the clerics and others in the madrasihs to be on their guard; while a search and investigation were instituted to locate the burial place of the twin martyrs, so that their bodies might be disinterred. Yet despite such measures, the patience and fortitude of these two distinguished souls in bearing calamity, and in renouncing wealth and family for the sake of the Almighty; and withal their acceptance of this trial with contentment, and of their own free will, became a consummate testimony to the power and pervasive majesty of this Cause. In particular, the grandeur and consequence of their stations, whether in the social or the moral spheres, as well as their piety, virtuous character and detachment, made plain and evident the importance, and the potent influence, of this Mighty Cause in man's spiritual education, whilst exposing before high and low alike the evil character, the tyranny, and the inhuman selfishness of those in authority. In truth, the effect of this supreme calamity was not felt by the Bahá'ís alone, for many devoted adherents of other religions too were plunged into grief and mourning, and composed on the subject verses such as the following:

Methinks in heaven's quiver was not another shaft than this.

Not long thereafter the She-Serpent and the prince fell out, and the She-Serpent took flight and disappeared. Then, on that very part of his neck where he had clapped his hand, so assuming the whole responsibility for their death, a boil broke out, which became so cankered and infected that all his relatives shrank from him in abhorrence, and, removing him from the house, placed him in a garden without the city, where at length he died of this same ailment. The wolfish Shaykh and the son of the Wolf were likewise abased and humiliated, and, like fruitless trees, became otiose and unproductive. The prince for his part was after a while rejected and ostracized by the Sháh, and sank to the lowest level of abasement and ignominy. Finally he was rejected by the whole populace of Írán, and compelled to live outside the realm. On one occasion he returned to Írán, intending to enjoy peaceably for a time in his native land the vast wealth he had accumulated by whatever means he pleased; but the people would not gratify this wish; held him captive; and treated him in the harshest and most humiliating manner. At this juncture it is pertinent to relate the following story. An oppressed Bahá'í of Isfahán, who at the time of the tyrannical government of this same prince suffered under the scourge of the rapacious clerics, and was wont, on account of the fear and danger in which he dwelt, to hide in nooks and corners, appealed to him on one occasion, invoking the ardent sighs of those whose hearts had been melted by oppression, and the moans and lamentations of those without a refuge to flee to; and reminding him of his responsibility before the Divine Threshold; in reply to which the latter, with the utmost arrogance and derision, reproached him, saying: "Go, take thy plaint of me to 'Abdu'l-Bahá, so that He may deal with me as He is able." It was above all this taunting reproach which seared the chagrined heart of that oppressed believer. Years later when, condemned and ostracized, the prince was held captive by the people on the borders of the realm, prohibited from entering his native land, and living in the utmost abasement and humiliation, this same believer journeyed thither from his distant abode, and, having met him, and introduced himself, reminded him of their previous encounter, saying: "In such and such a year when I complained to thee of the injustices and oppression I was suffering, telling thee that all my property had been plundered; that I had no place where I could be safe or at ease; and that I was continually in hiding for fear of my enemies, thou didst bid me go take my complaint to 'Abdu'l-Bahá. Outwardly, I made no complaint to Him; yet the exigency of Divine justice is reward and punishment, even as His Holiness Bahá'u'lláh hath said: 'The canopy of world order is upraised upon the two pillars of reward and punishment.' Wherefore is such recompense doomed and inevitable." In fine, under such conditions these powerful enemies passed from the world, and their lamp was extinguished. Their honours the King of Martyrs and the Beloved of Martyrs, however, kindled a lamp that will for ever shine refulgent, while from each day of their lives up to their final martyrdom a blissful fragrance was shed abroad that shall always perfume the nostrils of the spiritual. Upon the twain His Holiness Bahá'u'lláh hath bestowed Tablets, favours, and the heavenly titles "King of Martyrs" and "Beloved of Martyrs," by which twin beatific names they are now famed throughout the East and the West. *Finis*.

* * *

* * *

**

The Account of the Well-known Orientalist

Edward Browne

Concerning

The Martyrdom of the

Twin Shining Lights

The celebrated Orientalist Edward Browne in his well-known book *A Year Amongst the Persians* has on several occasions, sometimes briefly and at other times in detail, touched generally upon the subject of the Blessed Cause, and specifically upon that of the martyrdom of the Twin Shining Lights. We shall at this point render into Persian from the English original the essence of some of these passages, choosing, so far as is possible, the part of concision in conveying their purport:

*Among these passages, he has written in the fifth chapter of the book, which concerns Tihrán, the following:*

The Sháh is not directly involved in the slaughter of the Bábís. The Sháh's son, Zillu's-Sultán, was the one who in 1879 A.D. had two of them put to death in Isfahán, and in 1888 caused also the death of Mírzá Ashraf-i-Ábádi'í. In the spring of 1889 the Bábís of Sidih and Najaf-Ábád suffered grievous molestation and persecution. In May 1890 Jalálu'd-Dawlih, the son of Zillu's-Sultán, and grandson of the Sháh, had seven Bahá'ís put to death in the city of Yazd. These sanguinary outrages were all occasioned indirectly by the Sháh of Írán. He gave the order, and was cognizant of the outcome. In July 1869 Mírzá Badí', having travelled all the way from 'Akká, personally delivered into the hand of the Sháh in Tihrán a long Tablet that had emanated from the pen of His Holiness Bahá'u'lláh, and the Sháh ordered him to be put to death.

*In the eighth chapter of "A Year Amongst the Persians," the author has provided a detailed account of the Twin Shining Lights, whose essence is as follows:*

A week had passed since my arrival in Isfahán. One afternoon as I was sitting unoccupied in the midst of the room, which overlooked a courtyard, considering within myself when I should set out for Shíráz and Yazd, two vendors arrived, bringing with them a quantity of rugs, old coins, brass implements, and other items. I left the room to inspect their wares, but the prices they asked for seemed to me excessive. One of the vendors was young, while the other was an old man whose beard was dyed with henna. The old man said: "Áqá! We have come far in order to bring thee these things. Thou too hast delayed us a little from our business. Shouldst thou now refrain from dealing with us, it would not be handsome of thee." I was about to say, "It was thou who camest hither, not I who sent after thee," when the young man, coming up to me, placed his mouth right next to my ear, and softly whispered, "Fear not that we shall defraud thee. Rest assured. I am no Muslim that I should wish to cozen thee. I am a Bahá'í (Bábí)." I had long been hoping to make the acquaintance of this community. I believe he imagined that the Christians were better acquainted with the Bahá'ís than were the Muslims. In any case, his words produced upon me a magical effect. All at once an opportunity had presented itself for me to fulfil the wish I had been cherishing for the last four months. When I had sufficiently recovered from the astonishment with which his words filled me to speak, I exclaimed: "Thou art a Bahá'í! From the moment of my arrival in Írán I have been hoping to meet a member of this faith. Prithee, if it be possible, provide me with a book. The things thou hast brought are of no interest to me; what I wish for is your books." The vendor said, "I shall see what I can do on thy behalf. I promise to bring thee at least one or two books. But tell me, for I am curious to know: Why art thou so eager to acquire our books? Where didst thou hear the name 'Bahá'í'? -- for to judge from the tenor of thy remarks, thou hast not yet met any of the Bahá'ís." I replied, "Years before I travelled to Írán -- or ever conceived the notion of doing so -- I had heard of your religion. A learned Frenchman who lived in Tihrán at the time of His Holiness the Báb wrote a book about the Bahá'ís, recording all the instances of torment and injury they suffered. Ever since reading that book, which is in French, I have desired to acquire further information on the subject -- an aim in which I have not so far been successful.
