Now however, by the grace of God, and through the assistance which thou art shortly to render me, I shall at last arrive at my object." "So the Call of the Revelation hath been heard in Frangistán too," observed the vendor, "Very good. Rest assured that I shall do all I can to assist thee, so that thou mayest be fully informed concerning this Cause. Shouldst thou be so inclined, thou mayest meet a learned and pious believer who hath suffered many vicissitudes for the sake of the Cause. With thy consent, I shall arrange for thee to meet him. This person is the chief and elder of our Bahá'í community in Isfahán, and once a fortnight visiteth each of our homes in order to ascertain that we are comfortably circumstanced, while at the same time encouraging and enthusing us. I myself am but a poor illiterate vendor; but he will tell thee all thou wishest to know." His words, which he had all this while been gently breathing into my ear, here came to an end. The old vendor, who had been awaiting with utmost impatience the conclusion of our conference, now, seeing that it was over, set aside a few items of his merchandise, which he presented to me, while I for my part bestowed upon him some money for his pains. Taking leave of them, I returned to my room, reminding the young vendor at the last moment, "See that thou forget not to bring the books! Come if thou canst tomorrow morning!" The following day, at about the same hour of their previous visit, my long-cherished wish was fulfilled, for the Bahá'í vendor arrived, and presented me with two books. I at once escorted him into my room. It happened that a devout Muslim, one Hájí Safar, was seated there, and upon my entering the room with the vendor he wished to know what was afoot. Being however afraid to utter a word in his presence, I of necessity possessed my soul in patience. Only after he had left did I receive the books from the vendor. Of the two books he had brought me, one was the Kitáb-i-Íqán, of which my companion said, "This book is very important, and containeth weighty proofs in vindication of the Blessed Cause." The other book was smaller, and, as I later understood, comprised a work of 'Abbás Effendi, the son of Bahá'u'lláh, who is these days the leader of the Bahá'ís, and resident in 'Akká, one of the towns of Syria. He wrote this book in reply to 'Alí Shawkat Páshá, who had inquired concerning the meaning of the Tradition "I was a hidden treasure." Examples of the calligraphy of one of the sons of His Holiness Bahá'u'lláh, and likewise of the penmanship of Mishkín-Qalam, were also placed in my hands. Mishkín-Qalam is among the Bahá'ís who in 1868 were exiled to Cyprus by order of the Ottoman government. My vendor companion told me that among the friends were many beautiful works of Mishkín-Qalam, especially those which, in what is known as his "avian" script, he had executed in the form of a bird. Mishkín-Qalam signs himself at the foot of his works:

*Dar díyár-i-khatt shah-i-sáhib-'alam*

*Bandiy-i-Báb-i-Bahá', Mishkín-Qalam*

Sháh of the realm of script, signal among men,

Thrall of the Gate of Bahá', "Musk-laden Pen."

Having received from him these inestimable gems, I next questioned the vendor, "Knowest thou where lie interred those twin Siyyids who, in 1879, were martyred on account of their Bahá'í affiliation?" "Ay, I know the spot well," he replied, "And shall, if thou desirest, direct thee thither. But Áqá, tell me truly: Thou, who art at such pains to obtain our books, and art ready to visit the grave of the Bahá'í martyrs; and who hast even travelled to 'Akká and met the Blessed Beauty -- I cannot but conceive that thou art a Bahá'í. Is't not so? Thou needest not conceal the matter from me." "No, my good friend," I said, "I am not a Bahá'í, nor have I ever travelled to 'Akká. It is merely that, impressed as I am by the spectacle of a new-born religion that hath reared up self-sacrificial and courageous disciples, and produced followers ready to die for their belief, to visit the grave of your martyrs, who have forgone wealth and life itself for the sake of their faith and conviction, is for me a way of showing respect for their steadfastness and courage. ...

At this moment, the old vendor entered with a number of photographs, as well as various other items. When they wished to leave, the young vendor, finding a convenient moment, whispered in my ear, "Next Saturday -- forget it not! I shall arrange for someone to meet thee in a certain part of the city, or else myself come and escort thee to my home, where thou shalt meet our honoured chief. Ere the appointed day, I shall come to inform thee of the spot where thou must wait. *Khudá Háfiz!*" Saturday at length arrived, and early in the morning my vendor companion appeared, and said, "Come thou to the entrance of such and such a caravanserai in the city, and wait there. A little after midday, my companion and I shall come to meet thee." Having readied myself, I proceeded at the stated hour to the appointed place. Erelong the old vendor arrived, and gestured to me to follow him. We passed through the bazaar, and when he knew that I was following, he ceased glancing behind him so frequently as he had at first. We pressed our steps hurriedly for half an hour until we arrived at the young Bahá'í vendor's home. The latter, having bade me welcome at the doorway, conducted me into the reception room, and, whilst awaiting the arrival of another person, busied himself preparing tea for me and the other guests. He showed me too a number of other precious objects, amongst which was a photograph of Mishkín-Qalam with his two sons; also a view of the graves of the martyrs of Isfahán, which he informed me had been taken by a European resident in the city, who had social and other ties with the martyrs. After a while, a sound of knocking was heard at the door. My host hastened to open it, and in came the Bahá'í teacher, whom he forthwith introduced to me. He was a man of pleasing aspect, dignified, about forty-five years of age. He sate down opposite me, and began drinking tea. His comportment was most agreeable, and his conversation interesting and engaging. Altogether, he was a man such as one could not forget, and whose countenance would imprint itself upon the memory of whosoever met him. The old vendor, who had left the house, now returned with one of the Bahá'í merchants. As it chanced, the presence of this respected personage somewhat circumscribed the scope of our conversation, and so I was unable to ask all I wished to know. This much, however, I gathered, that he was a well-known Bahá'í teacher who had suffered great hardship in the path of this faith, and had frequently been cast into prison, and on several occasions exiled. "Pray enlighten me," I asked him, "What was it induced thee to bear such adversity and tribulation for the sake of the Bahá'í Faith?" "Thou must visit 'Akká," he replied, "In order to apprehend the root cause of this matter." "Hast thou thyself been to 'Akká?" I inquired. "If so, what didst thou witness?" "I have indeed been to 'Akká," he replied, "And what I witnessed was this: the sum total of human perfection embodied in a mortal frame." He said no more on this head; but then continued, "As I understand it, thou art to leave Isfahán a few days hence. Fortunately there is a way in which thou mayest hear all thou desirest to know. I shall write to the friends of Shíráz and Ábádih, and tell them about thee, commending thee to their hospitality. Shouldst thou travel to other cities too, there are Bahá'ís everywhere with whom thou canst meet." He then provided me with the names of two of the friends in Ábádih and Shíráz, saying, "Make a note of these. They will come to meet thee. If thou wishest it, thou mayest associate with them. For the rest, I shall now depart. May God keep thee, and guide thee unto the right way." The young vendor said to him, "Áqá! He wisheth to visit the graves of the martyrs, and I have promised to take him thither. Shouldst thou do us the honour to join us, we would profit from thy company on the way." "It is highly commendable to visit the graves of the martyrs," he replied, "And I bless this action on his part. I shall not, however, myself accompany you, for I am known to most people, and it might not be the course of wisdom. *Khudá Háfiz!*" A little while after he departed, I too decided to take my leave. The Bahá'í merchant undertook to escort me to the entrance of the aforesaid caravanserai. Although there was no suitable opportunity for conversation on the way, he whispered to me, "Áqá, behold in what a place we live! Like rapacious beasts the people fall upon and slay us. Our sole crime is to have recognized God, and to have believed in the Manifestation of His Cause." When we arrived at the caravanserai I bade my guide farewell, and straightway returned home in order to prepare for the journey to Shíráz. A certain 'Abdu'r-Rahím from the village of Khuráskán, a carrier by trade, proved ready to hire me four of his beasts for the journey to Shíráz at a cost of three túmáns per head, agreeing that I might stop wherever I pleased along the way. I paid him half the cost of the hire, and the preparations for my journey were complete. It was a little past noon the next day when my young vendor friend came to conduct me to the graves of the martyrs. For the space of an hour we made our way beneath the blazing sun until we arrived at a large cemetery by the name of Takhtih-Fúlád. My companion looked about him, and hailed from afar a poor grave-digger, who was also a Bahá'í. The three of us set off together, eventually arriving at a place where a few stones and a quantity of fine sand met our eyes. The vendor told me, "The grave of the two martyrs is here. The reason why we have set no headstone over it is that if the Muslims see aught, they immediately come to destroy and smash it. Now will we sit here awhile, and I shall relate to thee the story of the martyrdom of these two brothers. First, however, it is fitting that our companion should read a prayer, and recite the Visitation Tablet for the martyrs." This said, he pointed to the nearer of the graves, and continued, "This is the grave of Hájí Mírzá Hasan, who is surnamed the King of Martyrs, while that other grave belongs to his elder brother, Hájí Mírzá Husayn, who is surnamed the Beloved of Martyrs." The grave-digger brought out of his pocket a small book from which he read certain Verses and prayers in Arabic and Persian; after which, as we were thus seated beside the graves, the vendor began his tale: "These two brothers were Siyyids, and engaged in commerce; yet neither their holy lineage nor their lavish bestowals to the poor and indigent could protect them from the malice of their enemies. The two brothers had lent the sum of ten thousand túmáns to Shaykh Báqir, who was one of the divines of Isfahán. Shaykh Báqir knew that both his creditors were Bahá'ís, and thought to himself that in order to be free of the debt he owed them, he could make use of their Bahá'í connection in order to have them put to death. He therefore consulted on the matter with the Imám-Jum'ih of Isfahán, who was the head of the clerical establishment, saying, 'These two brothers are Bahá'ís, and by the law of Islám must be put to death since they do not recognize His Holiness the Apostle as the Seal of the Prophets, and believe Mírzá Siyyid 'Alí-Muhammad-i-Shírází to be the author of a new Revelation. This apart, these two brothers are exceedingly wealthy, and if they be slain on the grounds of opposition to Islám, their fortune will revert unto us.' The Imám-Jum'ih accepted his proposal. These two reprobates now went to see Zillu's-Sultán, who was a prince, and the governor of Isfahán. The prince would not at first associate himself with their scheme, saying, 'Since these two brothers are not acting contrary to the interests of the state and the government, I cannot have them slain: unless ye should issue a fatvá decreeing that under the law of Islám they must be put to death -- then should I be able to take some measure.' Shaykh Báqir and the Imám-Jum'ih, together with seventeen other divines of the city, accordingly issued a fatvá decreeing that those two wronged ones should be put to death. At first they were thrown into prison. Their arrest provoked a strange uproar among the people, and even Europeans resident in Isfahán who were acquainted with the virtue and generosity of the two brothers were perturbed. Numerous telegrams were despatched to the Sháh in Tihrán, and he in turn telegraphed to the Zillu's-Sultán. His message, however, only reached the prince after the two brothers had been executed. Their throats were slit. A cord was then fastened to their feet, and their bodies were dragged through the thoroughfares and markets as far as the city gate. There they were cast beneath an earth wall, which was knocked down over them. When night fell, an old servant of the martyrs, who knew where their bodies lay, came and rescued them from beneath the wall. The impact of the collapse of the wall had crushed together those two pure temples. The old man washed from them the dust and blood with water from the Záyandih Rúd, and brought them to the cemetery. He dug two new graves, and there laid them to rest. The old man washed their two corses beside the Záyandih Rúd. The next morning servants of the prince, and soldiers in the area, noticed that the bodies had been removed from beneath the wall. A search was instituted for them, but to no avail, and thus the two bodies were preserved. However, as I mentioned, we cannot place a headstone over their grave; and even to visit the place we must perforce come in secret, for the Muslims are fiercely hostile, and should the matter come to their notice, such is their animosity towards us that they would utterly destroy the graves." "It was on account of these twin martyrs," he continued, "That this companion of ours (the grave-digger) became a believer in the Blessed Cause." "Is't not so?" he asked him; to which the old man replied, "Ay, truly, after the martyrdom of these two brothers I had a dream, in which I saw that a host of people had congregated in this graveyard, as though they had come to visit a particular site. Still in my dream I asked someone, 'Whose are these graves which the people are visiting?' 'These two graves,' I was told in reply, 'belong to the King of Martyrs and the Beloved of Martyrs.' For this reason I became a believer in the Blessed Cause -- in this Cause whose followers, having recognized its Divine origin, attest its truth with their own blood. From that day forth, I have made it my endeavour to guard and protect these twin graves, and have placed upon them some simple marks lest their location should be forgotten." The vendor said, "This is a good man. Previously, when the friends came to visit these graves, he would take them to his home, and entertain them with tea and a narghile. His home lay in this same vicinity. When the Muslims realized what he was about, they destroyed his home, beat him, and smashed his tea utensils and narghile. He is a very poor man." Then he whispered to me, "Wert thou to bestow upon him a small bounty it would be a most fitting gesture." I made him a modest donation, whereupon he arose, and, expressing his thanks, departed. We remained seated there a while longer, and then set off on our way. I cast back a last glance at the two graves, and by way of a memorial picked up a little stone from each. We headed towards the city, and on the way saw again our grave-digger acquaintance, who was walking in company with his small son. When he noticed us, he once more expressed his thanks, saying, "I wish thee Godspeed!" I am deeply touched by these poor and loving people. When I shared a part of my thoughts with my companion, he replied, "It is even so. Ye Christians are much closer to us Bahá'ís than are the Muslims. Ye know that the Muslims consider you unclean. If any of them associate with you, it is in appearance only, for their religious fanaticism is intense. But we Bahá'ís are not so. We have been enjoined to regard all men as clean and pure, whatever their religion. In particular we harbour affection for you Christians. The tribulations borne by His Holiness Bahá'u'lláh, the Lawgiver of the Bahá'í Faith, Who is the return of Christ, are the selfsame sufferings borne by His Holiness Christ, the Founder of your religion. Their Holinesses Bahá'u'lláh and Christ were both possessed of Divine knowledge and wisdom. From childhood, the signs of greatness were evident in both. They were both averse to the reprehensible customs and habits prevalent in their societies. Such too was the case with His Holiness the Báb. Their Holinesses the Báb and Christ were both put to death by the sentence of the fanatical clergy, and by the order of the rulers of their age. ..."

*Edward Browne now turns to providing, on the authority of the Bahá'í vendor, an account of the blessed teachings; the progress of the Cause; the sacrifices of the friends; the month of fasting; and other matters, which it is not considered necessary here to translate.*

*Likewise, in the tenth chapter of this same book, he provides a detailed account of some notable events in Bahá'í history, amongst which, on the subject of the Twin Shining Lights, he states the following:*

... A few days later I went to meet my companion Mírzá 'Alí. Shortly after my arrival, Hájí Mírzá Hasan also made his appearance, and then for about three consecutive hours we discussed the Bahá'í religion. Our discussion was interrupted at a certain point by the arrival of a stranger. Hájj Mírzá Hasan then provided me with the following description of an encounter he had had with Bahá'u'lláh. He said, "Thou hast doubtless heard of the martyrs of Isfahán. A little ere their martyrdom, I was in 'Akká. Hájí Mírzá Hasan-'Alí, whom thou didst meet in Isfahán, and Áqá Siyyid Hádí were there also. One or two days before we left 'Akká for Írán, we were admitted into the presence of His Holiness Bahá'u'lláh. There was a garden there to which He sometimes repaired. On that day He was seated in this garden, and we too were standing in His blessed presence. He bade us be seated. When we had seated ourselves, He ordered tea to be brought for us. Whilst we were occupied in drinking tea, He observed, 'An important event is shortly about to befall.' That evening, Áqá Siyyid Hádí inquired in person of His Holiness where this great event would occur. He replied, 'In the Land of Sád.' Siyyid Hádí wrote to some of his friends in Írán informing them of the matter. When we arrived back in Írán, Hájj Mírzá Hasan-'Alí remained in Tihrán, while I continued my journey towards Isfahán. In Káshán I heard the news of the arrest of the Twin Shining Lights, but told myself, 'They are wealthy, and will inevitably be released.' I never imagined that Bahá'u'lláh's words could be an allusion to their martyrdom, and was convinced that the great event intended should be an outbreak of cholera, a famine, or an earthquake. Four or five days later, the news of their martyrdom reached me in Káshán. I decided not to continue my journey to Isfahán, and returned to Tihrán. I realized that the expression 'important event' was an allusion to their martyrdom. The Imám-Jum'ih, in pronouncing their death sentence, clapped his hand upon his throat, saying, 'If there be any crime in having these Bábís put to death, upon my neck be it.' A little while after their martyrdom, the Imám-Jum'ih fell into disgrace, and left Isfahán for Mashhad. It was not long before malignant tumours appeared in his throat and upon his neck, and he died of this same malady. A month after their martyrdom, a Blessed Tablet arrived from 'Akká containing dire warnings to Shaykh Báqir. Shortly after this the Shaykh journeyed to Karbilá, only to find, after his return to Isfahán, that his wife and daughter, who were beyond words beautiful and comely, had been borne off by the prince-governor to his own harem. This misfortune was followed swiftly by another; and, just as His Holiness Bahá'u'lláh had intimated in the Tablet He addressed to him, he died in the utmost humiliation, having derived not the slightest benefit from all the wealth and riches he had wrongfully amassed." Mírzá 'Alí affirmed that he had himself read the stated Tablet and taken a copy of it.

Specimen of the Handwriting of the King of Martyrs

*Facsimile of a letter from the King of Martyrs to a believer in the Holy Land concerning an order the latter had made for binding Tablets. The text of the letter is as follows: "May my soul be a sacrifice for thy love. I perused at the time of its arrival the amber-scented purest letter wherewith, (by the hand of) Áqá Muhammad-Husayn the courier, thou didst graciously honour and dignify this broken-winged one at the beginning of the month of Shavvál, and (rejoiced) exceedingly at the news it contained of the health of thy blessed temple; for the which I rendered thanks unto His Holiness the Adored One -- may my soul and the souls of all who are in the kingdoms of Revelation and Creation be His sacrifice. Since in thy letter thou hadst bidden me (await) some suitable means of communication, I have refrained from writing to thee until now, when Áqá Muhammad-Husayn hath (returned) from the Land of Bá' and Shín (Búshihr) with the intention of visiting Him Who is the Object of our adoration, and the trusts thou didst leave with me have by the grace of the True One been discharged. Although thou hadst ordered the Most Holy Divine Tablets to be arranged separately, it seemed in my humble opinion preferable and more fitting for them to be bound in a single volume. For this reason I requested Áqá Muhammad-Hádí the book-binder -- upon him be the Glory of God -- to arrange them in volumes, whence with the utmost care and diligence he hath produced two volumes, each containing Holy, Incomparable and Blessed Tablets to the number of Váhid. The numbers of Most Holy Tablets (for) thee one Tablet (...) become this servant (...) ... // I donated one Holy and Blessed Tablet to Áqá Muhammad-Hádí, that he might include it among thy Tablets, to the end that the volumes should be identical. Also accompanying the volumes (is) the Blessed Ode which, as thou hadst bidden, hath been gilded and bound. My hope is that all the completed work will meet with thine approval. As regards the expenses of the Holy and Incomparable Tablets and the Blessed Ode, since thou hadst ordered me to charge them to the account of Áqá 'Abdu'l-Husayn, I have, obedient to thine order, made out the (invoice of expenses) in the name of Áqá 'Abdu'l-Husayn -- upon whom be the Glory of God. Assuredly this will come to thine attention. (...) Do thou convey (...) the greetings of this evanescent one unto (...) blessed thine (esteemed father) -- upon him be the Glory of God -- and thy respected brothers -- upon them be the Glory of God -- and crave on my behalf their pardon for my not writing to each a (separate) letter. I await ever thine honoured communications, thy (seemly) commands, and tidings of thy physical well-being. Glory, (mercy) and splendour be upon you, and upon all the friends of God on the part of this evanescent one, the Name Há'. Dated the (third) of Safar 92"*

Specimen of the Handwriting of the Beloved of Martyrs

*Facsimile of a letter written by the Beloved of Martyrs to an unidentified recipient. So far as the text can be made out, it reads as follows: "May my soul, my essence and my being be a sacrifice for the dust of thy feet. After (...) I was completely unaware (of) thy having been indisposed in the manner thou didst describe in thy letter; otherwise I should have written to thee repeatedly. When Áqá (...) Muhammad-Hasan (in Yazd) wrote to say that thou hadst been ill of fever, and thy condition had improved, I (was) reassured that thou hadst made a full recovery. When last week I was honoured to receive thy letter, (...) I realized that thou hadst still not made a full recovery. This (abject) evanescent one too hath (from the beginning of the noble month of) Ramadán (until now) been afflicted by a severe pain in the side, and even after (many) purgative (treatments) the ailment hath still not been entirely cured. Although it is three years since the pain in the side of this servant (began, and still I have not found for it, nor is there, any cure...), I swear by His pure Branches that were He to vouchsafe to thy blessed being a full recovery from thine indisposition, I would accept that (whatever) residue of it remaineth should fall to the lot of this evanescent one. I swear by thy blessed head, that even in this frail condition I am nevertheless occupied both night and day responding to orders arriving from all quarters, and am thus absolutely unable to attend to other work. At least, in occupying myself with writing letters, I become to some degree diverted from attending to my poor health. At all events, (we resign ourselves to God, Who) is the source of grace and favours to all mankind, whilst shortcoming and failure proceed only from these sinful creatures. At all events, last week ... // ... I sent thee as enclosures in my letter (copies) of a number of Incomparable Tablets (...) -- may my soul be a sacrifice for the dust of His feet. God willing, thou wilt have seen them. (Copies) of two or three further Tablets were also sent to (Rahbar), to the end that he might forward (copies) of the same. This week too a (copy of a) Blessed Tablet that had been vouchsafed to the friends of Há' and Mím (Hamadán) arrived from the Land of Tá' (Tihrán). Should a scribe come to hand, this too will be forwarded. (Áqá Shaykh Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Qá'iní's present) arrived within thy blessed letter. His reply arrived this week (...) in a letter from Alexandria (dated ... of the year ...). I noted all the matters thou hadst set forth. Thou hadst written concerning conditions in that land. (The whole region is suffering) on account of the absence of rainfall. Some went forth from the city to pray for rain. That same night a strange earthquake took place. It is evident that the influence of malicious souls hath produced this effect. In this land too there hath been absolutely no rainfall. All the people have (become) disturbed and troubled. The governorship of Yazd too hath been bestowed upon his honour Zillu's-Sultán. (He is to have his way in Yazd.) Even as thou hast said, the signs of the wrath and anger of God have encompassed all. May the Lord keep His believing servants firm and steadfast in His Cause. Thou hadst inquired of Jináb-i-Amín. Having set off for (...), he is presently in Yazd. He was (...) in Tihrán. These days he will not be arriving in this land. This is told thee for thine information. Do thou kindly convey the greetings of this servant. (...) Glory of God (...). // ... and convey to the blessed womenfolk and the sons and (daughters...). (All) the children and the servant (...) present their (self-effacement and nothingness). (God willing) thou wilt gladden me with tidings of the improvement of thy blessed condition. The sacred month of Muharram 96. // (Prayer for healing) 'None shall be able to withstand the sovereign potency of His healing, neither in the heavens, nor in the earth, nor in whatsoever lieth between them. He, verily, is the Healer, the Curer, the Restorer.'"*

*Text of a message from the Universal House of Justice concerning Jináb-i-Hasan-i- Mahbúbí: "Jináb-i-Mahbúbí, who throughout his life approved himself a lover of the Blessed Cause and of our beloved and puissant liege, his Holiness the Guardian of the Cause of God, and who attested by the unstinting services he rendered in divers fields the measure of his devotion at His sacred threshold, won finally the glorious crown of martyrdom, thereby winning and attaining his heart's desire. This tape, together with similar memorials, will remain preserved in the Holy Land, that it may stand as a testimonial to the courage, the valour and the fidelity of the dear friends in Írán. // With cordial salutations. On behalf of the Secretariat of the Universal House of Justice. "*

He died in 1242 A.H. \year commencing 5 August 1826\ between Mecca and Medina, and was buried in the Baqí' cemetery in Medina the Fragrant. In His biographical account of Shamsu'd-Duhá in *Memorials of the Faithful* (see below) 'Abdu'l-Bahá says in this respect: "... since she and her husband were both strongly attracted to the mystical teachings of that great luminary, the excellent and distinguished Siyyid Kázim-i-Rashtí, they left for Karbilá, accompanied by Mírzá Hádí's brother, Mírzá Muhammad-'Alíy-i-Nahrí."*

Hájí Siyyid Kázim is the son of Siyyid Qásim-i-Rashtí, and the successor of the late Shaykh Ahmad-i-Ahsá'í. He passed away after sixty years of existence on the ninth day of Dhi'l-Hijjih, which is known as the Day of 'Arafih, in the year 1259 A.H. \31 December 1843\, and is buried in Karbilá in the precincts of the illumined resting-place of His Holiness the Prince of Martyrs. An account of him is provided in *Nabíl's Narrative* and the *Rayhánatu'l-Adab*, to which reference should be made. (Ishráq-Khávarí)

Khurásán; see *The Dawn-Breakers*, p. 269: "In pursuance of the Divine decree, in the days when Quddús was still residing in Mashhad, there was revealed from the pen of the Báb a Tablet addressed to all the believers of Persia, in which every loyal adherent of the Faith was enjoined to 'hasten to the Land of Khá',' the province of Khurásán. The news of this high injunction spread with marvellous rapidity and aroused universal enthusiasm."

This section contains also (on the pattern of that following) an account of the *descendants* of the paternal uncles of the Twin Shining Lights.*

The Sultánu'l-'Ulamá \literally, "King of the 'Ulamá"\ is the same who was ordered by Manúchihr Khán, the Mu'tamidu'd-Dawlih \the governor of Isfahán\, to entertain the Báb in his home, and at whose request the Commentary on the Súrih of Va'l-'Asr was revealed. At first he was favourably disposed towards the Báb, but later, fearing lest his standing and authority should be diminished, he issued a fatvá declaring the Báb to be insane. He died in 1291 A.H. \year commencing 18 February 1874\ (Ishráq-Khávarí) For a description of the said evening reception, see *The Dawn-Breakers*, pp. 201-2.

Mír Muhammad-Husayn, by reason of the part he played in the martyrdom of the Twin Shining Lights, was afterwards styled the "She-Serpent" by the Pen of the All-Merciful in the Lawh-i-Burhán (Tablet of the Proof). (Ishráq-Khávarí)

He is stated on p. 100 of *The Dawn-Breakers* to be one of the few Siyyids of Isfahán who recognized the truth of the Cause. Listed on p. 422 as the 133^rd^ of 173 souls who fell in the siege of the fort of Shaykh Tabarsí, he is described as "a distinguished Siyyid and a highly esteemed divine, whose declared purpose to enlist under the banner of Mullá Husayn caused a great tumult among the 'ulamás of Isfahán."

Literally, "Hájí Muhammad the Merchant" (the aforesaid Hájí Áqá Muhammad).

A well-known cleric who was among the earliest converts to the Faith (see *The Dawn-Breakers*, p. 100, footnote), and who translated the Báb's Risáliy-i-Furú'-i-'Adlíyyih into Persian (see *God Passes By*, p. 24).

Year commencing 9 December 1847 in the Gregorian calendar.

Shaykh Salmán, the Courier of the All-Merciful, received many Tablets, and performed mighty services for the Cause. 'Abdu'l-Bahá included an account of him in *Memorials of the Faithful*. His children and grandchildren are still living, and under the shadow of the Cause. He was originally from Cham Khalaf-i-'Ísá, one of the villages around Hindíján in the region of Ahváz. He was named by his mother Shaykh Khanjar; but after he embraced the Blessed Cause he became known as Shaykh Salmán. His native village of Cham Khalaf-i-'Ísá near Hindíján, whose name survives to this day, is now known as Daríhak. He passed away in 1316 A.H. \year commencing 22 May 1898\, and his grave is in Shíráz. (Ishráq-Khávarí)

i.e., specifically, the port of Jaddih, from whence pilgrims proceed to Mecca.

She was known as 'Izzíyyih. She was very much opposed to the Cause of God, and a treatise was written in her name to refute it. Meaning in Persian the act of gazing at a spectacle.

Because the child was always wishing to "have a look" at things, the word was often on his lips; and Bahá'u'lláh thus represents him as indulging this predilection to his heart's content in the world to come.

The Báb was designated among other titles "Possessor of the Twin Names," (sc. 'Alí and Muhammad), whose significance, thus conjoined, resides partly in their designating the stations respectively of *Vicegerency* (identified with the Imám 'Alí) and *Prophecy* (identified with the Prophet Muhammad).

i.e. the Holy Sites in 'Iráq -- the same earlier designated by the author "Exalted Thresholds."

As mentioned above, 'Abdu'l-Bahá states in His biographical account of Shamsu'd-Duhá in *Memorials of the Faithful* (see below) that Áqá Mírzá Hádí and his wife Shamsu'd-Duhá, being strongly attracted to the mystical teachings of Siyyid Kázim, removed to Karbilá, together with the as yet unmarried Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-'Alí, in order to benefit from his knowledge and learning at first hand.*

In reference to the Muslim Traditions, which depict the Countenance of God as concealed by seventy thousand veils of light.

Reminiscent of Shaykh Hasan-i-Zunúzí's description, recorded in *The Dawn-Breakers*, of the impression made upon him by the Báb: "I watched Him \the Báb\ several times as He stood in an attitude of prayer at the doorway of the shrine of the Imám Husayn. So wrapt was He in His devotions that He seemed utterly oblivious of those around Him. ... those of the visiting pilgrims who were near enough to hear Him ... marvelled at the evidences of piety and veneration which that youthful countenance evinced. ... That Youth had set my heart aflame. ... My soul was wedded to His till the day when the call of a Youth from Shíráz, proclaiming Himself to be the Báb, reached my ears. The thought instantly flashed through my mind that such a person could be none other than that selfsame Youth whom I had seen in Karbilá, the Youth of my heart's desire."---p. 30

'Abdu'l-Bahá states however in the said account of Shamsu'd-Duhá in *Memorials of the Faithful* that Mírzá Hádí returned to Karbilá (though it was doubtless by way of Isfahán).*

18 June 1920.

The site of the city's former graveyard, and one of the foremost cemeteries in the Shí'ite World. Dating back to before the Muslim conquest in the seventh century, the cemetery is the resting-place of a number of famous figures, including (allegedly) the prophet Joshua, and Siyyid Abu'l-Qásim Mír-Findiriskí, who is referred to on p. 41 of the Epistle to the Son of the Wolf. Since 1363 A.H.S. (c. 1984) there have been no further burials in the cemetery, save in the case of victims of the Írán-'Iráq War; and it has now been replaced as the principal graveyard of the city by the "Bágh-i-Ridván" -- the "Ridván Garden."

Literally, "Imám-born:" the descendant of an Imám, or, as here, his shrine, forming a place of pilgrimage (*zíyárat*).

The transference of Mishkín-Qalam's family to the Holy Land is described in Húshang Guhar-Ríz's *Haváríyyún-i-Hadrat-i-Bahá'u'lláh* ("The Apostles of Bahá'u'lláh") as follows: "Mishkín-Qalam, who had travelled to Baghdád without the knowledge of his wife and children, had by now had no word of them for nearly twenty years. However diligently that respected family had searched for him, they had come across no trace of him. The Royal Court too had directed that his picture be posted on all gateways to the city, so that whoever had any clue as to his whereabouts should inform the government. // After a long while, the King and the Beloved of Martyrs became aware, from seeing him photographed in the vicinity of the Bahá'í Holy Places, that Mishkín-Qalam was resident in the Holy Land. Mírzá Haydar-'Alí happened to be visiting Isfahán at this time, and after he had contacted the family, spoken with them and conveyed to them tidings of the Blessed Cause, the whole family embraced its truth, and attained the honour of believing in its message. Pursuant to directions, that respected family, with the assistance of their honours the King of Martyrs and the Beloved of Martyrs, now set out for 'Akká. // Uninformed of this development, Mishkín-Qalam, intoxicated with the wine of reunion, was, by his sweet discourse and witticisms, engaged both night and day in brightening the lives of the Holy Family, when all at once he was confronted by the arrival of his wife and children. // The wife of Mishkín-Qalam was accorded the honour of sewing the vestments of the Most Holy Abhá Beauty, on account of which God-given bestowal her heart was filled with gratitude, and feelings of Divine contentment welled up from her very soul."---p. 148

The Arabic word used in the original here (*yattaqí*) carries with it the implication that he resorted to the practice of *taqíyyih*, or dissimulation of his faith.

i.e. he made a spoken, rather than a written, final testament of faith (cf. the Kitáb-i-Aqdas, Paragraph 109 -- the verb here represented as "to pronounce \one's\ confession" is the same as has there been rendered "to bear witness \unto").

i.e., possibly, 'Abdu'l-Bahá Himself, considering that, as the Son of Bahá'u'lláh, He was in a sense the "Remnant" of One Whose advent represents the fulfilment of the promised appearance of God on the Last Day in His guise of the Most Merciful (Rahmán): "By Him Who is the Great Announcement! The All-Merciful is come invested with undoubted sovereignty."---*Gleanings*, Selection XVII, p. 40. 'Abdu'l-Bahá's affirmation may thus be regarded as effectively, and *mercifully*, taking the place of the written confession of faith enjoined in the Kitáb-i-Aqdas. The word "Remnant" ("*Bid'at*"), it may be noted, is applied to descendants of the Prophet, as in the title "*Bid'atu'r-Rasúl*" ("The Remnant of the Prophet"), which is a designation of His daughter Fátimih, and has also, significantly, been used in Bahá'u'lláh's Tablet of the Proof (see Section Eight below) in reference to the Twin Shining Lights: "Thou hast torn in pieces a remnant of the Prophet Himself, and imagined that thou hadst helped the Faith of God."*

i.e. Munírih Khánum to be.

According to Munírih Khánum's own account, as recorded in *Munírih Khánum: Memoirs and Letters* (pp. 22-24), Áqá Mírzá Kázim, "a most dignified, loving, pleasant, and attractive person," who "would bring all the news from Adrianople, from the Presence of the Beloved, to Isfahán," had originally been destined as her husband, but, being taken ill on his wedding night, was unable to consummate the marriage, and passed away a few days later. "This story is related," she concludes, "so that if in the world of being difficulties and trials encompass a person, then he should know that behind the mystic veil there is a wisdom concealed. In reality that youth was an escort who delivered me to my true goal and ultimate destination. He it was who joined this drop to the Most Mighty Ocean." It would not indeed seem consistent with the ideals of the Cause, or with the Master's rôle as Perfect Exemplar, that His marriage should have been attended by such a consequence as that intimated in the present account.*

i.e. innumerable souls have perished on account of the Divine retribution entailed by these grievous transgressions. i.e. the provincial levies, each of whose units was designated a "Fawj" ("Regiment"); e.g. the "Fawj-i-Aráminih," the "Armenian Regiment." Ja'far-Qulí Khán was Mír-Panj of the Fawj of Firaydan.

Chihil Sutún ("Forty Pillars") was built by Sháh 'Abbás II between 1646-48 as part of the palace complex initiated by Sháh 'Abbás I in the region of the aforesaid Naqsh-i-Jihán Square, and replaced the Tálár-i-Tavílih (see the first extract in Section Nine below) as the monarch's principal audience hall. It was supposedly so named because of the reflections created by its twenty pillars in a pool of water.

"His accomplice, Mír Muhammad-Husayn, surnamed the 'She-Serpent,' whom Bahá'u'lláh described as one 'infinitely more wicked than the oppressor of Karbilá,' was, about that same time, expelled from Isfahán, wandered from village to village, contracted a disease that engendered so foul an odour that even his wife and daughter could not bear to approach him, and died in such ill-favour with the local authorities that no one dared to attend his funeral, his corpse being ignominiously interred by a few porters."---*God Passes By*, pp. 232-233

i.e. he died, returning to that place which his deeds had made his natural dwelling in the life to come.

i.e. dissimulation of one's faith as a means of escaping persecution. Colonel 'Ináyatu'lláh-i-Suhráb here bluntly states what had been more delicately treated of in the foregoing account of Áqá Mírzá Ismá'íl.

Referred to in Section Two above as "Mírzá Taqí."

Referred to in Section Two above as "Mírzá Muhammad-Shafí'-i-Khú'í."

i.e. of the notebook of Colonel 'Ináyatu'lláh-i-Suhráb's reminiscences, not of the present volume.*

i.e. the period of Qájár rule, before the Iranian Constitutional Revolution of 1905-11.

A colloquial pronunciation of "Siyyid *Ján*," meaning "*Dear* Siyyid," or "Siyyid *Love*."

Year commencing 30 November 1913.

Presumably the same encounter to which 'Abdu'l-Bahá refers in His account of Shamsu'd-Duhá.*

It goes without saying that Zillu's-Sultán was directly involved in the martyrdom of the Twin Shining Lights; as for why he spoke in this manner before His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá, it was in order to exonerate himself in His sacred presence, imagining that by such falsehoods he could represent himself as innocent, heedless that the One True God has knowledge of all realities and matters. (Ishráq-Khávarí) Against this assertion, however, must be represented that 'Abdu'l-Bahá, in His account of the martyrdom of the King and the Beloved of Martyrs cited in Section One above, states: "Then the Sháh joined forces with those two wild animals \the Wolf and the She-Serpent\; and he commanded that the blood of both brothers, the King of Martyrs and the Beloved of Martyrs, be spilled out." In fact, according to this account, it was precisely the *Sháh* who caused the original sentence (which 'Abdu'l-Bahá says had been pronounced only against the King of Martyrs), to be broadened to include the Beloved of Martyrs as well.*

i.e., presumably, instructing the prince to transfer them from ecclesiastical to civil custody.*

sc. "other than to put you to death."

i.e., presumably, the Tálár-i-Tavílih, mentioned in Mírzá Abu'l-Fadl's account in Section Nine below.*

Zillu's-Sultán here refers to his father familiarly as "Daddy Sháh."

The author here likens the Twin Shining Lights to "Al-Hasanayn," or the "Two Hasans:" namely, Al-Hasan and Al-Husayn, the sons of Fátimih and the Imám 'Alí, respectively the second and third Imáms.

The invocation is such as in Shí'ite Muslim usage customarily accompanies mention of the Imáms.

Agreeably with a figure frequently encountered in the Bahá'í Holy Writings, 'Abdu'l-Bahá here equivocates upon the addressee's given name of Sádiq, signifying "truthful," "sincere," and the like.

i.e. a euphemism for the former dwelling-place of the Twin Shining Lights.

Year commencing 22 March 1943.

Her services in Írán are encapsulated in the following extract from *God Passes By*: "Many and diverse forces combined now to urge the American Bahá'í community to strong action: ... the tenacity and self-sacrifice of the fearless and brilliant Keith Ransom-Kehler, the first American martyr, who, journeying to Persia had pleaded in numerous interviews with ministers, ecclesiastics and government officials the cause of her down-trodden brethren in that land, had addressed no less than seven petitions to the Sháh, and, heedless of the warnings of age and ill-health, had at last succumbed in Isfahán."---p. 398

6 October 1932.

i.e., presumably, in the portion of that cemetery reserved for Bahá'í burials.*

"After more than a year of intense teaching work in Persia, ... our precious Keith left this world under so tragic circumstances that she was given by the Guardian the title of the first western martyr on Persian soil, and was raised by him to the station of one of the Hands of the Cause."---Letter on behalf of the Guardian dated 26 November 1933, published in *Dawn of a New Day*, pp. 45-46. A fuller account of her life may be found in Barron Harper's monograph on the Hands of the Cause, *Lights of Fortitude*.

i.e. the same daughter of Áqá Mírzá Muhammad-Sádiq, son of the Beloved of Martyrs, who has been mentioned in the foregoing.

i.e. the actual graves themselves (for the original expression might be taken in a metaphorical sense).

In order to visit the martyrs' resting-place in a large cemetery such as Takhtih-Fúlád, it would be necessary first to *know* generally in what quarter it was situated; having ascertained this, to *find* it amidst a host of other graves; and finally to thread one's way between them in order to *come before* it.

The opening passage of the Visitation Tablet appears in fact to be addressed to the whole company of the martyrs of Isfahán, who are then addressed separately in subsequent passages of the Tablet.*

See Qur'án, 2.156.

(13 February 1879), a month before the King and the Beloved of Martyrs: "In Isfahán Mullá Kázim was beheaded by order of Shaykh Muhammad-Báqir, and a horse made to gallop over his corpse, which was then delivered to the flames, while Siyyid Áqá Ján had his ears cut off, and was led by a halter through the streets and bazaars."---*God Passes By*, p. 200. An account of his martyrdom is provided in Section Seven below.

sc. in response to His primordial Call, figuratively repeated down the ages by each successive Manifestation, and voiced most recently by the tongue of Bahá'u'lláh, "Am I not your Lord?"

To sum up, the Visitation Tablet appears to comprise separate sections addressed to: the martyrs of Isfahán in general (followed by the entreaty for fulfilment of needs); Hasan and his brother Husayn, in that order; Mullá Kázim; Husayn; Hasan; Hasan and Husayn without distinction; Hasan. Thus construed, the Tablet both begins and ends with passages addressed to Hasan, extolled for his fidelity.*

i.e. Hasan, Husayn and Kázim.

Cf. Qur'án, 77.10.

Note that the following Visitation Tablet has been excerpted from a much larger Tablet, included in *Áthár-i-Qalam-i-A'lá*, Vol. II, pp. 76-101 (pp. 91-94). Since the text there differs in some important respects from that provided in *Núrayn-i-Nayyirayn*, the latter should be subjected to careful scrutiny.*

It is not at present known for whom this Tablet of Visitation was revealed.*

"The hamstringing of the She-Camel goes back to Qur'án 7:71; 11.67; 54: 27, etc. The She-Camel was a sign of God, the proof of the Prophet Sálih's mission. The reference ... is to an act of blasphemy."---Marzieh Gail, Introduction to the Epistle to the Son of the Wolf, p. xv.

"Will (*Mashíyyat* ) ... Purpose (*Irádat*) ... Destiny (*Qadar*)": reference to a Muslim Tradition, often cited in the Bahá'í Writings, which states: "Naught existeth in the heavens or on the earth save on account of seven: Will, Purpose, Decree, Destiny (*Qadá'*), Permission (*Idhn*), Term (*Ajal* ), Book (*Kitáb*)."

It is hard to determine whether this closing salutation forms part of the actual Visitation Prayer, or belongs rather to the surrounding Tablet addressed to the unidentified "friends."*

The signature is followed in other versions of the Tablet by the date 2 August 1919.*

The Tablet contains no direct indication that it is addressed to the Beloved of Martyrs: the appellation "Name of God," as distinct from "My Name Há'," might indeed suggest that it is addressed rather to the subsequently mentioned Khalíl. (Cf. for example the following directions at the beginning of a Tablet of 'Abdu'l-Bahá quoted on p. 57 of Dr. Dhabíhu'lláh 'Azízí's *Táj-i-Vahhá*j: "Tihrán. By the hand of the son of his honour the *Name of God*, Ustád 'Azíz, scion of the august *Khalíl*, upon him be the Glory of God ")*

The referent of the name is not at present known. It could be, for example: i) the Beloved of Martyrs himself, in view of his being destined, like Abraham, the Friend of God (Khalíl) to be cast as it were into a pit of fire; ii) his father, whose given name was Ibráhím, the Arabic form of Abraham (especially considering that, as the author states in Section One, he received numerous Tablets from Bahá'u'lláh); or iii) one of the other believers, such as the famous Bahá'í teacher, Muhammad-Ibráhím-i-Qazvíní.*

The figure of shattering idols is suggested by the Qur'ánic story of Ibráhím.

i.e. like His prototype Joseph.

Cf. The Kitáb-i-Aqdas, Paragraph 36: "By the righteousness of the one true God! Were anyone to wash the feet of all mankind, and were he to worship God in the forests, valleys, and mountains, upon high hills and lofty peaks, to leave no rock or tree, no clod of earth, but was a witness to his worship -- yet, should the fragrance of My good pleasure not be inhaled from him, his works would never be acceptable unto God."

i.e. an indication, whether written or spoken, of the good pleasure of the Manifestation of God. In one of His Tablets, Bahá'u'lláh says: "O Murtadá! Upon thee be the Glory of God, the Lord of all mankind. The Herald of this Most Great Revelation saith: 'All is to the end of being mentioned in His Court, though it be but once.' In this day, the treasures of the earth cannot compare with a single word expressive of good pleasure that hath emanated from the Pen of the Most High. Blessed be those souls who have been favoured with such a word, and become possessed of a Divine Tablet."---*La'áli'u'l-Hikmah*, Vol. III, p. 352

i.e. his state of ideal, constructive martyrdom was crowned by an actual martyrdom in the flesh. Cf. the following passage of similar import from the Writings of Bahá'u'lláh: "In this day, service to the Cause is among the greatest of all deeds. Well-assured souls must, with perfect wisdom, occupy themselves in teaching the Cause of God, so that the fragrances of the Divine garment may be diffused in all regions. Such testimony is not limited to martyrdom and the shedding of one's blood, *for it may chance that a person, although living in comfortable circumstances, is still registered in the Book of Him Who is the Lord of all names as a martyr.*"---quoted in *Payám-i-Ásimání*, Vol. I, p. 24

i.e. that of the "true," or living, martyrdom.

i.e. "O Thou Manifestation of the Godhead in His character of the Most Merciful!" It will be seen that Bahá'u'lláh is almost indistinguishably merged with the Godhead throughout this prayer.

The martyrdom of the King and the Beloved of Martyrs occurred on 17 March 1879, and thus would still have been a recent event, and the news of it yet more so, on the first day of Ridván that year.

Reminiscent again of the Prayer of Nudbih.

i.e. in respect alike of their relation to the Truth and of their interaction with each other, a state affirmed in the Qur'án to be typical of the oppressors and the unbelievers.

Bahá'u'lláh appears to apply the familiar term -- so intimately associated in the Bahá'í Revelation with the idea of a garden -- to Isfahán, perhaps in reference to the many gardens (*ríyád*) for which the city is renowned. As previously noted, the modern cemetery of Isfahán has been named precisely the Bágh-i-Ridván (the Garden of Ridván). Alternatively, He may simply be *personifying* the Festival of Ridván, for, as mentioned, it was around this season that the martyrdom of the Twin Shining Lights occurred.*

The invocation derives from the shortly-to-be-quoted passage of the Qayyúmu'l-Asmá', suggesting that the present apostrophe to Bahá'u'lláh, notwithstanding that it is ostensibly attributed to the "Ridván" of Isfahán, proceeds in reality from the Báb. "Out of utter nothingness, *O great and omnipotent Master,* Thou hast, through the celestial potency of Thy might, brought me forth and raised me up to proclaim this Revelation. I have made none other but Thee my trust; I have clung to no will but Thy will... *O Thou Remnant of God! I have sacrificed myself wholly for Thee: I have accepted curses for Thy sake, and have yearned for naught but martyrdom in the path of Thy love.* Sufficient witness unto me is God, the Exalted, the Protector, the Ancient of Days."---*The Dispensation of Bahá'u'lláh,* p. 101

The possessive adjective is capitalized on the assumption that the speaker may be the Báb.*

The name and identity of the recipient of the Tablet are not at present known.*

A different version of this Visitation Tablet, omitting the previous and following paragraphs, but including further matter after that next-but-one following, is given at the end of Section Three above. Both versions should be carefully checked against a reliable copy.*

i.e. the actual graves themselves (for the original expression might be taken in a metaphorical sense).

In order to visit the martyrs' resting-place in a large cemetery such as Takhtih-Fúlád, it would be necessary first to *know* generally in what quarter it was situated; having ascertained this, to *find* it amidst a host of other graves; and finally to thread one's way between them in order to *come before* it.

The opening passage of the Visitation Tablet appears in fact to be addressed to the whole company of the martyrs of Isfahán (who are then addressed separately in the previous version of the Tablet).*

The visitant is here given the opportunity to beseech the fulfilment of some personal need.

Bahá'u'lláh here plays on the literal meaning of the addressee's name, or title, of Khalíl ("Friend, Companion") which, as noted, is a designation of the Prophet Abraham. The referent of the name is not at present known. Possibly the famous Bahá'í teacher, Muhammad-Ibráhím-i-Qazvíní, surnamed "Khalíl."*

Reminiscent of the passage of similar import in a preceding Tablet: "Did the people think that they had gained by their transaction? Nay, by Him Who is the King of Names! God is My witness that they suffered a loss such as hath had no equal in the knowledge of God, the Lord of the throne above and of earth below. The Tongue of Grandeur testifieth that it is thou who hast gained by this transaction. Nay by My life! All gain is thine, inasmuch as thou hast quit the earth, whose every atom beareth witness unto its evanescence; hast set thy face towards the dawning-place of eternity; and hast yielded thy life in thy yearning for the love of God, the Creator of the heavens and the Lord of all names."

Cf. the famous words of Hugh Latimer to Nicholas Ridley, as both were about to be burned at the stake outside Balliol College, Oxford, for heresy: "Be of good comfort, Master Ridley, and play the man: *we shall this day this light such a candle, by God's grace, in England, as I trust shall never be put out.*"

As is frequently the case in these Tablets, it is not made clear which of the two martyrs is intended.*

Used in the Bahá'í Writings as a generic term for the prison-city of 'Akká: "'Know thou,' Bahá'u'lláh, wishing to emphasize the criticalness of the first nine years of His banishment to that prison-city, has written, 'that upon Our arrival at this Spot, We chose to designate it as the "Most Great Prison." Though previously subjected in another land (Tihrán) to chains and fetters, We yet refused to call it by that name. Say: Ponder thereon, O ye endued with understanding!'"---*God Passes By,* p. 185

It is good,' he remarked, 'to live under the sky, in the sunshine and fresh air.'"---*'Abdu'l-Bahá in London*, p. 81

The Tablet bears no obvious reference to the martyrs of Isfahán, albeit a connection may perhaps be detected in its theme of *generosity*: "A month later occurred in that same city the tragedy of the two famous brothers Mírzá Muhammad-Hasan and Mírzá Muhammad-Husayn, the 'Twin Shining Lights,' respectively surnamed 'Sultánu'sh-Shuhadá' (King of Martyrs) and 'Mahbúbu'sh-Shuhadá' (Beloved of Martyrs), *who were celebrated for their generosity, trustworthiness, kindliness and piety.*"---*God Passes By*, p. 200 *

sc. with the virtue of generosity.

i.e. on the grounds that if the poverty be not "conspicuous," then there is no need to give.

i.e. the time when the performance of goodly deeds is most likely to yield an abundant harvest of results. "Man is like unto a tree: if he be adorned with fruit, then is he worthy of praise and commendation; otherwise a tree without fruit is fit for the fire. The fruits of the tree of man are most delicate, agreeable and well-pleasing. A praiseworthy character, goodly deeds and a kindly word are accounted among the fruits of this tree. The springtime of trees in the physical world occurreth, as is witnessed, but once a year; *whereas the springtime of the tree of man is the days of the revelation of the One True God.* If, during this Divine springtime, the trees of existence become adorned with the aforesaid fruits, then will the lights of the sun of justice assuredly encompass the whole earth, and all will behold themselves detached and at rest beneath the shadow of His Holiness the Adored One "---*Payám-i-Ásimání*, Vol. I, p. 27

To summarize, then, the Tablet, although addressed to Shaykh Kázim-i-Samandar, contains subsidiary addresses to: Samandar's paternal uncle Muhammad-Rahím, Ibráhím, 'Alí, Muhammad-'Alí, Hasan Khán, and Husayn; as well as notices of (between the addresses to 'Alí and Muhammad-'Alí) the consort of 'Abdu'r-Rahím and 'Abdu'r-Rahím himself (including in the latter case a Visitation Tablet), and, at the very end of the Tablet, another Muhammad-'Alí -- that is, ten individuals all told.

The language of the Tablet here changes from Arabic to Persian, providing an occasion for the substance of the preceding to be briefly summarized before the principal matter of the Tablet is introduced.

i.e. the Twin Shining Lights.

Cf. the similar statements in two previous Tablets: i) "... the Hand of Divine power raised them up and arrayed them with the robe of honour; and from the cloud of bounty blessings rained down upon them, ...;" and ii) "... yet the Hand of Divine power raised them up; arrayed them with the robe of honour; and from the heaven of bounty rained down blessings upon them, ..."

Cf. the statements in the same aforesaid two previous Tablets: i) "What greater bounty is there than this, that, praise be to God, in the nether world they were identified with the name of the True One, it being observed that even the ungodly behaved with deference in their presence; while at the end of their days they attained a station whose description is beyond aught that the pen of this servant could compass, or the tongue of this evanescent one express?" and ii) "Consider now: praise be to God, so long as they lived, they were seen to occupy, through the loving-kindness of the One True God, a position of the utmost honour among His servants, love for them indeed having been instilled into people's hearts to a degree that none save God, the True One, hath ever been, or is now, able to reckon; while at the end of their days they attained unto that Supreme Sacrifice which they themselves had sought."

sc. and not out of sorrow over the incident *per se*, which would betray a lack of true reliance.

Cf. the statements in two previous Tablets: i) "Subsequent to their return to the Land of Sád, two letters came from them to the Most Holy Court, and likewise other letters. Therein, with utmost lowliness and ardour, they craved, and expressed the hope of attaining, the very station which afterwards they won." and ii) "After their return from the Most Holy Court unto the Land of Sád, they despatched hither two letters wherein, with utmost lowliness and ardour, they besought of Him Who is the All-Possessing, the Most High, the very thing which actually befell."

Cf. the statement in an earlier Tablet: "Although this calamity is most grievous, and the greatest abasement hath been witnessed on its account, yet, by the righteousness of God, is it in reality the mightiest honour, which hath but outwardly assumed the form of an abasement." also Bahá'u'lláh's advice on p. 29 of the Epistle to the Son of the Wolf: "Say, O friends! Strive that haply the tribulations suffered by this Wronged One and by you, in the path of God, may not prove to have been in vain."

Glancing back again at the backs, eyes and ears mentioned in the fourth preceding sentence.

Reinforcing the idea that afflictions and the remembrance of God are alike instances of His bounty.

i.e. the *namesake* of the martyr, who is the addressee of this concluding portion of the Tablet to Varqá.

i.e., presumably, Husayn the Beloved of Martyrs; quoted in the Tablet to Mírzá Siyyid Muhammad the Afnán above. As there explained, this Word was uttered in preceding Dispensations (most notably, in the Arabic form in which it is here cited, by the Prophet Muhammad), and has thus been *re-revealed* in the Bahá'í Dispensation: "Although this Word is ancient -- meaning that the Manifestations of the Cause spake it of old -- yet today is it made manifest in a wondrous new attire, inasmuch as the true Isráfíl hath breathed a fresh spirit of meaning into the temples of words. It is this: 'Whoso is for God, God is for him.'"*

As previously noted, an emblem of superiority and success (from the ancient Arabian game of *maysir*).

13 February 1879.

According to the Eastern usage, the fortieth day after someone's passing is held to mark the end of the period of official mourning: "During the week following His \sc. 'Abdu'l-Bahá's\ passing, from fifty to a hundred of the poor of Haifa were daily fed at His house, whilst on the seventh day corn was distributed in His memory to about a thousand of them irrespective of creed or race. *On the fortieth day* an impressive memorial feast was held in His memory, to which over six hundred of the people of Haifa, 'Akká and the surrounding parts of Palestine and Syria, including officials and notables of various religions and races, were invited. More than one hundred of the poor were also fed on that day."---*God Passes By,* p. 313

Hájí Mírzá Haydar-'Alí sets the number of martyrs rather at 70: "Then as the Friends of God proceeded to their homes, the malicious multitude attacked them on the streets, capturing and beating them. Whomever they found, they tortured and wounded, in all seventy people, killing some outright. // Siyyid Abu'l-Qásim-i- Márnání was one of the martyrs. This beautiful soul was about eighty years of age, and during his life was renowned for his piety, faith, knowledge and virtue; even his enemies bore record to his chastity and sanctity, testifying to his generosity and charity. He was in truth a help to the poor, refuge and support for the suffering and afflicted; his word was influential, honoured and accepted by all the people. When they assaulted him he cried out: 'Yá Bahá'u'l-Abhá! We are from God and unto Him we are returning,' and in the very moment of his expiration he called out in a loud voice with great joy and exultation these words: 'You have done us no harm! You are only transmitting us to our Lord! Yá Bahá'u'l-Abhá!' // While his tormentors, like wild beasts, were stoning him and chopping him with axes, this single, helpless man of such venerable years, this holy soul, under the weapons of the persecutors, wounding and cursing him, suffered it all with a fortitude of grandeur and majesty, facing the Kingdom of Light as though he felt not a blow nor received a wound, but was rather perceiving the open gates of the Supreme Concourse, and yielded his life to his Beloved with utmost joy, happiness and exhilaration. // O that I had been with him to partake of his joy and to attain his great happiness!"---from the account of "recent martyrdoms in Persia, up to the present time" written by the author "in compliance to the holy command of 'Abdu'l-Bahá;" issued by the Bahá'í Publishing Trust in Chicago in Persian in 1904, and thereafter in an English translation by Dr. Yúnis Khán-i-Afrúkhtih in 1917, and currently available on the "Bahá'í Library Online" website under the title "Bahá'í Martyrdoms in Persia in the Year 1903 A.D."

See Qur'án, 62.5. The quotation differs slightly from the wording of the original, which reads: "*The likeness of* those who were charged with the Torah, then bore it not, *is as that of* an ass laden with books ..." The sense appears to be in general that those who burden themselves with punctilious observance of the outward forms of religion, without however comprehending its informing spirit and animating purpose, are like nothing so much as brute unreasoning beasts of burden freighted down with books.

This in conformity with the Qur'ánic advice to pass by mockery and ridicule in dignified silence: "... and those who bear not false witness, and who, if they pass by raillery, do so with dignity" (25.71); cf. also 23.3 and 28.55.

i.e. if He can raise the She-Camel of Sálih, a mere beast, to so high a station, then assuredly He can do the same in the case of the King and the Beloved of Martyrs, whose martyrdom has been explicitly likened in previously quoted Tablets to the hamstringing of that creature by the Prophet's people Thamúd.

A Tablet was revealed in honour of Abu'l-Fadá'il, author of the work *Fará'id*, in the form of a prayer from the pen of His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá -- may the life of all else be a sacrifice unto Him. *His most sweet utterance:* "Alláh-u-Abhá! O Thou Glory of the Most Glorious! I have wakened on this Naw-Rúz morning to find the lights of Thy holiness gleaming from all regions, the verses of Thy unity recited by the tongues of all things, and the tokens of Thy singleness evident in the scroll of the Book of Creation. Blessed is he who chanteth them in such accents as to fill with ecstasy the Concourse on high, and to reach the ears of the dwellers of the Abhá Kingdom. Glorified then be my Lord, the Most High! When, O my God, I had taken my station facing the Spot around which circle those that are nigh unto Thee, lo, there was before me a written letter, a guarded tablet, and an outspread roll containing cogent proofs, clear testimonies and lucid evidences in refutation of one who had striven to repudiate Thy truth. O God! Assist Thou its author through the confirmations of Thine Abhá Kingdom; strengthen his loins by Thine almighty power; cause him to utter Thy praises in great assemblies; and make him Thy most mighty sign, a compelling proof in vindication of Thy Cause among all peoples, a resplendent sign in the world of creation, a standard raised above the pavilions of sublimest glory, a matchless pearl and a peerless jewel glittering in the diadem of loftiness. O my Lord! Illumine his countenance with the lights shining from the Abhá Kingdom, and the rays dawning from the Most Exalted Horizon, inasmuch as he hath served Thy Cause, published Thy testimony, manifested Thine evidence, elucidated Thy path, and adorned the leaves of utterance with the verses of Thy unity. Thou, verily, art the Bestower, the Compassionate. 'Ayn 'Ayn" Abu'l-Fadá'il was born in the year 1260 A.H. \c. 1844\, and became a believer in the Blessed Cause in the year of the revelation of the Lawh-i-Ra'ís and the Lawh-i-Káf-Zá' \Tablet of Kázim ([Shaykh Kázim-i-Samandar)\], which arrived in Írán by the hand of Dhabíh-i-Káshání, pursuant to witnessing the fulfilment of the matters foretold in these Tablets. He suffered innumerable vicissitudes, being imprisoned in 1300 A.H. \c. 1883\ along with the rest of the friends by order of Kámrán Mírzá, the governor of Tihrán, at which time strangers and enemies bore off all his possessions. Thereafter he embarked on a series of teaching trips, in the course of which he travelled widely, visiting many different places in Írán, Russia, Turkistán and Ádhirbáyján, and was for long engaged in diffusing the Divine fragrances in Bukhárá. In the end he settled down in Egypt, from whence, at the blessed behest of 'Abdu'l-Bahá, he undertook a trip to America. In this manner he remained occupied with service to the Cause of God until finally in Egypt, in the month of Safar, 1332 A.H. \c. January 1914\, he ascended to the Abhá Kingdom. At first he was buried in the house of Hájj Mírzá Hasan; then later his most pure corse was transferred to the Eternal Rose Garden of Egypt. His writings are well-known; his station is remembered before God; and he was the recipient of numerous Tablets from the pen of the Ancient Beauty, as well as of countless favours from His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá. After his passing, the late Fasíhu'l-Mulk, Shúrídiy-i-Shírází, who died in 1345 A.H. \1926-7\, and is accounted an eloquent writer and a honey-tongued poet alike of the reign of Násiri'd-Dín Sháh and of the more recent era, and who was moreover one of the faithful friends and believers in the Blessed Cause, composed the following verses concerning the date of Abu'l-Fadá'il's passing: "In one thousand three hundred and thirty-two * Bu'l-Fadl in Safar to the world beyond sped. // If the same in the Abjad count be expressed: * *Lo: Bu'l-Fadl beheld spring, breathed his last, then fled.*" (The final hemistich of the two couplets has been so ingeniously composed in Persian that the numerical values of its constituent letters according to the Abjad system add up precisely to the year of the subject's passing in 1332: "*Bu'l-Fadl bín kih díd bahárá-u-dád ján:*" \Bá' = 2\ + \Váv = 6\ + \Alif = 1\ + \Lám = 30\ + \Fá' = 80\ + \Dád = 800\ + \Lám = 30\ + \Bá' = 2\ + \Yá' = 10\ + \Nún = 50\ + \Káf = 20\ + \Há' = 5\ + \Dál = 4\ + \Yá' = 10\ + \Dál = 4\ + \Bá' = 2\ + \Há' = 5\ + \Alif = 1\ + \Rá' = 200\ + \Alif = 1\ + \Váv = 6\ + \Dál = 4\ + \Alif = 1\ + \Dál = 4\ + \Jím = 3\ + \Alif = 1\ + \Nún = 50\ = ***1332***. The same effect has also been produced in the English rendering, the numerical values of whose last hemistich add up to the equivalent in the Gregorian calendar of the year of Abu'l-Fadl's passing, as follows: "*Lo: Bu'l-Fadl beheld spring, breathed his last, then fled:*" \L = 30\ + \O = 60\ + \Β = 2\ + \U = 300\ + \L = 30\ + \F = 6\ + \Α = 1\ + \D = 4\ + \L = 30\ + \Β = 2\ + \Ε = 5\ + \Η = 8\ + \Ε = 5\ + \L = 30\ + \D = 4\ + \S = 100\ + \P = 70\ + \R = 90\ + \I = 9\ + \N = 50\ + \G = 7\ + \B = 2\ + \R = 90\ + \E = 5\ + \A = 1\ + \T = 200\ + \H = 8\ + \E = 5\ + \D = 4\ + \H = 8\ + \I = 9\ + \S = 100\ + \L = 30\ + \A = 1\ + \S = 100\ + \T = 200\ + \T = 200\ + \H = 8\ + \E = 5\ + \N = 50\ + \F = 6\ + \L = 30\ + \E + 5\ + \D = 4\ = ***1914***.) After the passing of Abu'l-Fadá'il, detailed descriptions of the greatness of his station flowed from the most pure tongue of His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá -- exalted be His praise -- which are recorded and available in their appropriate places. The late Malkum Khán, who was a well-known Iranian statesman, inquired of Abu'l-Fadá'il concerning the attempted shooting of Násiri'd-Dín Sháh by the Bábís, in reply to which the latter composed a lengthy treatise wherein he expatiated upon the history of the Faith and its blessed teachings. It was in this treatise that he provided his description of the martyrdom of the Twin Shining Lights. Since the subject of Mírzá Malkum Khán has arisen, it is pertinent to record that reference has been made to him in the Tablets issuing from the blessed pen of His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá; among them a Tablet concerning Malkum Khán in which He says: *His most sweet utterance:* "In this world thousands of statesmen have come and gone -- men who on earth were responsible for exceedingly important publications, but of whom no mention or trace now remains. Malkum Khán -- upon whom be God's mercy and good pleasure -- occupied himself for fifty years with political publications, filled Írán with the newspaper 'Qánún,' and became a catalyst for initiating new thoughts. In the end others entered the arena, raised a great tumult, and the efforts of fifty years, the homelessness, the wanderings, all came to this, that at the time of his final illness, however much he wrote and begged that at least one among his Iranian companions might be present at the time of his passing so as to hear that hapless wanderer's last wishes, it was not to be. Finally, in the utmost despair and hopelessness, and vexation with his friends, he passed away. Upon him be the mercy of God. In truth, the poor man was deprived of high position, the title 'Nizámu'd-Dawlih,' and glory and popularity in Írán, while in the political world he neither secured gain, nor reaped fruit, nor obtained that name which was the utmost object of his hopes. Disappointed and sorrowful, he departed from this world, while the Republicans one and all consider themselves greater than he ..." *Finis*. (Ishráq-Khávarí)

The same addressed by Bahá'u'lláh in the following Tablet: "*Revealed for Mullá 'Alíy-i-Kandí, who ruleth in Tá' \Tihrán*: He is the Avenger, Endued with Mighty Power: O 'Alí! Muhammad the Apostle of God hath wept at thy cruelty, inasmuch as thou hast followed thy passions, and eschewed the path of guidance. Rejoice not at that which thou hast wrought: thy Lord, truly, lieth in wait. Thou hast pronounced judgment against him that believed in God in this day whereon thy face, and the faces of such as have broken the Covenant, have been darkened. The Announcer came unto you, and announced unto you the tidings of this Revelation whereby the world's horizons have been illumined; yet ye turned back from Him, even as those before you turned back when the Sun of Knowledge shone forth from the horizon of the Hijáz. God laid hold upon them for their iniquity, and left them as a sign unto all that are endowed with insight. Thinkest thou thyself a guide unto the people? Nay, by Him Who is the Lord of Lords! At thy bidding hath been shed the blood of those through whom the winds have wafted, and the streams have flowed. The Holy Spirit hath bewailed them; yea, the thunder hath called out, and the cloud shed forth its tears. Fear God, and bear not thyself haughtily before Him that created thee at His bidding! Return thou unto Him lowly and repentant. Rejoicest thou at the affliction that hath overtaken Us? We have accepted it in the path of God, and, undismayed by the swords brandished against Us, summon His servants unto the Promised Day. All the peoples of the earth are powerless to prevent Us from fulfilling that whereunto thy Lord, the All-Powerful, the All-Compelling, hath commanded Us. Shall the dominion of the people suffice to overawe Us? Nay, by the Self of Him Who is the Sovereign Truth, even should all who are invested with power and authority rise up against Us! Hearken thou unto the words of Him that counselleth thee: shouldst thou indeed hearken, then for thine own behoof shalt thou have done so; and shouldst thou turn aside -- He, verily, is the All-Possessing, the Most High."---*Áthár-i-Qalam-i-A'lá*, Vol. 1, p. 372

Who dwelt in this world for more than seventy years, and, after accepting the Blessed Cause, rendered befitting services to the Faith of God. After the passing of His Holiness 'Abdu'l-Bahá, He undertook at the behest of his Holiness the Guardian of the Cause of God a further teaching trip to America. He compiled, after thirty years of diligent efforts and exertions to acquire the necessary documents and references, a history of the most exalted Cause. Finally, in this present year of 1336 A.H.S. \c. 1957\, whilst occupied with teaching the Cause in the town of Khurramshahr, in the province of Khúzistán, he ascended to the Abhá Kingdom. It is evident from all foregoing that the King of Martyrs and Beloved of Martyrs occupy in the Bahá'í Faith a station precisely comparable to that assigned in Islám to the Prince of Martyrs, if not yet higher.

Renowned too *despite* and *thanks to*, this affiliation: cf. the following passages deriving respectively from the Tablet to Mírzá Siyyid Muhammad, known as Áqá Mírzá Áqá, Núri'd-Dín; the Lawh-i-Kitábu's-Sidq (Tablet of the Book of Truth); and the second Tablet to Varqá -- all in Section Five above: i) "What greater bounty is there than this, that, praise be to God, in the nether world they were identified with the name of the True One, it being observed that even the ungodly behaved with deference in their presence; while at the end of their days they attained a station whose description is beyond aught that the pen of this servant could compass, or the tongue of this evanescent one express?" ii) "Consider now: praise be to God, so long as they lived, they were seen to occupy, through the loving-kindness of the One True God, a position of the utmost honour among His servants, love for them indeed having been instilled into people's hearts to a degree that none save God, the True One, hath ever been, or is now, able to reckon; while at the end of their days they attained unto that Supreme Sacrifice which they themselves had sought." iii) "They attained a station such that even their enemies testified to the loftiness of their rank; while at the end of their days they attained the most sublime and lofty of all stations, which is that of Supreme Sacrifice."

The correct date is 28 Jamádíyu'th-Thání 1269 (8 April 1853): see *God Passes By*, p. 109.*

The period of Bahá'u'lláh's sojourn in Baghdád cannot be accounted longer than 11 years, whether in the solar or the lunar calendars.*

Cf. the author's words in Section Three above: "Remarkably, in the famine of 1288, which afflicted the people of Írán after the martyrdom of Badí', these two brothers had shown the utmost kindness towards the people of Isfahán in general, saving a great multitude from starvation and hunger. In particular, they had lavished much kindness and assistance on a person by the name of Ramadán, who was the executioner. Yet still the people were prepared to countenance so grievous a wrong against their twin benefactors."
