The emphasis which, in the Tablet to the King of Martyrs "Containing an Exhortation to Teach the Cause" cited in Section Four above, is placed on the importance of doing so *with wisdom* might seem to indicate -- what is also here suggested -- a certain tendency to pay insufficient regard to this point.

Cf. the following passages of similar import occurring in the Tablets respectively to Khalíl; to the "leaf of My Divine Lote Tree;" and to Javád -- all in Section Five above: i) "From time immemorial even until now, the Divine Lote Tree, which the hand of God's sovereign purpose hath planted in the good and blessed land, hath been watered by the blood of them that are wholly devoted to His Cause, and truly recognize His unity." ii) "Although outwardly this matter seareth the heart, yet is it, and shall it ever be, the means and cause of the growth of the Divine Lote Tree." and iii) "The groaning and lamentation of the Pen of the Most High have been, and ever shall be, in token of His loving-kindness, His grace, and His fidelity; for otherwise the Divine Lote Tree is exalted through the blood of His chosen ones." Cf. also in the same vein: iv) "Verily I say: Whatever befalleth in the path of God is the beloved of the soul and the desire of the heart. Deadly poison in His path is pure honey, and every tribulation a draught of crystal water. In the Tablet to His Majesty the Sháh it is written: 'By Him Who is the Truth! I fear no tribulation in His path, nor any affliction in My love for Him. *Verily God hath made adversity as a morning dew upon His green pasture, and a wick for His lamp which lighteth earth and heaven.*'"---Epistle to the Son of the Wolf, p. 17; v) "Ours is the duty to raise the call of the New Day and to proclaim this Divine Message unto all people. Many a soul will, in this city \Tihrán\, shed his blood in this path. That blood will water the Tree of God, will cause it to flourish, and to overshadow all mankind."--- Mullá Husayn's parting words to Mullá Muhammad-i-Mu'allim of Núr before setting out for Khurásán.---*The Dawn Breakers*, p. 108; vi) "In the blood of the unnumbered martyrs of Persia lay the seed of the Divinely-appointed Administration which, though transplanted from its native soil, is now budding out, under your loving care, into a new order, destined to overshadow all mankind."---*The World Order of Bahá'u'lláh*, p. 52; vii) "Great is the blessedness of those whose blood Thou hast chosen wherewith to water the Tree of Thine affirmation, and thus to exalt Thy holy and immutable Word."---*Selections from the Writings of the Báb*, p. 190

Cf. the opening passage of the book: "Hasan attained unto a twofold martyrdom, for throughout the days of his life he was selfless in the Will of the One True God. Whosoever hath assimilated his will and purpose to those of his Lord and Master hath, verily, attained unto true martyrdom. He attained likewise unto an outward martyrdom. Blessed be he and his brother, and he who was called Kázim." also the following from the Tablet to 'Alí in Section Five above: "He had attained unto the true martyrdom. By My life! Years passed over him during which he was wholly selfless in the Will of his Lord, and in the Purpose of His Master. In no respect had he any thought, purpose, or will of his own; and at all times did he wait upon the Command of God. Without the leave and permission of the True One he embarked upon no enterprise. For years indeed he had already been a sacrifice in the path of the Beloved, although to outward seeming he spoke, heard, and walked like others. This is the Supreme Sacrifice, the Most Sublime Station, and the Most August Seat; and this exalted and most glorious station was crowned with outward martyrdom."

A proverbial expression indicating that a fair-seeming form may disguise a venomous interior.

i.e. the "hubs" of that system of *haválih* transfers mentioned in Section Three above.

Colonel 'Ináyatu'lláh-i-Suhráb attributes the seizure in his reminiscences, quoted in Section Three above (see under the heading "Wealth"), rather to the *government*: "It is known that on the very day of their martyrdom there arrived in Isfahán a caravan laden with their wares, which, after it had proceeded to the Maydán-i-Naqsh-i-Jihán, known as the Maydán-i-Sháh, and its ownership been ascertained, was confiscated in its entirety by the government."*

The author states rather in Section Three above that these telegrams were sent to the Sháh: "The merchants of Isfahán ... who had transactions and dealings with those two wronged ones, and were anxious to preserve their investments, sent numerous telegrams to the Sháh of Írán, Násiri'd-Dín Sháh."*

Followed, according to the author's account in Section Three above, by numerous others: "A telegram came from the Sháh to Zillu's-Sultán instructing him to send the prisoners to Tihrán. ... Successive telegrams arrived for the governor from the Sháh, conveying in respect of the prisoners a definite recommendation that under no circumstances should they be harmed."*

Again, the main clause of the sentence is much delayed, commencing after numerous intervening subordinate clauses with the words "yet the Wolf and the She-Serpent."

i.e. the said part, not the whole sum (see the author's account in Section Three).

Since this article was written in America, the word "dollar," denoting the American currency, has been used in place of "túmán." As regards their respective valuations, the túmán stood in those days towards the dollar in a ratio of two to three, meaning that nine thousand túmáns \the sum specified in Section Three above\ were the equivalent of thirteen thousand dollars. (Ishráq-Khávarí)

The comment helps to clarify why precisely it is the younger brother who is designated as the *King* of Martyrs. One may perhaps also infer from it that he was the principal in the trading concern.

Recalling the notion expressed at various points in the preceding that martyrdom is regarded, among other things, as the highest expression of the quality of *fidelity*: the King of Martyrs, for example, appears to have been designated the "King *and* Letter of Fidelity."

Cf. the author's words in Section Three above: "In particular, they had lavished much kindness and assistance on a person by the name of Ramadán, who was the executioner;" also the following passage from Colonel 'Ináyatu'lláh-i-Suhráb's reminiscences of the Twin Shining Lights, quoted in that same section, concerning specifically their generosity: "It is known that the assistance of the two brothers was extended without distinction to all people, including even Ramadán the executioner, who was afterwards compelled to put them to death. For a while Ramadán sought by various pretexts to put off the deed, considering himself to be indebted to the two brothers for various favours they had rendered him."

According to Áqá Husayn-'Alí Núr, as cited in the aforesaid *Khátirát-i-Muhájirí az Isfahán*, the burial spot lay close to Isfahán's renowned Sí-u-Sih Pul ("Thirty-Three\-Arch\ Bridge"). This impressive structure, originally commissioned by Sháh 'Abbás I, remains to this day a major attraction of the city.

It is not at present known who was the author of this hemistich.*

Cf. Bahá'u'lláh's words in His Tablet to Khalíl quoted in Section Five above: "They have lit a fire whose blaze shall not be extinguished in the world."

*A Year Amongst the Persians: Impressions as to the Life, Character, & Thought of the People of Persia Received during Twelve Months' Residence in that Country in the Year 1887-1888*, by Edward Granville Browne, first published by Messrs A & C Black Ltd 1893.

For the sake of verisimilitude to the original, these extracts are rendered from the author's Persian.*

That concerning Tihrán.

See Section Seven above.

Both settlements in the vicinity of Isfahán: the former, as mentioned in Section Three above, was the birthplace of the eminent Bahá'í poet Na'ím-i-Sidihí; the latter, as mentioned in Section Seven above, was the birthplace of the "fourth" Bahá'í martyr of Isfahán, Mírzá Ashraf-i-Ábádi'í.

Moojan Momen, in an article on the Bahá'í history of Isfahán, states: "In the villages around Isfahán there were also numerous episodes of persecution and some martyrdoms: in Najaf-Ábád in 1864, 1889, 1899, and 1910; in Sidih in 1890; and in Zavárih in 1926."

The same several times referred to in Section Eight above.

Moojan Momen, in an article on the Bahá'í history of Yazd, assigns this episode rather to 1891: "From about 1290/1873 onwards the persecutions intensified. *The first culmination of this was the episode of the Seven Martyrs of Yazd in 1891.*"*

This and the previous sentence seem to misrepresent the sense of the corresponding passage in *A Year Amongst the Persians*, which is rather that the last Bábí to be killed by the Sháh's order was Mírzá Badí', whose death he decreed after the latter had delivered into his hands Bahá'u'lláh's Epistle to him, the Lawh-i-Sultán. But such a claim is itself inconsistent with 'Abdu'l-Bahá's account of the life of Shamsu'd-Duhá, quoted in Section One above, in which He definitely assigns responsibility for the martyrdom of the Twin Shining Lights to the Sháh: "Then the Sháh joined forces with those two wild animals; *and he commanded that the blood of both brothers, the King of Martyrs and the Beloved of Martyrs, be spilled out*."*

That concerning Isfahán.

A. L. M. Nicolas, 1864-1937.

Presumably *Siyyid 'Alí-Muhammad dit le Báb*, quoted on numerous occasions in *The Dawn-Breakers*.

Literally, "the land of the Franks:" i.e. in general Europe and the West.

Identified in H. M. Hasan Balyuzi's *Edward Granville Browne and the Bahá'í Faith*, p. 11, as Hájí Mírzá Haydar-'Alí: "In Isfahán Browne met for an hour or two one of the most remarkable Bahá'ís of all times, Hájí Mírzá Haydar-'Alí, who had known nine years of banishment in Khartoum, first in chains and close confinement and later in relative freedom (during the governorship of Ismá'íl Páshá Ayyúb), until released by the arrival of General Gordon in 1877."

The signification, as noted, of "Mishkín-Qalam."

Reminiscent of the following incident related by the same author in his introduction to *A Traveller's Narrative Written to Illustrate the Episode of the Báb*: "Besides these a very old man with light blue eyes and white beard, whose green turban proclaimed him a descendant of the Prophet, advanced to welcome me, saying, 'We know not how we should greet thee, whether we should salute thee with "*as-selámu 'aleykum*" or with "*Alláhu abhá*."'"---pp. xxxvii-xxxviii

"God keep thee!"

The only children of Mishkín-Qalam mentioned in the present work are a daughter and a son: Sháh Begum and Mírzá 'Alí-Akbar.*

The scene is reminiscent of the circumstances in which, in Jacques-Henri Bernadin de Saint-Pierre's novel *Paul et Virginie*, the author, during a visit to Mauritius, hears from an old islander the tale of two families who had created for themselves there a little Utopia, before their idyllic existence was curtailed by tragedy. "'My son,' replied the old man, 'those heaps of rubbish, and that unfilled land, were, twenty years ago, the property of two families, who then found happiness in this solitude. Their history is affecting; but what European, pursuing his way to the Indies, will pause one moment to interest himself in the fate of a few obscure individuals?...' The old man, after a short silence, during which he leaned his face upon his hands, as if he were trying to recall the images of the past, thus began his narration:--- ..."

See the end of Section Three above.

This is the only reference in the volume to the King and the Beloved of Martyrs having been "Hájís:" perhaps the young vendor intends by the term that they had been on a visit to Bahá'u'lláh.*

The sum in question seems in fact to have been around eighteen thousand túmáns.*

This matter is mistaken, for the one that was indebted to those two brothers was the Imám-Jum'ih of Isfahán, Shaykh Báqir; however, we have here simply set forth the text of the English original. (Ishráq-Khávarí)*

Not just of Isfahán, but of the whole of Írán: "According to Mírzá Abu'l-Fadl (manuscript, p. 66), ... 'The office of Sadru's-Sudúr, or chief priest of Safaví times, was abolished by Nádir Sháh, and the Imam-Jum'ih of Isfahán is now the principal ecclesiastical dignitary of Persia.' (C. R. Markham's 'A General Sketch of the History of Persia,' p. 365.)"---*The Dawn-Breakers,* Footnote 3, p. 199

It appears that the original fatvá was issued by Shaykh Muhammad-Báqir and Mír Muhammad-Husayn, but then ratified by a larger assemblage of divines convened in the prince's presence on the seventh day. The number of divines in attendance on this latter occasion is set by Mírzá Abu'l-Fadl at around sixty.*

Among them, according to Áqá Husayn-'Alí Núr, as cited in the aforesaid *Khátirát-i-Muhájirí az Isfahán*, a telegram despatched by a servant of the Twin Shining Lights, one Mashhadí Haydar, a resident of their personal village of Áb-i-Níl (signifying "Water of the Nile"), who, on learning of their arrest, and finding the Bahá'ís debarred from sending telegrams, journeyed expeditiously to Káshán, from whence, with the help of the local friends, he telegraphed to the Sháh news of the proceeding (p. 18 ff).

Browne writes in the margin, "So far as is known, the Sháh's telegram reached Zillu's-Sultán *before* the death of the two brothers, but he kept the matter quiet; then after they had been put to death telegraphed to the Sháh saying that the royal decree had arrived too late -- after the deed had already been done -- and expressing profound regret at the circumstance." (Ishráq-Khávarí)

The place in question is understood from preceding accounts, as that of Mírzá Abu'l-Fadl, to have been a ruined wall or arch, situated in a waste place near the Níyásarm river. Áqá Husayn-'Alí Núr, however, is quoted in the aforesaid work as saying that the ruined place lay "behind the kitchen" (presumably of the Naqsh-i-Jihán Gardens palace complex), and that the reason why it was possible to extricate the bodies was because the collapsed wall had *fallen onto another wall*, so preserving the bodies that lay beneath (p. 37).*

According to previous accounts -- that of the author in Section Three above, and those of Mírzá Abu'l-Fadl and Fádil-i-Mázindarání in the present section -- this office was performed by Áqá Mírzá Ismá'íl, assisted by a number of his servants, and seemingly by Áqá Muhammad-Báqir-i-Mudarris too.*

The young vendor might be thought of as gesturing towards the river at this point.

"The body of Paul was placed by the side of his Virginia, at the foot of the same shrubs; and on that hallowed spot the remains of their tender mothers, and their faithful servants, are laid. No marble covers the turf, no inscription records their virtues; but their memory is engraven upon our hearts, in characters, which are indelible; and surely, if those pure spirits still take an interest in what passes upon earth, they love to wander beneath the roofs of these dwellings, which are inhabited by industrious virtue, to console the poor who complain of their destiny, to cherish in the hearts of lovers the sacred flame of fidelity, to inspire a taste for the blessing of nature, the love of labour, and the dread of riches."--- *Paul et Virginie*

"'The loved ones of God and His kindred,' is Bahá'u'lláh's testimony in the Súriy-i-Ra'ís, 'were left on the first night without food... The people surrounded the house \sc. of 'Izzat Áqá in Adrianople\, and Muslims and Christians wept over Us... We perceived that the weeping of the people of the Son (Christians) exceeded the weeping of others -- a sign for such as ponder.'"---*God Passes By*, pp. 179-80

"God hath, likewise, as a bounty from His presence, abolished the concept of 'uncleanness', whereby divers things and peoples have been held to be impure. He, of a certainty, is the Ever-Forgiving, the Most Generous. Verily, all created things were immersed in the sea of purification when, on that first day of Ridván, We shed upon the whole of creation the splendours of Our most excellent Names and Our most exalted Attributes. This, verily, is a token of My loving providence, which hath encompassed all the worlds. ..."---The Kitáb-i-Aqdas, Paragraph 75

In fact, Bahá'ís are enjoined to love all men equally, irrespective of race, class, nation, creed or sex.

"The passion of Jesus Christ, and indeed His whole public ministry, alone offer a parallel to the Mission and death of the Báb, a parallel which no student of comparative religion can fail to perceive or ignore."---*God Passes By*, p. 56

"O Báqir! Rely not on thy glory, and thy power. Thou art even as the last trace of sunlight upon the mountain-top. Soon will it fade away, as decreed by God, the All-Possessing, the Most High.
